Parsing [sent. 498 len. 55]: `` I can tell you , sir , why they refused the bait , '' said the boatswain of the vessel , who was standing by ; `` it 's because we are now on the track of the Brazilian slavers , and they have been well fed lately , depend upon it . ''
Parsing [sent. 499 len. 19]: `` I should not be surprised if you were correct in your idea , '' replied Mr. Swinton .
Parsing [sent. 500 len. 13]: `` There are many varieties of sharks , are there not ? ''
Parsing [sent. 501 len. 3]: inquired Wilmot .
Parsing [sent. 502 len. 78]: `` Yes , a great many ; the fiercest , however , and the largest kind is the one which has just left us , and is termed the white shark ; it ranges the whole Atlantic Ocean , but is seldom found far to the northward , as it prefers the tropics : it is , however , to be seen in the Mediterranean , in the Gulf of Lyons , and is there remarkably fierce .
Parsing [sent. 503 len. 35]: In the English Channel you find the blue shark , which is seldom dangerous ; there is also a very large-sized but harmless shark found in the north seas , which the whalers frequent .
Parsing [sent. 504 len. 67]: Then there is the spotted or tiger-shark , which is very savage , although it does not grow to a large size ; the hammer-headed shark , so called from the peculiar formation of its head ; and the ground shark , perhaps the most dangerous of all , as it lies at the bottom and rises under you without giving you notice of its approach .
Parsing [sent. 505 len. 11]: I believe I have now mentioned the principal varieties . ''
Parsing [sent. 506 len. 41]: `` If a man was to fall overboard and a shark was nigh , what would be the best plan to act upon -- that is , if there would be any chance of escape from such a brute ? ''
Parsing [sent. 507 len. 48]: `` The best plan , and I have seen it acted upon with success , is , if you can swim well , to throw yourself on your back and splash as much as you can with your feet , and halloo as loud as you can .
Parsing [sent. 508 len. 14]: A shark is a cowardly animal , and noise will drive it away .
Parsing [sent. 509 len. 30]: `` When I went out two or three years ago , I had a Newfoundland dog , which was accustomed to leap into the water from almost any height .
Parsing [sent. 510 len. 64]: I was very partial to him , and you may imagine my annoyance when , one day , as we were becalmed along the Western Islands , and a large shark came up alongside , the dog , at once perceiving it , plunged off the taffrail to seize it , swimming toward the shark , and barking as loud as he could .
Parsing [sent. 511 len. 43]: I fully expected that the monster would have dispatched him in a moment ; but to my surprise the shark was frightened and swam away , followed by the dog , until the boat that was lowered down picked him up . ''
Parsing [sent. 512 len. 14]: `` I do n't think the shark could have been very hungry . ''
Parsing [sent. 513 len. 20]: `` Probably not ; at all events I should not have liked to have been in Neptune 's place .
Parsing [sent. 514 len. 24]: I think the most peculiar plan of escaping from sharks is that pursued by the Cingalese divers , and often with success . ''
Parsing [sent. 515 len. 9]: `` Tell me , if you please . ''
Parsing [sent. 516 len. 86]: `` The divers who go down for the pearl oysters off Ceylon generally drop from a boat , and descend in ten or twelve fathoms of water before they come to the bed of pearl oysters , which is upon a bank of mud : it often happens that when they are down , the sharks make for them , and I hardly need say that these poor fellows are constantly on the watch , looking in every direction while they are filling their baskets .
Parsing [sent. 517 len. 65]: If they perceive a shark making for them , their only chance is to stir up the mud on the bank as fast as they can , which prevents the animal from distinguishing them , and under the cover of the clouded water they regain the surface ; nevertheless , it does not always answer , and many are taken off every year . ''
Parsing [sent. 518 len. 31]: `` A lady , proud of her pearl necklace , little thinks how many poor fellows may have been torn to pieces to obtain for her such an ornament . ''
Parsing [sent. 519 len. 55]: `` Very true ; and when we consider how many pearl-fisheries may have taken place , and how many divers may have been destroyed , before a string of fine pearls can be obtained , we might almost say that every pearl on the necklace has cost the life of a human creature . ''
Parsing [sent. 520 len. 15]: `` How are the pearls disposed of , and who are the proprietors ? ''
Parsing [sent. 521 len. 58]: `` The government are the proprietors of the fishery , I believe ; but whether they farm it out yearly , or not , I can not tell ; but this I know , that as the pearl oysters are taken , they are landed unopened and packed upon the beach in squares of a certain dimension .
Parsing [sent. 522 len. 80]: When the fishing is over for the season , these square lots of pearl oysters are put up to auction , and sold to the highest bidder , of course ` contents unknown ; ' so that it becomes a species of lottery ; the purchaser may not find a single pearl in his lot , or he may find two or three , which will realize twenty times the price which he has paid for his lot . ''
Parsing [sent. 523 len. 33]: `` It is , then , a lottery from beginning to end ; the poor divers ' lottery is shark or no shark ; the purchasers ' , pearls or no pearls .
Parsing [sent. 524 len. 23]: But Mr. Fairburn is coming up the ladder , and I am anxious to know what was the fate of Mokanna . ''
Parsing [sent. 525 len. 42]: Mr. Fairburn , who had come on deck on purpose to continue the narrative , took his seat by his two fellow passengers and went on as follows : -- `` I stated that Mokanna had been forwarded to the Cape .
Parsing [sent. 526 len. 79]: You must have perceived that his only crime was that of fighting for his native land against civilized invaders ; but this was a deep crime in the eyes of the colonial government ; he was immediately thrown into the common gaol , and finally was condemned to be imprisoned for life on Robben Island , a place appropriated for the detention of convicted felons and other malefactors , who there work in irons at the slate-quarries . ''
Parsing [sent. 527 len. 11]: `` May I ask , where is Robben Island ? ''
Parsing [sent. 528 len. 25]: `` It is an island a few miles from the mainland , close to Table Bay , upon which the Cape Town is built .
Parsing [sent. 529 len. 72]: `` Mokanna remained there about a year , when , having made his intentions known to some Caffres who were confined there with him , he contrived out of the iron hoops of the casks to make some weapons like cutlasses , with which he armed his followers , rose upon the guard and overpowered them ; he then seized the boat , and with his Caffres made for the mainland .
Parsing [sent. 530 len. 52]: Unfortunately , in attempting to disembark upon the rocks of the mainland , the boat was upset in the surf , which was very violent ; Mokanna clung some time to a rock , but at last was washed off , and thus perished the unfortunate leader of the Caffres . ''
Parsing [sent. 531 len. 27]: `` Poor fellow , '' said Alexander ; `` he deserved a better fate and a more generous enemy ; but did the war continue ? ''
Parsing [sent. 532 len. 19]: `` No ; it ended in a manner every way worthy of that in which it was begun .
Parsing [sent. 533 len. 23]: You recollect that the war was commenced to support Gaika , our selected chief of the Caffres , against the real chiefs .
Parsing [sent. 534 len. 53]: The Caffres had before been compelled to give up their territories on our side of the Fish River ; the colonial government now insisted upon their retiring still further , that is , beyond the Keisi and Chumi rivers , by which 3,000 more square miles were added to the colonial territory .
Parsing [sent. 535 len. 33]: This was exacted , in order that there might be a neutral ground to separate the Caffres and the Dutch boors , and put an end to further robberies on either side .
Parsing [sent. 536 len. 45]: The strangest part of the story is , that this territory was not taken away from the Caffre chiefs , against whom we had made war , but from Gaika , our ally , to support whom we had entered into the war . ''
Parsing [sent. 537 len. 18]: `` Well , it was even-handed -- not justice , but injustice , at all events . ''
Parsing [sent. 538 len. 69]: `` Exactly so ; and so thought Gaika , for when speaking of the protection he received from the colonial government , he said , ` But when I look upon the large extent of fine country which has been taken from me , I am compelled to say , that , although protected , I am _ rather oppressed _ by my _ protectors _ . ' ''
Parsing [sent. 539 len. 34]: `` Unjust as was the mode of obtaining the neutral ground , I must say that it appears to me to have been a good policy to put one between the parties . ''
Parsing [sent. 540 len. 15]: `` I grant it ; but what was the conduct of the colonial government ?
Parsing [sent. 541 len. 28]: This neutral ground was afterward given away in large tracts to the Dutch boors , so as again to bring them into contact with the Caffres . ''
Parsing [sent. 542 len. 6]: `` Is it possible ? ''
Parsing [sent. 543 len. 38]: `` Yes ; to men who had always been opposed to the English government , who had twice risen in rebellion against them , and who had tried to bring in the Caffres to destroy the colony .
Parsing [sent. 544 len. 38]: Neither were the commandoes , or excursions against the Caffres , put an end to : Makomo , the son of Gaika , our late ally , has , I hear , been the party now attacked .
Parsing [sent. 545 len. 48]: I trust , however , that we may soon have affairs going on in a more favorable and reputable manner ; indeed , I am sure that , now the government at home have been put in possession of the facts , such will be the case .
Parsing [sent. 546 len. 22]: `` I have now given you a very brief insight into the history of the Cape up to the present time .
Parsing [sent. 547 len. 44]: There are many points which I have passed over , not wishing to diverge from a straightforward narrative ; but upon any questions you may wish to ask , I shall be most happy to give you all the information in my power .
Parsing [sent. 548 len. 49]: I can not , however , dismiss the subject without making one remark , which is , that it is principally , if not wholly , to the missionaries , to their exertions and to their representations , that what good has been done is to be attributed .
Parsing [sent. 549 len. 46]: They are entitled to the greatest credit and the warmest praise ; and great as has been the misrule of this colony for many years , it would have been much greater and much more disgraceful , if it had not been for their efforts .
Parsing [sent. 550 len. 21]: Another very important alteration has been taking place in the colony , which will eventually be productive of much good .
Parsing [sent. 551 len. 42]: I refer to the British immigration , which every year becomes more extensive ; and as soon as the British population exceeds and masters that of the old Dutch planters and boors , we shall have better feeling in the colony .
Parsing [sent. 552 len. 22]: Do not suppose that all the Dutch boors are such as those whose conduct I have been obliged to point out .
Parsing [sent. 553 len. 13]: There are many worthy men , although but few educated or enlightened .
Parsing [sent. 554 len. 44]: `` I know from my own observation that the failings and prejudices against the natives are fast fading away , and that lately the law has been able to hold its ground , and has been supported by the people inhabiting the districts .
Parsing [sent. 555 len. 41]: The Dutch , with all their prejudices and all their vices , will soon be swallowed up by the inundation of English settlers , and will gradually be so incorporated and intermingled by marriage that no distinction will be known .
Parsing [sent. 556 len. 46]: Time , however , is required for such consolidation and cementation ; that time is arriving fast , and the future prospects of the Cape are as cheering , as you may think , from my narrative , they have been disheartening and gloomy . ''
Parsing [sent. 557 len. 16]: `` I trust in God that such will be the case , '' replied Alexander .
Parsing [sent. 558 len. 32]: `` If this wind continues , in a few days we shall be at the Cape , and I shall be most anxious to hear how affairs are going on . ''
Parsing [sent. 559 len. 53]: `` I had a letter just before I set out from England , stating that the Zoolu tribes , to the northward of the Caffres , are in an unquiet state ; and as you must pass near to these tribes on your journey , I am anxious to know the truth .
Parsing [sent. 560 len. 21]: At all events , Chaka is dead ; he was murdered about two years back by his own relations . ''
Parsing [sent. 561 len. 6]: `` Who was Chaka ? ''
Parsing [sent. 562 len. 3]: inquired Alexander .
Parsing [sent. 563 len. 29]: `` That I have yet to tell you ; at present we have only got as far as the Caffres , who are immediately on our frontiers . ''
Parsing [sent. 564 len. 3]: CHAPTER VII .
Parsing [sent. 565 len. 13]: The wind continued fair , and the vessel rapidly approached the Cape .
Parsing [sent. 566 len. 51]: Alexander , who had contracted a great friendship for Mr. Swinton , had made known to him the cause of his intended journey into the interior , and the latter volunteered , if his company would not be displeasing , to accompany Alexander on his tedious and somewhat perilous expedition .
Parsing [sent. 567 len. 43]: Alexander gladly accepted the offer , and requested Mr. Swinton would put himself to no expense , as he had unlimited command of money from his grand-uncle , and Mr. Swinton 's joining the caravan would make no difference in his arrangements .
Parsing [sent. 568 len. 70]: After it had been agreed that they should travel together , the continued subject of discourse and discussion was the nature of the outfit , the number of wagons , their equipment , the stores , the number of horses and oxen which should he provided ; and they were busy every day adding to their memoranda as to what it would be advisable to procure for their journey .
Parsing [sent. 569 len. 33]: Mr. Fairburn often joined in the discussion , and gave his advice , but told them that , when they arrived at Cape Town , he might be more useful to them .
Parsing [sent. 570 len. 73]: Alexander , who , as we have before observed , was a keen hunter , and very partial to horses and dogs , promised himself much pleasure in the chase of the wild animals on their journey , and congratulated himself upon being so well provided with guns and rifles , which he had brought with him , more with the idea that they might be required for self-defense than for sport .
Parsing [sent. 571 len. 9]: At last , `` Land , ho ! ''
Parsing [sent. 572 len. 33]: was cried out by the man who was at the mast-head in the morning watch , and soon afterward , the flat top of Table Mountain was distinctly visible from the deck .
Parsing [sent. 573 len. 29]: The _ Surprise _ , running before a fresh breeze , soon neared the land , so that the objects on it might be perceived with a glass .
Parsing [sent. 574 len. 37]: At noon they were well in for the bay , and before three o'clock the _ Surprise _ was brought to an anchor between two other merchant vessels , which were filling up their home cargoes .
Parsing [sent. 575 len. 59]: After a three months ' voyage , passengers are rather anxious to get on shore ; and therefore before night all were landed , and Alexander found himself comfortably domiciled in one of the best houses in Cape Town ; for Mr. Fairburn had , during the passage , requested Alexander to take up his abode with him .
Parsing [sent. 576 len. 40]: Tired with the excitement of the day , he was not sorry to go to bed early , and he did not forget to return his thanks to Him who had preserved him through the perils of the voyage .
Parsing [sent. 577 len. 29]: The next morning Mr. Fairburn said to Alexander -- `` Mr. Wilmot , I should recommend you for the first ten days to think nothing about your journey .
Parsing [sent. 578 len. 35]: Amuse yourself with seeing the public gardens , and other things worthy of inspection ; or , if it pleases you , you can make the ascent of Table Mountain with your friend Swinton .
Parsing [sent. 579 len. 24]: At all events , do just as you please ; you will find my people attentive , and ready to obey your orders .
Parsing [sent. 580 len. 21]: You know the hours of meals ; consider yourself at home , and as much master here as I am .
Parsing [sent. 581 len. 57]: As you may well imagine , after so long an absence , I have much to attend to in my official capacity , and I think it will be a week or ten days before I shall be comfortably reseated in my office , and have things going on smoothly , as they ought to do .
Parsing [sent. 582 len. 23]: You must therefore excuse me if I am not quite so attentive a host at first as I should wish to be .
Parsing [sent. 583 len. 31]: One thing only I recommend you to do at present , which is , to accompany me this afternoon to Government-house , that I may introduce you to the governor .
Parsing [sent. 584 len. 28]: It is just as well to get over that mark of respect which is due to him , and then you will be your own master . ''
Parsing [sent. 585 len. 6]: Alexander replied with many thanks .
Parsing [sent. 586 len. 23]: He was graciously received by the governor , who promised him every assistance in his power in the prosecution of his journey .
Parsing [sent. 587 len. 23]: Having received an invitation for dinner on the following day , Alexander bowed and took his leave in company with Mr. Fairburn .
Parsing [sent. 588 len. 11]: On the following day Alexander was visited by Mr. Swinton .
Parsing [sent. 589 len. 19]: Mr. Swinton was accompanied by a major in the Bengal Cavalry , whom he introduced as Major Henderson .
Parsing [sent. 590 len. 33]: He had arrived a few days before from Calcutta , having obtained leave of absence for the recovery of his health , after a smart jungle-fever , which had nearly proved fatal .
Parsing [sent. 591 len. 19]: The voyage , however , had completely reinstated him , and he appeared full of life and spirits .
Parsing [sent. 592 len. 30]: They walked together to the Company 's gardens , in which were a few lions , and some other Cape animals , and the discourse naturally turned upon them .
Parsing [sent. 593 len. 39]: Major Henderson described the hunting in India , especially the tiger-hunting on elephants , to which he was very partial ; and Alexander soon discovered that he was talking to one who was passionately fond of the sport .
Parsing [sent. 594 len. 13]: After a long conversation they parted , mutually pleased with each other .
Parsing [sent. 595 len. 43]: A day or two afterward , Mr. Swinton , who had been talking about their intended journey with Alexander , said to him : -- `` You must not be surprised at the off-hand and unceremonious way we have in the colonies .
Parsing [sent. 596 len. 13]: People meeting abroad , even Englishmen occasionally , throw aside much ceremony .
Parsing [sent. 597 len. 22]: I mention this , because Major Henderson intends to call this afternoon , and propose joining our party into the interior .
Parsing [sent. 598 len. 35]: I do not know much of him , but I have heard much said in his favor , and it is easy to see by his manners and address that he is a gentleman .
Parsing [sent. 599 len. 23]: Of course , when he stated his intention , I could do nothing but refer him to you , which I did .
Parsing [sent. 600 len. 8]: What do you think , Wilmot ? ''
Parsing [sent. 601 len. 57]: `` I think very well of Major Henderson , and I consider that , as the journey must be one of some peril , the more Europeans the better , especially when we can find one who is used to danger from his profession , and also to dangerous hunting , which we must also expect .
Parsing [sent. 602 len. 26]: So far from not wishing him to join us , I consider him a most valuable acquisition , and am delighted at the idea . ''
Parsing [sent. 603 len. 18]: `` Well , I am glad to hear you say so , for I agree with you .
Parsing [sent. 604 len. 18]: He is hunting mad , that is certain , and I hear , a most remarkable shot .
Parsing [sent. 605 len. 10]: I think with you he will be an acquisition .
Parsing [sent. 606 len. 39]: It appears that it was his intention to have gone into the interior , even if he went by himself ; and he has two Arab horses which he brought with him from India with that view . ''
Parsing [sent. 607 len. 53]: `` If you see him before he comes , you may say that you have stated his wishes to me , and that I am quite delighted at his joining our party , -- it being perfectly understood that he is at no expense for any thing connected with the outfit . ''
Parsing [sent. 608 len. 27]: `` I will tell him so , '' replied Swinton ; `` and I think the sooner we begin to collect what is necessary the better .
Parsing [sent. 609 len. 9]: We must have Major Henderson in our councils .
Parsing [sent. 610 len. 22]: Depend upon it , he will be very useful and very active ; so , for the present , farewell . ''
Parsing [sent. 611 len. 33]: Mr. Swinton and Major Henderson called together that afternoon , and the latter , as soon as he was admitted into the party , began to talk over the plans and preparations .
Parsing [sent. 612 len. 30]: `` My suite is not very large , '' said he ; `` I have two horses and two dogs , a Parsee servant , and a Cape baboon .
Parsing [sent. 613 len. 15]: I should like to take the latter with us as well as my servant .
Parsing [sent. 614 len. 46]: My servant , because he is a good cook ; and my monkey , because , if we are hard put to it , she will show us what we may eat and what we may not ; there is no taster like a monkey .
Parsing [sent. 615 len. 19]: Besides , she is young and full of tricks , and I like something to amuse me . ''
Parsing [sent. 616 len. 22]: `` The baboons have another good quality : they give notice of danger sooner than a dog , '' observed Swinton .
Parsing [sent. 617 len. 16]: `` I think , Wilmot , we must admit the monkey into the party . ''
Parsing [sent. 618 len. 32]: `` I shall be most happy , '' replied Alexander , laughing ; `` pray give her my compliments , Major Henderson , and say how happy I shall be . ''
Parsing [sent. 619 len. 39]: `` I call her Begum , '' said Major Henderson ; `` because she is so like the old Begum princess whom I was once attending , when in India with my troop , as guard of honor .
Parsing [sent. 620 len. 40]: You must look out for some good horses , Mr. Wilmot ; you will want a great many , and if you do not wish them to have sore backs , do n't let the Hottentots ride them . ''
Parsing [sent. 621 len. 42]: `` We have been discussing the point , Major Henderson , as to whether it will not be better to go round in a vessel to Algoa Bay , complete our equipment there , and make that our starting place . ''
Parsing [sent. 622 len. 40]: `` If you do , you will save a long journey by land , and find yourself not very far from what I understand are the best of hunting-grounds , near to the country of the Vaal River . ''
Parsing [sent. 623 len. 30]: The topics then dwelt upon were what articles they should procure in Cape Town , and what they should defer providing themselves with until their arrival at Algoa Bay .
Parsing [sent. 624 len. 43]: They agreed to provide all their stores at Cape Town , and as many good horses as they could select ; but the wagons and oxen , and the hiring of Hottentots , they put off until they arrived at Algoa Bay .
Parsing [sent. 625 len. 16]: Mr. Fairburn was now more at leisure , and Alexander had more of his society .
Parsing [sent. 626 len. 25]: One evening after dinner Mr. Fairburn had opened a map of the country , to give Alexander some information relative to his projected journey .
Parsing [sent. 627 len. 50]: He pointed out to him the track which appeared most advisable through the Caffre country , and then observed that it was difficult to give any advice as to his proceedings after he had passed this country , governed by Hinza , as every thing would depend upon circumstances .
Parsing [sent. 628 len. 12]: `` Do you know any thing of the country beyond ? ''
Parsing [sent. 629 len. 50]: `` Not much ; we know that it was overrun by the Zoolus , the tribe of which Chaka was the chief ; and last year our troops went to the assistance of the Caffres , who were attacked by another tribe from the northward , called the Mantatees .
Parsing [sent. 630 len. 10]: These were dispersed by our troops with immense slaughter .
Parsing [sent. 631 len. 27]: The Zoolu country , you perceive , is on the east side of the great chain of mountains , and to the northward of Port Natal .
Parsing [sent. 632 len. 19]: The Mantatees came from the west side of the mountains , in about the same parallel of latitude .
Parsing [sent. 633 len. 33]: It is impossible to say what may be going on at present , or what may take place before you arrive at your destination , as these northern irruptions are continual . ''
Parsing [sent. 634 len. 13]: `` You promised me the history of that person , Chaka . ''
Parsing [sent. 635 len. 24]: `` You shall have it now : he was the king of the Zoolu nation -- I hardly know what to call him .
Parsing [sent. 636 len. 24]: He was the Nero and the Napoleon of Africa ; a monster in cruelty and crime , yet a great warrior and conqueror .
Parsing [sent. 637 len. 13]: He commenced his career by murdering his relatives to obtain the sovereignty .
Parsing [sent. 638 len. 28]: As soon as he had succeeded , he murdered all those whom he thought inimical to him , and who had been friends to his relatives . ''
Parsing [sent. 639 len. 8]: `` But are the Zoolus Caffres ? ''
Parsing [sent. 640 len. 18]: `` No ; but there are many races to the northward which we consider as Caffre races .
Parsing [sent. 641 len. 37]: You may have observed , in the history of the world , that the migrations of the human race are generally from the north to the south : so it appears to have been in Africa .
Parsing [sent. 642 len. 42]: Some convulsion among the northern tribes , probably a pressure from excessive population , had driven the Zoolus to the southward , and they came down like an inundation , sweeping before them all the tribes that fell in their path .
Parsing [sent. 643 len. 21]: Chaka 's force consisted of nearly 100,000 warriors , of whom 15,000 were always in attendance to execute his orders .
Parsing [sent. 644 len. 20]: In every country which he overran he spared neither age nor sex ; it was one indiscriminate slaughter . ''
Parsing [sent. 645 len. 6]: `` What a monster ! ''
Parsing [sent. 646 len. 19]: `` He ruled by terror , and it is incredible that his orders met with such implicit obedience .
Parsing [sent. 647 len. 45]: To make his army invincible , he remodeled it , divided it into companies , distinguished by the color of their shields , and forbade them to use any other weapon but a short stabbing-spear , so that they always fought at close quarters .
Parsing [sent. 648 len. 25]: He weeded his army by picking out 1000 of his veteran warriors , who had gained his victories , and putting them to death .
Parsing [sent. 649 len. 42]: Any regiment sent out to battle , if they were defeated , were instantly destroyed on their return ; it was , therefore , victory or death with them ; and the death was most cruel , being that of impalement .
Parsing [sent. 650 len. 16]: Well he was surnamed ` the Bloody , ' '' `` Yes , indeed . ''
Parsing [sent. 651 len. 10]: `` His tyranny over his own people was dreadful .
Parsing [sent. 652 len. 44]: On one occasion , a child annoyed him ; he ordered it to be killed ; but the child ran among seventy or eighty other children , and could not be distinguished , so he ordered the whole to be put to death .
Parsing [sent. 653 len. 13]: He murdered two or three hundred of his wives in one day .
Parsing [sent. 654 len. 23]: At the slightest suspicion he would order out his chiefs to execution , and no one knew when his turn might come .
Parsing [sent. 655 len. 11]: His will was law : every one trembled and obeyed .
Parsing [sent. 656 len. 26]: To enter into a detail of all his cruelties would fill volumes ; it will be sufficient to mention the last act of his life .
Parsing [sent. 657 len. 14]: His mother died , and he declared that she had perished by witchcraft .
Parsing [sent. 658 len. 38]: Hundreds and hundreds were impaled , and , at last , tired of these slow proceedings , he ordered out his army to an indiscriminate slaughter over the whole country , which lasted for fourteen days . ''
Parsing [sent. 659 len. 5]: `` How horrible ! ''
Parsing [sent. 660 len. 18]: `` He was a demon who reveled in blood ; but his own turn came at last .
Parsing [sent. 661 len. 25]: He was murdered by his brother Dingaam , who knew that he was about to be sacrificed ; and thus perished the bloody Chaka .
Parsing [sent. 662 len. 17]: His brother Dingaam is now on the Zoolu throne , and appears inclined to be quiet .
Parsing [sent. 663 len. 41]: There is another great warrior chief named Moselekatsee , who revolted from Chaka , and who is much such another character ; but our accounts of these people are vague at present , and require time to corroborate their correctness .
Parsing [sent. 664 len. 19]: You will have to act and decide when you arrive there , and must be guided by circumstances .
Parsing [sent. 665 len. 27]: With the caravan you propose to travel with , I think there will not be much danger ; and if there is , you must retreat .
Parsing [sent. 666 len. 24]: The favor of these despots is easily to be obtained by judicious presents , which of course you will not be unprovided with .
Parsing [sent. 667 len. 24]: I have ordered your letters to the authorities to be made out , and you will have the governor 's signature to them .
Parsing [sent. 668 len. 9]: When do you propose to , start ? ''
Parsing [sent. 669 len. 23]: `` We shall be ready in a few days , and have only to find a vessel going to Algoa Bay . ''
Parsing [sent. 670 len. 28]: `` You will be asked to take charge of several articles which are to be sent to the missionary station which you will pass on your way .
Parsing [sent. 671 len. 8]: I presume you have no objection ? ''
Parsing [sent. 672 len. 25]: `` Certainly not ; they deserve every encouragement , and any kindness and attention I can show them will give me great pleasure . ''
Parsing [sent. 673 len. 21]: Alexander received many proposals from different parties who wished to join the expedition , but they were all civilly declined .
Parsing [sent. 674 len. 21]: In a few days a vessel arrived , which was about to go round to the settlement at Algoa Bay .
Parsing [sent. 675 len. 66]: Their stores , horses , and dogs , not forgetting Begum the baboon , were all embarked , and , taking leave of Mr. Fairburn and the governor , Alexander , Major Henderson , and Mr. Swinton embarked , and on the evening of the fourth day found themselves safe at anchor in company with ten or twelve vessels which were lying in Algoa Bay .
Parsing [sent. 676 len. 3]: CHAPTER VIII .
Parsing [sent. 677 len. 40]: The vessels which lay at anchor in Algoa Bay had just arrived from England , with a numerous collection of emigrants , who , to improve their fortunes , had left their native land to settle in this country .
Parsing [sent. 678 len. 16]: Many had landed , but the greater proportion were still on board of the vessels .
Parsing [sent. 679 len. 26]: The debarkation was rapidly going on , and the whole bay was covered with boats landing with people and stores , or returning for more .
Parsing [sent. 680 len. 42]: The wind blowing from the westward , there was no surf on the beach ; the sun was bright and warm , and the scene was busy and interesting ; but night came on , and the panorama was closed in .
Parsing [sent. 681 len. 29]: Alexander and his companions remained on the deck of their vessel till an undisturbed silence reigned where but an hour or two before all was noise and bustle .
Parsing [sent. 682 len. 48]: The stars , so beautiful in the southern climes , shone out in cloudless brilliancy ; the waters of the bay were smooth as glass , and reflected them so clearly that they might have fancied that there was a heaven beneath as well as above them .
Parsing [sent. 683 len. 29]: The land presented a dark opaque mass , the mountains in the distance appearing as if they were close to them , and rising precipitately from the shore .
Parsing [sent. 684 len. 44]: All was of one somber hue , except where the lights in the houses in the town twinkled here and there , announcing that ; some had not yet dismissed their worldly cares , and sought repose from the labors of the day .
Parsing [sent. 685 len. 31]: Yet all was silent , except occasionally the barking of a dog , or the voice of the sentry in Fort Frederick , announcing that `` all was well . ''
Parsing [sent. 686 len. 53]: `` What a gathering in a small space of so many people with so many different histories , so many causes for leaving their native land , and with so many different fortunes in store for them , must there be on board of an emigrant ship , '' observed Mr. Swinton .
Parsing [sent. 687 len. 33]: `` Yet all united in one feeling , and instigated by the same desire , -- that of independence , and , if possible , of wealth , '' rejoined Major Henderson .
Parsing [sent. 688 len. 101]: `` Of that there can be no doubt , '' said Alexander ; `` but it must be almost like beginning a new life ; so many ties broken by the vast ocean which has separated them ; new interests usurping the place of old ones ; all novelty and adventure to look forward to ; new scenes added to new hopes and new fears ; but we must not remain too long even to watch these beautiful heavens , for we must rise at daylight , so I shall set the example , and wish you both good-night . ''
Parsing [sent. 689 len. 20]: At daylight on the following morning the long-boat was hoisted out , and the horses safely conveyed on shore .
Parsing [sent. 690 len. 52]: After a hasty breakfast , Alexander and his two companions landed , to see if it were possible to obtain any roof under which they could shelter themselves ; but the number of emigrants who had arrived put that out of the question , every house and every bed being engaged .
Parsing [sent. 691 len. 27]: This was a great disappointment , as they had no wish to return on board and reoccupy the confined space which had been allotted to them .
Parsing [sent. 692 len. 20]: Having found accommodation for their horses , they proceeded to examine the town and resume their search for lodgings .
Parsing [sent. 693 len. 109]: The streets presented a bustling and animated scene ; wagons with goods , or returning empty with their long teams of oxen ; horses , sheep , and other animals , just landed ; loud talking ; busy inquirers ; running to and fro of men ; Hottentots busy with the gods , or smoking their pipes in idle survey ; crates and boxes , and packages of all descriptions , mixed up with agricultural implements and ironware , lining each side of the road , upon which were seated wives and daughters watching the property , and children looking round with astonishment , or playing or crying .
Parsing [sent. 694 len. 55]: Further out of the town were to be seen tents pitched by the emigrants , who had provided themselves with such necessaries before they had quitted England , and who were bivouacking like so many gipsies , independent of lodgings and their attendant expenses , and cooking their own provisions in kettles or frying-pans .
Parsing [sent. 695 len. 27]: As Alexander perceived the latter , he said , `` At all events , we have found lodgings now ; I never thought of that . ''
Parsing [sent. 696 len. 7]: `` How do you mean ? ''
Parsing [sent. 697 len. 30]: `` I have two tents in the luggage I brought from Cape Town ; we must get them on shore , and do as these people have done . ''
Parsing [sent. 698 len. 3]: `` Bravo !
Parsing [sent. 699 len. 27]: I am glad to hear that , '' replied Major Henderson ; `` any thing better than remaining on board to be nibbled by the cockroaches .
Parsing [sent. 700 len. 7]: Shall we return at once ? ''
Parsing [sent. 701 len. 25]: `` By all means , '' said Mr. Swinton ; `` we have but to get our mattresses and a few other articles . ''
Parsing [sent. 702 len. 21]: `` Leave my man to do all that , '' said the Major ; `` he is used to it .
Parsing [sent. 703 len. 12]: In India we almost live in tents when up the country .
Parsing [sent. 704 len. 16]: But here comes one that I should know ; -- Maxwell , I believe ? ''
Parsing [sent. 705 len. 27]: `` Even so , my dear Henderson , '' replied the military officer who had been thus addressed ; `` why , what brought you here ?
Parsing [sent. 706 len. 9]: -- surely you are not a settler ? ''
Parsing [sent. 707 len. 44]: `` No ; I am here because I am not a settler , '' replied Henderson , laughing ; `` I am always on the move ; I am merely on my own way with my two friends here to shoot a hippopotamus .
Parsing [sent. 708 len. 10]: Allow me to introduce Mr. Wilmot and Mr. Swinton .
Parsing [sent. 709 len. 15]: But I see you are on duty ; are you in the fort ? ''

Parsing [sent. 711 len. 10]: Can I be of any use to you ? ''
Parsing [sent. 712 len. 32]: `` That depends upon circumstances ; we are now going on board for our tents , to pitch them on the hill there , as we can get no lodgings . ''
Parsing [sent. 713 len. 64]: `` Well , I can not offer you beds in the fort , but I think if you were to pitch your tents outside the fort , on the glacis , you would be better than on the hill ; your baggage would be safer , and I should be more able to render you any attention or assistance you may require . ''
Parsing [sent. 714 len. 28]: `` An excellent idea ; if it were only on account of the baggage , '' replied Henderson ; `` we accept your offer with pleasure . ''
Parsing [sent. 715 len. 50]: `` Well then , get them on shore as quick as you can ; my men will soon have them out for you and assist in transporting your luggage ; and do n't distress yourself about your dinner , I will contrive to have something cooked for you . ''
Parsing [sent. 716 len. 14]: `` A friend in need is a friend indeed , my good fellow .
Parsing [sent. 717 len. 21]: We will accept your offers as freely as they are made : so farewell for an hour or so . ''
Parsing [sent. 718 len. 25]: As they parted with Captain Maxwell , Henderson observed , `` That was a lucky meeting , for we shall now get on well .
Parsing [sent. 719 len. 39]: Maxwell is an excellent fellow , and he will be very useful to us in making our purchases , as he knows the people and the country : and our luggage will be safe from all pilferers . ''
Parsing [sent. 720 len. 12]: `` It is indeed very fortunate , '' replied Mr. Swinton .
Parsing [sent. 721 len. 9]: `` Where did you know Captain Maxwell ? ''
Parsing [sent. 722 len. 4]: `` In India .
Parsing [sent. 723 len. 9]: We have often been out hunting tigers together .
Parsing [sent. 724 len. 18]: How he would like to be of our party ; but that is of course impossible . ''
Parsing [sent. 725 len. 14]: `` But how shall we manage about our living , Major Henderson ? ''

Parsing [sent. 729 len. 20]: `` Upon such terms , I shall gladly take up my quarters in the fort , '' replied Alexander .
Parsing [sent. 730 len. 12]: `` But which is our boat out of all these ? ''
Parsing [sent. 731 len. 55]: `` Here , sir , '' cried out one of the sailors ; `` come along , my lads , '' continued he to the other men , who were lounging about , and who all jumped into the boat , which pushed off , and they were soon on board of the ship .
Parsing [sent. 732 len. 58]: As the master of the vessel was equally glad to get rid of his passengers and their luggage as they were to leave , the utmost expedition was used by all parties , and in a few hours everything was landed , Begum , the baboon , being perched upon the stores conveyed in the last boat .
Parsing [sent. 733 len. 75]: A party of soldiers sent down by Captain Maxwell assisted the seamen to carry the various packages up to the fort , and before the evening closed in , the tents were pitched , their beds made up , and their baggage safely housed , while they were amusing themselves after dining with Captain Maxwell , leaning on the parapet and watching the passing and repassing of the boats which were unlading the vessels .
Parsing [sent. 734 len. 37]: As there was little chance of rain in the present season , they lay down on their mattresses in perfect security and comfort , and did not wake up the next morning until breakfast was ready .
Parsing [sent. 735 len. 97]: After breakfast they sallied out with Captain Maxwell to look after wagons and oxen , and as , on the arrival of the emigrants , a number of wagons had been sent down to take them to their destinations , Captain Maxwell soon fell in with some of the Dutch boors of the interior with whom he had been acquainted , and who had come down with their wagons ; but previous to making any bargains , Alexander went with Captain Maxwell to the landroost , for whom he had brought a letter from the governor .
Parsing [sent. 736 len. 64]: This gentleman immediately joined the party , and through his intervention , before night , four excellent wagons with their tilts and canvas coverings , and four span of oxen of fourteen each , were bought and promised to be brought down and delivered up in good order , as soon as they had carried up the freights with which they were charged .
Parsing [sent. 737 len. 60]: As these wagons could not return under four days , the next object that they had in view was to procure some more horses , and here they met with difficulty ; for Major Henderson , who , as an excellent judge of horses , was requested to select them , would not accept of many that were offered .
Parsing [sent. 738 len. 56]: Still they had plenty of time , as the wagons would require fitting out previous to their departure , and this would be a work of some days ; and many articles which they had decided to procure at Algoa Bay , instead of the Cape , were now to be sought for and selected .
Parsing [sent. 739 len. 16]: At the time appointed , the wagons and teams were delivered over and paid for .
Parsing [sent. 740 len. 43]: Carpenters were then engaged , and the wagons were fitted out with lockers all round them , divided off to contain the luggage separate , so that they might be able to obtain in a minute any thing that they might require .
Parsing [sent. 741 len. 48]: While this work was proceeding , with the assistance of the landroost , they were engaging Hottentots and other people to join the expedition , some as drivers to the wagons , others as huntsmen , and to perform such duties as might be required of them .
Parsing [sent. 742 len. 61]: Some very steady brave men were selected , but it was impossible to make up the whole force which they wished to take of people of known character ; many of them were engaged rather from their appearance , their promises , and the characters they obtained from others or gave themselves , than from any positive knowledge of them .
Parsing [sent. 743 len. 32]: This could not be avoided ; and as they had it in their power to dismiss them for bad conduct , it was to be presumed that they could procure others .
Parsing [sent. 744 len. 147]: It was more than three weeks before every thing was ready for their departure , and then the caravan was composed as follows : -- The persons who belonged to it were our three gentlemen ; the servant of Major Henderson ; eight drivers of the teams of oxen ; twelve Hottentot and other hunters -LRB- for some of them were of a mixed race -RRB- ; two Hottentots who had charge of the horses , and two others who had charge of a flock of Cape sheep , which were to follow the caravan , and serve as food until they could procure oxen by purchase or game with their guns : so that the whole force of the party amounted to twenty men : two Hottentot women , wives of the principal men , also accompanied the caravan to wash and assist in cooking .
Parsing [sent. 745 len. 66]: The animals belonging to the caravan consisted of fifty-six fine oxen , which composed the teams ; twelve horses , as Major Henderson could only procure six at Algoa Bay , or they would have purchased more ; thirteen dogs of various sizes , and Begum , the baboon , belonging to Captain Henderson : to these were to be added the flock of sheep .
Parsing [sent. 746 len. 19]: The wagons were fitted out as follows , chiefly under the direction of Major Henderson and Mr. Swinton .
Parsing [sent. 747 len. 90]: The first wagon , which was called Mr. Wilmot 's wagon , was fitted up with boxes or lockers all round , and contained all the stores for their own use , such as tea , sugar , coffee , cheeses , hams , tongues , biscuits , soap , and wax candles , wine and spirits in bottles , besides large rolls of tobacco for the Hottentots or presents , and Alexander 's clothes ; his mattress lay at the bottom of the wagons , between the lockers .
Parsing [sent. 748 len. 32]: The wagon was covered with a double sail-cloth tilt , and with curtains before and behind ; the carpenter 's tools were also in one of the lockers of this wagon .
Parsing [sent. 749 len. 87]: The second wagon was called Mr. Swinton 's wagon ; it was fitted up with lockers in the same way as the other , but it had also a large chest with a great quantity of drawers for insects , bottles of spirits for animals , and every thing necessary for preserving them ; a ream or two of paper for drying plants , and several other articles , more particularly a medicine-chest well filled , for Mr. Swinton was not unacquainted with surgery and physic .
Parsing [sent. 750 len. 34]: The other lockers were filled with a large quantity of glass beads and cutlery for presents , several hundred pounds of bullets , ready cast , and all the kitchen ware and crockery .
Parsing [sent. 751 len. 25]: It had the same covering as the first , and Mr. Swinton 's mattress was at night spread in the middle between the lockers .
Parsing [sent. 752 len. 24]: The third wagon was called the armory , or the Major 's wagon ; it was not fitted up like the two first .
Parsing [sent. 753 len. 32]: The whole bottom of it was occupied with movable chests , and four large casks of spirits , and the Major made up his bed on the top of the chests .
Parsing [sent. 754 len. 25]: In the chests were gunpowder in bottles and a quantity of small shot for present use ; tobacco in large rolls ; 1 cwt .
Parsing [sent. 755 len. 23]: of snuff ; all the heavy tools , spades , shovels , and axes , and a variety of other useful articles .
Parsing [sent. 756 len. 26]: The tilt-frame was much stouter than that of the two other wagons , for the hoops met each other so as to make it solid .
Parsing [sent. 757 len. 42]: It was covered with a tarred sail-cloth so as to be quite water-proof , and under the tilt-frame were suspended all the guns , except the two which Alexander and Mr. Swinton retained in their own wagons in case of emergency .
Parsing [sent. 758 len. 64]: The back and front of this wagon were closed with boards , which were let down and pulled up on hinges , so that it was a little fortress in case of need ; and as it could be locked up at any time , the Hottentots were not able to get at the casks of spirits without committing a sort of burglary .
Parsing [sent. 759 len. 10]: Begum was tied up in this wagon at night .
Parsing [sent. 760 len. 81]: The fourth wagon was called the store wagon , and contained several articles which were not immediately wanted ; such as casks of flour and bags of rice : it also held most of the ammunition , having six casks of gunpowder , a quantity of lead , two coils of rope , iron bars , bags of nails of various sizes , rolls of brass wire , and the two tents , with three chairs and a small table .
Parsing [sent. 761 len. 14]: Like the wagon of Major Henderson , it was covered with water-proof cloth .
Parsing [sent. 762 len. 49]: Such was the fit-out which was considered necessary for this adventurous expedition , and the crowds who came to see the preparations for the great hunting-party , as it was called , were so great and so annoying that the utmost haste was made to quit the town .
Parsing [sent. 763 len. 41]: At last the wagons were all loaded , the Hottentots collected together from the liquor-shops , their agreements read to them by the landroost , and any departure from their agreements , or any misconduct , threatened with severe punishment .
Parsing [sent. 764 len. 18]: The horses and oxen were brought in , and the next morning was fixed for their departure .
Parsing [sent. 765 len. 64]: Having taken leave of the landroost and other gentlemen of the town , who had loaded them with civilities , they retired to the fort , and passed the major part of the night with Captain Maxwell ; but to avoid the crowd which would have accompanied them , and have impeded their progress , they had resolved to set off before daylight .
Parsing [sent. 766 len. 48]: At two o'clock in the morning the Hottentots were roused up , the oxen yoked , and an hour before day-break the whole train had quitted the town , and were traveling at a slow pace , lighted only by the brilliant stars of the southern sky .
Parsing [sent. 767 len. 3]: CHAPTER IX .
Parsing [sent. 768 len. 10]: The plans of our travelers had been well digested .
Parsing [sent. 769 len. 74]: They had decided that they would first prosecute the object of their journey by proceeding straight through the Caffre country to the borders of the Undata River , near or whereabout it was reported that the descendants of the whites would be found located ; and as soon as Alexander had accomplished his mission , that they would cross the chain of mountains , and return through the Bushmen and the Koranna country .
Parsing [sent. 770 len. 52]: Their reason for making this arrangement was , that throughout the whole of the Caffre country , with the exception of lions and elephants in the forest , and hippopotami in the rivers , there was little or no game to be found , the Caffres having almost wholly destroyed it .
Parsing [sent. 771 len. 48]: This plan had been suggested by Major Henderson , and had been approved by Alexander and Mr. Swinton , -- Alexander being equally desirous as the Major to have plenty of field-sport , and Mr. Swinton anxious to increase his stock and knowledge of the animal kingdom .
Parsing [sent. 772 len. 63]: There was little to be feared in their advance through the Caffre country , as the missionaries had already planted two missions , one at Butterworth and the other at Chumie ; and the first of these Alexander had decided upon visiting , and had , in consequence , several packages in his wagon , which had been entrusted to his care .
Parsing [sent. 773 len. 21]: It was on the 7th of May , 1829 , that the caravan quitted Algoa Bay for Graham 's Town .
Parsing [sent. 774 len. 46]: The weather had been for some weeks fine , the heavy rains having ceased , and the pasturage was now luxuriant ; the wagons proceeded at a noiseless pace over the herbage , the sleepy Hottentots not being at all inclined to exert themselves unnecessarily .
Parsing [sent. 775 len. 18]: Alexander , Swinton , and Henderson were on horseback , a little ahead of the first wagon .
Parsing [sent. 776 len. 30]: `` I do n't know how you feel , '' said the Major ; `` but I feel as if I were a prisoner just released from his chains .
Parsing [sent. 777 len. 10]: I breathe the air of independence and liberty now .
Parsing [sent. 778 len. 26]: After the bustle , and noise , and crowding together of the town , to find ourselves here so quiet and solitary is freedom . ''
Parsing [sent. 779 len. 65]: `` I had the same feeling , '' replied Alexander ; `` this wide-extended plain , of which we can not yet discern the horizontal edge ; these brilliant stars scattered over the heavens , and shining down upon us ; no sound to meet our ears but the creaking of the wagon-wheels in the slow and measured pace , is to me delightful .
Parsing [sent. 780 len. 24]: They say man is formed for society , and so he is ; but it is very delightful occasionally to be alone . ''
Parsing [sent. 781 len. 33]: `` Yes ; alone as we are , '' replied Swinton , laughing ; `` that is , with a party of thirty people , well armed , in search of adventure .
Parsing [sent. 782 len. 44]: To be clear of the bustle of the town , and no longer cooped up in the fort , is pleasant enough ; but , I suspect , to be quite alone in these African wilds would be any thing but agreeable . ''
Parsing [sent. 783 len. 5]: `` Perhaps so . ''
Parsing [sent. 784 len. 32]: `` Neither would you feel so much at ease if you knew that your chance of to-morrow 's dinner was to depend wholly upon what you might procure with your gun .
Parsing [sent. 785 len. 16]: There is a satisfaction in knowing that you have four well-filled wagons behind you . ''
Parsing [sent. 786 len. 28]: `` I grant that also , '' replied the Major ; `` but still there is solitude even with this company , and I feel it . ''
Parsing [sent. 787 len. 49]: `` A solitary caravan -- but grant that there is some difference between that and a solitary individual , '' rejoined Swinton ; `` however , we have not come to solitude yet , for we shall find Dutch boors enough between this and Graham 's Town . ''
Parsing [sent. 788 len. 87]: `` I think , Wilmot , '' observed Henderson , `` that I should , if I were you , proceed by slow stages at first , that we may get our men into some kind of order and discipline , and also that we may find out whether there are any who will not suit us ; we can discharge them at Graham 's Town , and procure others in their place , at the same time that we engage our interpreters and guides . ''
Parsing [sent. 789 len. 36]: `` I think your plan very good , '' replied Alexander ; `` besides , we shall not have our wagons properly laden and arranged until we have been out three or four days . ''
Parsing [sent. 790 len. 50]: `` One thing is absolutely necessary , which is , to have a guard kept every night , '' said Swinton ; `` and there ought to be two men on guard at a time ; for one of them is certain to fall asleep , if not both .
Parsing [sent. 791 len. 7]: I know the Hottentots well . ''
Parsing [sent. 792 len. 27]: `` They will be excellent guards , by your account , '' said Alexander ; `` however , the dogs will serve us more faithfully . ''
Parsing [sent. 793 len. 29]: `` I do not mean my remark to include all Hottentots ; some are very faithful , and do their duty ; but it comprehends the majority . ''
Parsing [sent. 794 len. 6]: `` Are they courageous ? ''
Parsing [sent. 795 len. 3]: inquired Alexander .
Parsing [sent. 796 len. 37]: `` Yes , certainly , they may be considered as a brave race of men ; but occasionally there is a poltroon , and , like all cowards , he brags more than the rest . ''
Parsing [sent. 797 len. 37]: `` I 've a strong suspicion that we have one of that kind among our hunters , '' replied Henderson ; `` however , it is not fair to prejudge ; I may be mistaken . ''
Parsing [sent. 798 len. 29]: `` I think I know which you refer to , nevertheless , '' said Alexander ; `` it is the great fellow that they call Big Adam . ''
Parsing [sent. 799 len. 19]: `` You have hit upon the man , and to a certain degree corroborated my opinion of him .
Parsing [sent. 800 len. 16]: But the day is dawning , the sun will soon be above those hills . ''
Parsing [sent. 801 len. 19]: `` When we stop , I will have some grease put to those wagon-wheels , '' said Alexander .
Parsing [sent. 802 len. 20]: `` I fear it will be of little use , '' replied the Major ; `` creak they will .
Parsing [sent. 803 len. 35]: I do n't know whether the oxen here are like those in India ; but this I know , that the creaking of the carts and hackeries there is fifty times worse than this .
Parsing [sent. 804 len. 26]: The natives never grease the wheels ; they say the oxen would not go on if they did not hear the music behind them . ''
Parsing [sent. 805 len. 55]: `` Besides , the creaking of the wheels will by and by be of service ; when we are traveling through grass higher than our heads , we shall not be able to stop behind a minute , if we have not the creaking of the wheels to direct us how to follow . ''
Parsing [sent. 806 len. 17]: `` Well , then , I suppose we must save our grease , '' said Alexander .
Parsing [sent. 807 len. 36]: `` In a very few days you will be so accustomed to it , '' said the Major , `` that if it were to cease , you would feel the loss of it . ''
Parsing [sent. 808 len. 26]: `` Well , it may be so ; use is second nature ; but at present I feel as if the loss would be gain .
Parsing [sent. 809 len. 11]: There is the sun just showing himself above the hill .
Parsing [sent. 810 len. 8]: Shall we halt or go on ? ''
Parsing [sent. 811 len. 35]: `` Go on for another hour , and the men can thus examine the traces and the wagons by daylight , and then , when we stop , we can remedy any defects . ''
Parsing [sent. 812 len. 27]: `` Be it so ; there is a house , is there not , on the rising ground , as far as you can see ? ''
Parsing [sent. 813 len. 12]: `` Yes , I think so , '' replied the Major .
Parsing [sent. 814 len. 30]: `` I know it very well , '' said Swinton ; `` it is the farm of a Dutch boor , Milius , whom we saw at Algoa Bay .
Parsing [sent. 815 len. 12]: I did not think that we had got on so fast .
Parsing [sent. 816 len. 17]: It is about three miles off , so it will just be convenient for our breakfast .
Parsing [sent. 817 len. 19]: It will take us a good hour to arrive there , and then we will unyoke the oxen .
Parsing [sent. 818 len. 7]: How many have we yoked ? ''
Parsing [sent. 819 len. 6]: `` Ten to each wagon .
Parsing [sent. 820 len. 17]: The other sixteen are following with the sheep and horses ; they are as relays . ''
Parsing [sent. 821 len. 11]: `` Let us gallop on , '' said the Major .
Parsing [sent. 822 len. 26]: `` Agreed , '' replied the others ; and putting spurs to their horses , they soon arrived at the farmhouse of the Dutch planter .
Parsing [sent. 823 len. 43]: They were saluted with the barking and clamor of about twenty dogs , which brought out one of the young boors , who drove away the dogs by pelting them with bullock-horns , and other bones of animals which were strewed about .
Parsing [sent. 824 len. 7]: He then requested them to dismount .
Parsing [sent. 825 len. 47]: The old boor soon appeared , and gave them a hearty welcome , handing down from the shelf a large brandy-bottle , and recommending a dram , of which he partook himself , stating that it was good brandy , and made from his own peaches .
Parsing [sent. 826 len. 32]: Shortly afterward the wife of the boor made her appearance , and having saluted them , took up her station at a small table , with the tea apparatus before her .
Parsing [sent. 827 len. 46]: That refreshing beverage she now poured out for the visitors , handing a box , with some sugar-candy in it , for them to put a bit into their youths , and keep there as they drank their tea , by way of sweetening it .
Parsing [sent. 828 len. 41]: The old boor told them he had expected them , as he had been informed that they were to set out that day ; but he had concluded that they would arrive in the afternoon , and not so early .
Parsing [sent. 829 len. 19]: We may as well here give a description of a Dutch farmer 's house at the Cape settlement .
Parsing [sent. 830 len. 38]: It was a large square building , the wall built up of clay , and then plastered with a composition made by the boors , which becomes excessively hard in time ; after which it is whitewashed .
Parsing [sent. 831 len. 22]: The roof was thatched with a hard sort of rushes , more durable and less likely to catch fire than straw .
Parsing [sent. 832 len. 56]: There was no ceiling under the roof , but the rafters overhead were hung with a motley assemblage of the produce of the chase and farm , as large whips made of rhinoceros-hide , leopard and lion skins , ostrich eggs and feathers , strings of onions , rolls of tobacco , bamboos , etc. .
Parsing [sent. 833 len. 16]: The house contained one large eating-room , a small private room , and two bedrooms .
Parsing [sent. 834 len. 13]: The windows were not glazed , but closed with skins every night .
Parsing [sent. 835 len. 21]: There was no chimney or stove in the house , all the cooking being carried on in a small outhouse .
Parsing [sent. 836 len. 41]: The furniture was not very considerable : a large table , a few chairs and stools , some iron pots and kettles , a set of Dutch teacups , a teapot , and a brass kettle , with a heater .
Parsing [sent. 837 len. 38]: The large , brass-clasped , family Dutch Bible occupied a small table , at which the mistress of the house presided , and behind her chair were the carcasses of two sheep , suspended from a beam .
Parsing [sent. 838 len. 32]: Inquiries about the news at the Cape , and details of all the information which our travelers could give , had occupied the time till breakfast was put on the table .
Parsing [sent. 839 len. 19]: It consisted of mutton boiled and stewed , butter , milk , fruits , and good white bread .
Parsing [sent. 840 len. 14]: Before breakfast was over the caravan arrived , and the oxen were unyoked .
Parsing [sent. 841 len. 25]: Our travelers passed away two hours in going over the garden and orchards , and visiting the cattlefolds , and seeing the cows milked .
Parsing [sent. 842 len. 26]: They then yoked the teams , and wishing the old boor a farewell , and thanking him for his hospitality , they resumed their journey .
Parsing [sent. 843 len. 16]: `` Is it always the custom here to receive travelers in this friendly way ? ''
Parsing [sent. 844 len. 8]: observed Alexander , as they rode away .
Parsing [sent. 845 len. 23]: `` Always , '' replied Swinton ; `` there are no inns on the road , and every traveler finds a welcome .
Parsing [sent. 846 len. 9]: It is considered a matter of course . ''
Parsing [sent. 847 len. 8]: `` Do they never take payment ? ''
Parsing [sent. 848 len. 31]: `` Never , and it must not be offered ; but they will take the value of the corn supplied to your horses , as that is quite another thing .
Parsing [sent. 849 len. 28]: One peculiarity you will observe as you go along , which is , that the Dutch wife is a fixture at the little tea-table all day long .
Parsing [sent. 850 len. 26]: She never leaves it , and the tea is always ready for every traveler who claims their hospitality ; it is an odd custom . ''
Parsing [sent. 851 len. 16]: `` And I presume that occasions the good woman to become so very lusty . ''
Parsing [sent. 852 len. 30]: `` No doubt of it ; the whole exercise of the day is from the bedroom to the teapot , and back again , '' replied Swinton , laughing .
Parsing [sent. 853 len. 28]: `` One would hardly suppose that this apparently good-natured and hospitable people could have been guilty of such cruelty to the natives as Mr. Fairburn represented . ''
Parsing [sent. 854 len. 18]: `` Many of our virtues and vices are brought prominently forward by circumstances , '' replied Swinton .
Parsing [sent. 855 len. 19]: `` Hospitality in a thinly-inhabited country is universal , and a Dutch boor is hospitable to an excess .
Parsing [sent. 856 len. 34]: Their cruelty to the Hottentots and other natives arises from the prejudices of education : they have from their childhood beheld them treated as slaves , and do not consider them as fellow-creatures .
Parsing [sent. 857 len. 28]: As Mr. Fairburn truly said , nothing demoralizes so much , or so hardens the heart of man , as slavery existing and sanctioned by law . ''
Parsing [sent. 858 len. 15]: `` But are not the Dutch renowned for cruelty and love of money ? ''
Parsing [sent. 859 len. 17]: `` They have obtained that reputation , and I fear there is some reason for it .
Parsing [sent. 860 len. 64]: They took the lead , it must be remembered , as a commercial nation , more commercial than the Portuguese , whose steps they followed so closely : that this eager pursuit of wealth should create a love of money is but too natural , and to obtain money , men , under the influence of that passion , will stop at nothing .
Parsing [sent. 861 len. 48]: Their cruelties in the East are on record ; but the question is , whether the English , who followed the path of the Dutch , would not , had they gone before them , have been guilty of the same crimes to obtain the same ends ?
Parsing [sent. 862 len. 35]: The Spaniards were just as cruel in South America , and the Portuguese have not fallen short of them ; nay , I doubt if our own countrymen can be acquitted in many instances .
Parsing [sent. 863 len. 30]: The only difference is , that the other nations who preceded them in discoveries had greater temptation , because there were more riches and wealth to be obtained . ''
Parsing [sent. 864 len. 35]: `` Your remarks are just ; well may we say in the Lord 's Prayer , ` Lead us not into temptation , ' for we are all too frail to withstand it . ''
Parsing [sent. 865 len. 41]: At noon they again unyoked , and allowed the cattle to graze for an interval ; after which they proceeded till an hour before dark , when they mustered the men , and gave them their several charges and directions .
Parsing [sent. 866 len. 44]: At Alexander 's request the Major took this upon himself , and he made a long speech to the Hottentots , stating that it was their intention to reward those who did their duty , and to punish severely those who did not .
Parsing [sent. 867 len. 25]: They then collected wood for the fires , and had their supper , -- the first meal which they had taken out of doors .
Parsing [sent. 868 len. 83]: Mahomed , the Parsee servant of Major Henderson , cooked very much to their satisfaction ; and having tied the oxen to the wagons , to accustom them to the practice , more than from any danger to be apprehended , the watch was set to keep up the fires : they then all retired to bed , the gentlemen sleeping in their wagons , and the Hottentots underneath them , or by the sides of the fires which had been lighted .
Parsing [sent. 869 len. 23]: It will be unnecessary to enter into a detail of the journey to Graham 's Town , which was performed without difficulty .
Parsing [sent. 870 len. 31]: They did not arrive there until eight days after their departure from Algoa Bay , as they purposely lost time on the road , that things might find their places .
Parsing [sent. 871 len. 21]: At Graham 's Town they received every kindness and attention from the few military who were there and the landroost .
Parsing [sent. 872 len. 54]: Here they dismissed three of the men , who had remained drunk in the liquor-houses during their stay , and hired nine more , who were well recommended ; among these were two perfectly well acquainted with the Caffre language and country ; so that they were serviceable both as interpreters and guides .
Parsing [sent. 873 len. 26]: The day after their arrival , when they were out in the skirts of the town , Mr. Swinton perceived something moving in the bushes .
Parsing [sent. 874 len. 43]: He advanced cautiously , and discovered that it was a poor little Bushman boy , about twelve years old , quite naked , and evidently in a state of starvation , having been left there in a high fever by his people .
Parsing [sent. 875 len. 32]: He was so weak that he could not stand , and Mr. Swinton desired the Hottentot who was with him to lift him up , and carry him to the wagons .
Parsing [sent. 876 len. 21]: Some medicine and good food soon brought the little fellow round again , and he was able to walk about .
Parsing [sent. 877 len. 25]: He showed no disposition to leave them ; indeed he would watch for Mr. Swinton , and follow him as far as he could .
Parsing [sent. 878 len. 46]: The child evidently appeared to feel attachment and gratitude , and when they were about to depart , Mr. Swinton , through the medium of one of the Hottentots who could speak the language , asked him if he would like to stay with them .
Parsing [sent. 879 len. 31]: The answer was in the affirmative , and it was decided that he should accompany them , the Major observing that he would be a very good companion for Begum .
Parsing [sent. 880 len. 9]: `` What name shall we give him ? ''
Parsing [sent. 881 len. 3]: said Swinton .
Parsing [sent. 882 len. 24]: `` Why , as my baboon is by title a princess , I think we can not create him less than a prince .
Parsing [sent. 883 len. 7]: Let us call him Omrah . ''
Parsing [sent. 884 len. 24]: `` Omrah be it then , '' replied Mr. Swinton , `` until we can name him in a more serious way . ''
Parsing [sent. 885 len. 25]: So Omrah was put into the wagon , with Begum to amuse him , and our travelers took their departure from Graham 's Town .
Parsing [sent. 886 len. 8]: -LSB- Illustration : THE BUSHMAN BOY . -RSB-
Parsing [sent. 887 len. 3]: CHAPTER X .
Parsing [sent. 888 len. 13]: It was in the afternoon that they moved from Graham 's Town .
Parsing [sent. 889 len. 30]: They had intended to have started earlier , but they found it impossible to collect the Hottentots , who were taking their farewells of their wives and their liquor-shops .
Parsing [sent. 890 len. 31]: As it was , most of them were in a state of intoxication , and it was considered advisable to get them out of the town as soon as possible .
Parsing [sent. 891 len. 37]: Late in the evening they arrived at Hermann 's Kraal , a small military fort , where they remained for the night to give the Hottentots an opportunity of recovering from the effects of the liquor .
Parsing [sent. 892 len. 26]: The next morning they again started , and the landscape now changed its aspect , being covered with thick bushes , infested with wild beasts .
Parsing [sent. 893 len. 31]: A barren and sterile country was soon spread before them , the sun was oppressively hot , and not a sign of water was to be observed in any direction .
Parsing [sent. 894 len. 32]: At last they arrived at a muddy pool , in which elephants had evidently been enjoying themselves , and the oxen and horses were but too glad to do the same .
Parsing [sent. 895 len. 20]: At night they halted as before , having lighted fires to keep off the wild beasts and the elephants .
Parsing [sent. 896 len. 36]: The following morning they renewed their journey at daylight , and the scene again changed ; they now plunged into the dense forests bordering on the great Fish River , which they forded in safety .
Parsing [sent. 897 len. 28]: The prospects all around were very beautiful , the river smoothly gliding through stupendous mountains and precipices , with verdant valleys on each side of its banks .
Parsing [sent. 898 len. 24]: In the afternoon they arrived at Fort Wiltshire , the outermost defense of the colony , situated on the banks of the Keiskamma .
Parsing [sent. 899 len. 27]: English troops were stationed there , to prevent any marauding parties from passing the river , or to intercept them on their return with their booty .
Parsing [sent. 900 len. 55]: As this was the last spot where they could expect to see any of their countrymen , and they were kindly received by the officers , they agreed to remain two days , that they might obtain all the information which they could , and rearrange the stowing of the wagons before they started .
Parsing [sent. 901 len. 61]: The original plan had been to direct their course to Chumie , the first missionary station , which was about twenty-five miles distant ; but as it was out of their way , they now resolved to proceed direct to Butterworth , which was forty miles further in the Caffre country , and the more distant of the two missions .
Parsing [sent. 902 len. 37]: Our party took leave of their kind entertainers , and , having crossed without difficulty at the ford the Keiskamma river , had passed the neutral ground , and were in the land of the Caffres .
Parsing [sent. 903 len. 17]: Up to the present they had very little trouble with the Hottentots whom they had hired .
Parsing [sent. 904 len. 37]: As long as they were within reach of the law they behaved well ; but now that they had passed the confines of the Cape territory , some of them began to show symptoms of insubordination .
Parsing [sent. 905 len. 37]: The dismissal of one , however , with an order to go back immediately , and threatening to shoot him if he was ever seen in the caravan , had the desired effect of restoring order .
Parsing [sent. 906 len. 32]: The country was now a series of hills and dales , occasionally of deep ravines , and their route lay through the paths made by the elephants , which were numerous .
Parsing [sent. 907 len. 74]: A Hottentot of the name of Bremen , who was considered as their best man and most practiced hunter , begged Alexander and his companions to be careful how they went along , if they preceded the rest on horseback ; as the elephants always return by the same path at evening or after nightfall , in whatever direction they may have been feeding , and it is very dangerous to intercept them .
Parsing [sent. 908 len. 17]: For two days they continued their course in nearly a straight line for the missionary establishment .
Parsing [sent. 909 len. 49]: On the second evening , just about dusk , as they were crossing a woody hill , by the elephants ' path , being then about 200 yards in advance of the wagons , they were saluted with one of the most hideous shrieks that could be conceived .
Parsing [sent. 910 len. 21]: Their horses started back ; they could see nothing , although the sound echoed through the hills for some seconds .
Parsing [sent. 911 len. 6]: `` What was that ? ''
Parsing [sent. 912 len. 3]: exclaimed Alexander .
Parsing [sent. 913 len. 23]: `` Shout as loud as you can , '' cried the Major ; `` and turn your horses to the wagons . ''
Parsing [sent. 914 len. 30]: Alexander and Swinton joined the Major in the shout , and were soon accompanied by the whole mass of Hottentots , shouting and yelling as loud as they could .
Parsing [sent. 915 len. 23]: `` Silence , now , '' cried the Major ; every one was hushed , and they listened for a few seconds .
Parsing [sent. 916 len. 17]: `` It was only one , sir , and he is gone , '' said Bremen .
Parsing [sent. 917 len. 7]: `` We may go on . ''
Parsing [sent. 918 len. 6]: `` Only one what ? ''
Parsing [sent. 919 len. 3]: inquired Alexander .
Parsing [sent. 920 len. 35]: `` An elephant , sir , '' replied the Hottentot ; `` it 's well that he did not charge you ; he would have tumbled you down the precipice , horse and all .
Parsing [sent. 921 len. 26]: There must be a herd here , and we had better stop as soon as we are down the other side of the hill . ''
Parsing [sent. 922 len. 11]: `` I think so too , '' replied the Major .
Parsing [sent. 923 len. 34]: `` I shall not get that shriek out of my ears for a month , '' said Alexander ; `` why , the roar of a lion can not be so bad . ''
Parsing [sent. 924 len. 11]: `` Wait till you hear it , '' replied Swinton .
Parsing [sent. 925 len. 31]: They had now arrived at the bottom of the hill which they had been passing , and by the light of the stars they selected a spot for their encampment .
Parsing [sent. 926 len. 28]: Whether they were near to any Caffre kraals or not it was impossible to say ; but they heard no barking of dogs or lowing of oxen .
Parsing [sent. 927 len. 52]: Having collected all the cattle , they formed a square of the four wagons , and passed ropes from the one to the other ; the horses and sheep were driven within the square , and the oxen were , as usual , tied up to the sides of the wagons .
Parsing [sent. 928 len. 72]: It should here be observed , that the oxen were turned out to graze early in the morning , yoked in the afternoon , and they traveled then as far as they could after nightfall , to avoid the extreme heat of the day , the continual visits of the Carries , and the risk of losing the cattle if they were allowed to be loose and fed during the night .
Parsing [sent. 929 len. 29]: On the night we have been referring to , a more than usual number of fires were lighted , to keep off the elephants and other wild animals .
Parsing [sent. 930 len. 49]: The hyenas and wolves were very numerous , and prowled the whole night in hopes of getting hold of some of the sheep ; but as yet there had not been seen or heard a lion , although an occasional track had been pointed out by the Hottentots .
Parsing [sent. 931 len. 32]: When the Hottentots had finished their labor , our travelers had to wait till the fires were lighted and a sheep killed before they could have their suppers cooked by Mahomed .
Parsing [sent. 932 len. 69]: Begum , the baboon , had been released from her confinement since their crossing the Fish River , and as usual , when they sat down , came and made one of the party , generally creeping in close to her master until supper was served , when she would have her finger in every dish , and steal all she could , sometimes rather to their annoyance .
Parsing [sent. 933 len. 30]: Our little Bushman had now quite recovered not only his strength but his gayety , and was one of the most amusing little fellows that could be met with .
Parsing [sent. 934 len. 37]: He could not make himself understood except to one or two of the Hottentots ; but he was all pantomime , trying , by gestures and signs , to talk to Mr. Swinton and his companions .
Parsing [sent. 935 len. 29]: He endeavored to assist Mahomed as much as he could , and appeared to have attached himself to him , for he kept no company with the Hottentots .
Parsing [sent. 936 len. 22]: He was not more than three feet and a half high , and with limbs remarkably delicate , although well made .
Parsing [sent. 937 len. 28]: His face was very much like a monkey 's , and his gestures and manners completely so ; he was quite as active and full of fun .
Parsing [sent. 938 len. 59]: The watch had been set as soon as the fires were lighted ; and close to where Alexander and the others were seated , Big Adam , the Hottentot we have mentioned as having raised doubts in the mind of the Major as to his courage , had just mounted guard , with his gun in his hand .
Parsing [sent. 939 len. 27]: Omrah came up to where they were sitting , and they nodded and smiled at him , and said , `` How do you do ? ''
Parsing [sent. 940 len. 3]: in English .
Parsing [sent. 941 len. 25]: The boy , who had already picked up a few sentences , answered in the same words , `` How do you do ? ''
Parsing [sent. 942 len. 72]: and then pointing to Big Adam , whose back was turned , he began making a number of signs , and nodding his head ; at last he bent down , putting his arm in front of him , and raising it like an elephant 's trunk , walking with the measured steps of that animal , so as fully to make them Understand that he intended to portray an elephant .
