Parsing [sent. 1363 len. 69]: For some minutes he remained motionless with fright , expecting every moment that he would be in the jaws of the monster ; at last he recovered his presence of mind , and casting his eye toward his gun , moved his hand slowly toward it ; upon which the lion raised up his head and gave a tremendous roar which induced him hastily to withdraw his hand .
Parsing [sent. 1364 len. 18]: With this the lion appeared satisfied , and crouched with his head between his fore-paws as before .
Parsing [sent. 1365 len. 16]: After a little while the man made another attempt to possess himself of his gun .
Parsing [sent. 1366 len. 39]: The lion raised his head and gave another roar , and the man desisted ; another and another attempt were at intervals made , but always with the same anger shown on the part of the lion . ''
Parsing [sent. 1367 len. 16]: `` Why , the lion must have known what he wanted the gun for . ''
Parsing [sent. 1368 len. 17]: `` Most certainly he did , and therefore would not allow the man to touch it .
Parsing [sent. 1369 len. 29]: It is to be presumed that the sagacious creature had been fired at before ; but you observe , that he did not wish to harm the man .
Parsing [sent. 1370 len. 37]: He appeared to say -- You are in my power ; you shall not go away : you shall not take your musket to shoot me with , or I will tear you to pieces . ''
Parsing [sent. 1371 len. 7]: `` It certainly was very curious .
Parsing [sent. 1372 len. 7]: Pray how did it end ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1373 len. 49]: `` Why the heat of the sun on the rock was so overpowering , that the man was in great agony ; his naked feet were so burned , that he was compelled to keep moving them , placing one upon the other and changing them every minute .
Parsing [sent. 1374 len. 17]: The day passed , and the night also ; the lion never moved from the spot .
Parsing [sent. 1375 len. 22]: The sun rose again , and the heat became so intense that the poor man 's feet were past all feeling .
Parsing [sent. 1376 len. 80]: At noon , on that day , the lion rose and walked to the pool , which was only a few yards distant , looking behind him every moment to see if the man moved ; the man once more attempted to reach his gun , and the lion , perceiving it , turned in rage , and was on the point of springing upon him ; the man withdrew his hand , and the beast was pacified . ''
Parsing [sent. 1377 len. 6]: `` How very strange ! ''
Parsing [sent. 1378 len. 32]: `` The animal went to the water and drank ; it then returned and lay down at the same place as before , about a yard from the man 's feet .
Parsing [sent. 1379 len. 13]: Another night passed away , and the lion kept at his post .
Parsing [sent. 1380 len. 38]: The next day , in the forenoon , the animal again went to the water , and while there looked as if he heard a noise in an opposite quarter , and then disappeared in the bushes .
Parsing [sent. 1381 len. 37]: `` Perceiving this , the man made an effort , and seized his gun , but in attempting to rise he found it not in his power , as the strength of his ankles was gone .
Parsing [sent. 1382 len. 43]: With his gun in his hand , he crept to the pool and drank , and , looking at his feet , he discovered that his toes had been quite roasted and the skin torn off as he crawled through the grass .
Parsing [sent. 1383 len. 59]: He sat at the pool for a few minutes expecting the lion 's return , and resolved to send the contents of his gun through his head ; but the lion did not return , so the poor fellow tied his gun on his back and crawled away on his hands and knees as well as he could .
Parsing [sent. 1384 len. 39]: He was quite exhausted , and could have proceeded no further , when providentially a person fell in with him and assisted him home ; but he lost his toes , and was a cripple for life . ''
Parsing [sent. 1385 len. 53]: `` What makes this story more remarkable is , '' observed the Major , `` that the lion , as it is rational to suppose , must have been hungry after watching the man for sixty hours , even admitting that he had taken a meal but a short time before . ''
Parsing [sent. 1386 len. 47]: `` I know many other curious and well-authenticated anecdotes about this noble animal , '' observed Swinton , `` which I shall be happy to give you ; but I must look at my memorandum-book , or I may not be quite correct in my story .
Parsing [sent. 1387 len. 72]: One fact is very remarkable , and as I had it from Mr. -- , the missionary , who stated that he had several times observed it himself , I have no hesitation in vouching for its correctness , the more so , as I did once perceive a similar fact myself ; it is , that the fifth commandment is observed by lions -- they honor their father and mother .
Parsing [sent. 1388 len. 60]: `` If an old lion is in company with his children , as the natives call them , although they are in size equal to himself , or if a number of lions meet together in quest of game , there is always one who is admitted by them to be the oldest and ablest , and who leads .
Parsing [sent. 1389 len. 65]: If the game is come up with , it is this one who creeps up to it , and seizes it , while the others lie crouched upon the grass ; if the old lion is successful , which he generally is , he retires from his victim , and lies down to breathe himself and rest for perhaps a quarter of an hour .
Parsing [sent. 1390 len. 30]: The others in the meantime draw round and lie down at a respectful distance , but never presume to go near the animal which the old lion has killed .
Parsing [sent. 1391 len. 46]: As soon as the old lion considers himself sufficiently rested , he goes up to the prey and commences at the breast and stomach , and after eating a considerable portion he will take a second rest , none of the others presuming to move .
Parsing [sent. 1392 len. 33]: `` Having made a second repast , he then retires ; the other lions watch his motions , and all rush to the remainder of the carcass , which is soon devoured .
Parsing [sent. 1393 len. 20]: I said that I witnessed an instance myself in corroboration of this statement , which I will now mention .
Parsing [sent. 1394 len. 62]: I was sitting on a rock after collecting some plants , when below me I saw a young lion seize an antelope ; he had his paw upon the dead animal , when the old lion came up , -- upon which the young one immediately retired till his superior had dined first , and then came in for the remainder .
Parsing [sent. 1395 len. 4]: Mercy on us !
Parsing [sent. 1396 len. 5]: what is that ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1397 len. 28]: `` I thought it was the lion again , '' said Alexander , `` but it is thunder ; we are about to have a storm . ''
Parsing [sent. 1398 len. 30]: `` Yes , and a fierce one too , '' said the Major ; `` I am afraid that we must break up our party and retire under cover .
Parsing [sent. 1399 len. 10]: We have some large drops of rain already . ''
Parsing [sent. 1400 len. 21]: A flash of lightning now dazzled them , and was followed by another , and an instantaneous peal of thunder .
Parsing [sent. 1401 len. 39]: `` There is no mistake in this , '' said Swinton ; `` and I can tell you that we shall have it upon us in less than a minute , so I am for my wagon . ''
Parsing [sent. 1402 len. 28]: `` At all events it will wash these Hottentots sober , '' observed the Major , as they all walked away to their separate wagons for shelter .
Parsing [sent. 1403 len. 3]: CHAPTER XIV .
Parsing [sent. 1404 len. 22]: They had scarcely gained the wagons before the thunder and lightning became incessant , and so loud as to be deafening .
Parsing [sent. 1405 len. 40]: It appeared as if they were in the very center of the contending elements , and the wind rose and blew with terrific force , while the rain poured down as if the flood-gates of heaven were indeed opened .
Parsing [sent. 1406 len. 63]: The lightning was so vivid , that for the second that it lasted you could see the country round to the horizon almost as clear as day ; the next moment all was terrific gloom accompanied by the stunning reports of the thunder , which caused every article in the wagons , and the wagons themselves , to vibrate from the concussion .
Parsing [sent. 1407 len. 25]: A large tree , not fifty yards from the caravan , was struck by the lightning , and came down with an appalling crash .
Parsing [sent. 1408 len. 15]: The Caffres had all roused up , and had sheltered themselves under the wagons .
Parsing [sent. 1409 len. 29]: The Hottentots had also begun to move , but had not yet recovered their senses -- indeed , they were again stupefied by the clamor of the elements .
Parsing [sent. 1410 len. 39]: The storm lasted about an hour , and then as suddenly cleared up again ; the stars again made their appearance in the sky above , and the red tinge of the horizon announced the approach of daylight .
Parsing [sent. 1411 len. 34]: When the storm ceased , our travelers , who had not taken off their clothes , came out from their shelter , and met each other by the side of the extinguished fire .
Parsing [sent. 1412 len. 37]: `` Well , '' said Alexander , `` I have been made wise on two points this night ; I now know what an African storm is , and also the roar of an African lion .
Parsing [sent. 1413 len. 13]: Have you heard if there is any mischief done , Bremen ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1414 len. 10]: continued Alexander to the Hottentot , who stood by .
Parsing [sent. 1415 len. 38]: `` No , sir ; but I am afraid it will take us a long while to collect the cattle ; they will be dispersed in all directions , and we may have lost some of them .
Parsing [sent. 1416 len. 16]: It will soon be daylight , and then we must set off after them . ''
Parsing [sent. 1417 len. 9]: `` Are those fellows quite sober now ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1418 len. 23]: `` Yes , sir , '' replied Bremen , laughing ; `` water has washed all the liquor out of them . ''
Parsing [sent. 1419 len. 22]: `` Well , you may tell them , as a punishment , I shall stop their tobacco for a week . ''
Parsing [sent. 1420 len. 33]: `` Better not now , sir , '' said Bremen , thoughtfully ; `` the men do n't like to go further up the country , and they may be troublesome . ''
Parsing [sent. 1421 len. 28]: `` I think so too , '' said Swinton ; `` you must recollect that the cask was running out , and the temptation was too strong .
Parsing [sent. 1422 len. 7]: I should overlook it this time .
Parsing [sent. 1423 len. 12]: Give them a severe reprimand , and let them off . ''
Parsing [sent. 1424 len. 55]: `` I believe it will be the best way , '' replied Alexander ; `` not that I fear their refusing to go on , for if they do , I will dismiss them , and go on with the Caffres ; they dare not go back by themselves , that is certain . ''
Parsing [sent. 1425 len. 63]: `` Sir , '' said Bremen , `` that is very true ; but you must not trust the Caffres too much -- Caffres always try to get guns and ammunition : Caffre king , Hinza , very glad to get the wagons and what is in them : make him rich man , and powerful man , with so many guns .
Parsing [sent. 1426 len. 66]: Caffre king will not rob in his own country , because he is afraid of the English ; but if the wagon 's robbed , and you are killed in this country , which is not his , then he make excuses , and say , ' I know nothing about it , ' Say that their people do it , not his people . ''
Parsing [sent. 1427 len. 35]: `` Bremen talks very sensibly , '' said the Major ; `` we must keep the Hottentots as a check to the Caffres , and the Caffres as a check to the Hottentots . ''
Parsing [sent. 1428 len. 14]: `` That is our policy , depend upon it , '' replied Swinton .
Parsing [sent. 1429 len. 19]: `` You are right , and we will do so ; but the day is breaking ; so ?
Parsing [sent. 1430 len. 23]: Bremen , collect the people together to search for the cattle ; and , Omrah , tell Mahomed to come here . ''
Parsing [sent. 1431 len. 65]: `` By the by , Swinton , '' said Major Henderson , `` those elephants ' tusks lying by the wagon remind me of a question I want to put to you : -- In Ceylon , where I have often hunted the elephant , they have no tusks ; and in India the tusks are not common , and in general very small .
Parsing [sent. 1432 len. 9]: How do you account for this variety ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1433 len. 44]: `` It has been observed before ; and it is but a fair surmise , that Providence , ever attentive to the wants of the meanest animals , has furnished such large tusks to the African elephant for the necessity which requires them .
Parsing [sent. 1434 len. 35]: In Ceylon there is plenty of grass , and an abundant supply of water all the year round ; and further , in Ceylon , the elephant has no enemy to defend himself against .
Parsing [sent. 1435 len. 57]: Here , in Africa , the rivers are periodical torrents , which dry up , and the only means which an elephant has of obtaining water during the dry season is to dig with his tusks into the bed of the river , till he finds the water , which he draws up with his trunk .
Parsing [sent. 1436 len. 21]: Moreover , he has to defend himself against the rhinoceros , which is a formidable antagonist , and often victorious .
Parsing [sent. 1437 len. 35]: He requires tusks also for his food in this country , for the elephant digs up the mimosa here with his tusks , that he may feed upon the succulent roots of the tree .
Parsing [sent. 1438 len. 15]: Indeed , an elephant in Africa without his tusks could not well exist . ''
Parsing [sent. 1439 len. 61]: `` Thank you for your explanation , which appears very satisfactory and conclusive ; and now let us go to breakfast , for Mahomed , I perceive , is ready , and Omrah has displayed our teacups , and is very busy blowing into the spout of the teapot , a Bushman way of ascertaining if it is stopped up .
Parsing [sent. 1440 len. 22]: However , we must not expect to make a London footman out of a ` Child of the Desert . ' ''
Parsing [sent. 1441 len. 14]: `` Where is his adversary and antagonist , the valiant Big Adam ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1442 len. 24]: `` He was among those who indulged in the liquor yesterday afternoon , and I believe was worse than any one of them .
Parsing [sent. 1443 len. 30]: The little Bushman did not fail to take advantage of his defenseless state , and has been torturing him in every way he could imagine during the whole night .
Parsing [sent. 1444 len. 28]: I saw him pouring water into the Hottentot 's mouth as he lay on his back with his mouth wide open , till he nearly choked him .
Parsing [sent. 1445 len. 67]: To get it down faster , Omrah had taken the big tin funnel , and had inserted one end into his mouth , which he filled till the water ran out ; after that he was trying what he could do with fire , for he began putting hot embers between Big Adam 's toes ; I dare say the fellow can not walk to-day . ''
Parsing [sent. 1446 len. 28]: `` I fear that some day he will kill Omrah , or do him some serious injury ; the boy must be cautioned , '' said Alexander .
Parsing [sent. 1447 len. 75]: `` I am afraid it will be of no use , and Omrah must take his chance : he is aware of Big Adam 's enmity as well as you are , and is always on his guard ; but as for persuading him to leave off his tricks , or to reconcile them to each other , it is impossible , '' said Swinton -- `` you do n't know a Bushman . ''
Parsing [sent. 1448 len. 31]: `` Then pray tell us something about them , '' said the Major , `` as soon as you have finished that elephant-steak , which you appear to approve of .
Parsing [sent. 1449 len. 8]: Of what race are the Bushmen ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1450 len. 41]: `` I will tell you when I have finished my breakfast , '' replied Swinton , `` and not before : if I begin to talk , you will eat all the steak , and that wo n't do . ''
Parsing [sent. 1451 len. 15]: `` I suspect that we shall not leave this to-day , '' said Alexander .
Parsing [sent. 1452 len. 49]: `` If , as Bremen says , the cattle have strayed very far , it will be too late to go in the afternoon , and to-morrow you recollect is Sunday , and that , we have agreed , shall be kept as it ought to be . ''
Parsing [sent. 1453 len. 37]: `` Very true , '' said the Major ; `` then we must make Swinton entertain us by telling us more about the lions , for he had not finished when the storm came on . ''
Parsing [sent. 1454 len. 43]: `` No , '' replied Swinton ; `` I had a great deal more to say , and I shall be very happy at any seasonable time , Major , to tell you what I know -- but not just now . ''
Parsing [sent. 1455 len. 68]: `` My dear fellow , '' said the Major , putting another piece of elephant-steak upon Swinton 's plate , `` pray do n't entertain the idea that I want you to talk on purpose that I may eat your share and my own too ; only ascribe my impatience to the true cause -- the delight I have in receiving instruction and amusement from you . ''
Parsing [sent. 1456 len. 15]: `` Well , Swinton , you have extorted a compliment from the Major . ''
Parsing [sent. 1457 len. 22]: `` Yes , and an extra allowance of steak , which is a better thing , '' replied Swinton , laughing .
Parsing [sent. 1458 len. 19]: `` Now I have finished my breakfast , I will tell what I know about Omrah 's people .
Parsing [sent. 1459 len. 38]: `` The Bushmen are originally a Hottentot race -- of that I think there is little doubt ; but I believe they are a race of people produced by circumstances , if I may use the expression .
Parsing [sent. 1460 len. 17]: The Hottentot on the plains lives a nomad life , pasturing and living upon his herds .
Parsing [sent. 1461 len. 99]: The Bushman may be considered as the Hottentot driven out of his fertile plains , deprived of his cattle , and compelled to resort to the hills for his safety and subsistence -- in short , a Hill Hottentot : impelled by hunger and by injuries , he has committed depredations upon the property of others until he has had a mark set upon him ; his hand has been against every man , and he has been hunted like a wild beast , and compelled to hide himself in the caves of almost inaccessible rocks and hills .
Parsing [sent. 1462 len. 30]: `` Thus , generation after generation , he has suffered privation and hunger , till the race has dwindled down to the small size which it is at present .
Parsing [sent. 1463 len. 45]: Unable to contend against force , his only weapons have been his cunning and his poisoned arrows , and with them he has obtained his livelihood -- or rather , it may be said , has contrived to support life , and no more .
Parsing [sent. 1464 len. 69]: There are , however , many races mixed up with the Bushmen ; for runaway slaves , brought from Madagascar , Malays , and even those of the mixed white breed , when they have committed murder or other penal crimes , have added to the race and incorporated themselves with them ; they are called the Children of the Desert , and they are literally such . ''
Parsing [sent. 1465 len. 9]: `` Have you seen much of them ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1466 len. 22]: `` Yes , when I was in the Namaqua-land and in the Bechuana territory I saw a great deal of them .
Parsing [sent. 1467 len. 31]: I do not think that they are insensible to kindness , and moreover , I believe that they may often be trusted ; but you run a great risk . ''
Parsing [sent. 1468 len. 9]: `` Have they ever shown any gratitude ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1469 len. 38]: `` Yes ; when I have killed game for them , they have followed me on purpose to show me the pools of waters without which we should have suffered severely , if we had not perished .
Parsing [sent. 1470 len. 40]: We were talking about lions ; it is an old-received opinion , that the jackal is the lion 's provider ; it would be a more correct one to say that the lion is the Bushman 's provider . ''
Parsing [sent. 1471 len. 4]: `` Indeed ! ''
Parsing [sent. 1472 len. 14]: `` I once asked a Bushman , ` How do you live ? '
Parsing [sent. 1473 len. 12]: His reply was , ' I live by the lions . '
Parsing [sent. 1474 len. 8]: I asked him to explain to me .
Parsing [sent. 1475 len. 72]: He said , ' I will show what I do : I let the lions follow the game and kill it and eat till they have their bellies full , then I go up to where the lion is sitting down by the carcass , and I go pretty near to him ; I cry out , What have you got there , can not you spare me some of it ?
Parsing [sent. 1476 len. 17]: Go away and let me have some meat , or I 'll do you some harm .
Parsing [sent. 1477 len. 46]: Then I dance and jump about and shake my skin-dress , and the lion looks at me , and he turns round and walks away ; he growls very much , but he do n't stay , and then I eat the rest . ' ''
Parsing [sent. 1478 len. 7]: `` And is that true ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1479 len. 17]: `` Yes , I believe it , as I have had it confessed by many others .
Parsing [sent. 1480 len. 65]: The fact is , the lion is only dangerous when he is hungry -- that is , if he is not attacked ; and if , as the Bushman said , the lion has eaten sufficiently , probably not wishing to be disturbed , after his repast , by the presence and shouts of the Bushman , the animal retires to some other spot .
Parsing [sent. 1481 len. 47]: I was informed that a very short time afterward , this Bushman , who told me what I have detailed to you , was killed by a lioness , when attempting to drive it away from its prey by shouting as he was used to do .
Parsing [sent. 1482 len. 61]: The fact was , that he perceived a lioness devouring a wild horse , and went up to her as usual ; but he did not observe that she had her whelps with her : he shouted ; she growled savagely , and before he had time to retreat , she sprang upon him and tore him to pieces . ''
Parsing [sent. 1483 len. 17]: `` The lion does not prey upon men , then , although he destroys them ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1484 len. 74]: `` Not generally ; but the Namaqua people told me that , if a lion once takes a fancy to men 's flesh -- and they do , after they have in their hunger devoured one or two -- they become doubly dangerous , as they will leave all other game and hunt man only ; but this I can not vouch for being the truth , although it is very probable . ''
Parsing [sent. 1485 len. 15]: `` If we judge from analogy , it is , '' replied the Major .
Parsing [sent. 1486 len. 39]: `` The Bengal tigers in India , it is well known , if they once taste human flesh , prefer it to all other , and they are well known to the natives , who term them man-eaters .
Parsing [sent. 1487 len. 29]: Strange to say , it appears that human flesh is not wholesome for them ; for their skins become mangy after they have taken to eating that alone .
Parsing [sent. 1488 len. 26]: I have shot a ` man-eater ' from the back of an elephant , and I found that the skin was not worth taking . ''
Parsing [sent. 1489 len. 46]: `` The Namaquas , '' replied Swinton , `` told me that a lion , once enamored of human flesh , would , in order to obtain it so far overcome his caution , that he would leap through a fire to seize a man .
Parsing [sent. 1490 len. 59]: I once went to visit a Namaqua chief , who had been severely wounded by a lion of this description -- a man-eater , as the Major terms them , -- and he gave me the following dreadful narrative , which certainly corroborates what they assert of the lion who had once taken a fancy to human flesh .
Parsing [sent. 1491 len. 30]: `` The chief told me that he had gone out with a party of his men to hunt : they had guns , bows and arrows , and assaguays .
Parsing [sent. 1492 len. 39]: On the first day , as they were pursuing an elephant , they came across some lions , who attacked them and they were obliged to save their lives by abandoning a horse , which the lions devoured .
Parsing [sent. 1493 len. 23]: They then made hiding-places of thick bushes by a pool , where they knew the elephant and rhinoceros would come to drink .
Parsing [sent. 1494 len. 50]: `` As they fired at a rhinoceros , a lion leaped into their inclosure , took up one of the men in his mouth and carried him off , and all that they afterward could find of him the next day was one of the bones of his leg .
Parsing [sent. 1495 len. 40]: The next night , as they were sitting by a fire inside of their inclosure of bushes , a lion came , seized one of the men , dragged him through the fire , and tore out his back .
Parsing [sent. 1496 len. 53]: One of the party fired , but missed ; upon which , the lion , dropping his dying victim , growled at the men across the fire , and they durst not repeat the shot ; the lion then took up his prey in his mouth , and went off with it .
Parsing [sent. 1497 len. 27]: `` Alarmed at such disasters , the Namaquas collected together in one strong inclosure , and at night sent out one of the slaves for water .
Parsing [sent. 1498 len. 50]: He had no sooner reached the pool than he was seized by a lion ; he called in vain for help , but was dragged off through the woods , and the next day his skull only was found , clean licked by the rough tongue of the lion .
Parsing [sent. 1499 len. 25]: `` Having now lost three men in three days , the chief and his whole party turned out to hunt and destroy lions only .
Parsing [sent. 1500 len. 61]: They followed the spoor or track of the one which had taken the slave , and they soon found two lions , one of which , the smallest , they shot ; and then , having taken their breakfast , they went after the other , and largest , which was recognized as the one which had devoured the man .
Parsing [sent. 1501 len. 67]: `` They followed the animal to a patch of reeds , where it had intrenched itself ; they set fire to the reeds and forced it out , and as it was walking off it was severely wounded by one of the party , when it immediately turned back , and , with a loud roar , charged right through the smoke and the burning reeds .
Parsing [sent. 1502 len. 25]: The monster dashed in among them and seized the chief 's brother by the back , tearing out his ribs and exposing his lungs .
Parsing [sent. 1503 len. 17]: `` The chief rushed to the assistance of his expiring brother ; his gun burned priming .
Parsing [sent. 1504 len. 16]: He dashed it down , and in his desperation seized the lion by the tail .
Parsing [sent. 1505 len. 43]: The lion let go the body , and turned upon the chief , and with a stroke of his fore-paw tore a large piece of flesh off the chief 's arm ; then struck him again and threw him on the ground .
Parsing [sent. 1506 len. 30]: The chief rose instantly , but the lion then seized him by the knee , threw him down again , and there held him , mangling his left arm .
Parsing [sent. 1507 len. 38]: `` Torn and bleeding , the chief in a feeble voice called to his men to shoot the animal from behind , which was at last done with a ball which passed through the lion 's brain .
Parsing [sent. 1508 len. 40]: After this destruction of four men in four days , the hunting was given over ; the body of the chief 's brother was buried , and the party went home , bearing with them their wounded chief . ''
Parsing [sent. 1509 len. 19]: `` Well , that is the most horrible lion-adventure I have yet heard , '' said the Major .
Parsing [sent. 1510 len. 10]: `` Heaven preserve us from a man-eating lion ! ''
Parsing [sent. 1511 len. 14]: `` It really has almost taken away my breath , '' said Alexander .
Parsing [sent. 1512 len. 33]: `` Well , then , I will tell you one more amusing , and not so fatal in its results ; I was told it by a Bushman , '' said Swinton .
Parsing [sent. 1513 len. 43]: `` A Bushman was following a herd of zebras , and had just succeeded in wounding one with his arrow , when he discovered that he had been interfering with a lion , who was also in chase of the same animals .
Parsing [sent. 1514 len. 50]: As the lion appeared very angry at this interference with his rights as lord of the manor , and evidently inclined to punish the Bushman as a poacher upon his preserves , the latter , perceiving a tree convenient , climbed up into it as fast as he could .
Parsing [sent. 1515 len. 19]: The lion allowed the herd of zebras to go away , and turned his attention to the Bushman .
Parsing [sent. 1516 len. 23]: He walked round and round the tree , and every now and then he growled as he looked up at the Bushman .
Parsing [sent. 1517 len. 22]: `` At last the lion lay down at the foot of the tree , and there he kept watch all night .
Parsing [sent. 1518 len. 37]: The Bushman kept watch also , but toward morning , feeling very tired , he was overcome by sleep , and as he slept , he dreamed , and what do you think that he dreamed ?
Parsing [sent. 1519 len. 16]: -- he dreamed that he fell from the tree into the jaws of the lion .
Parsing [sent. 1520 len. 37]: Starting up in horror from the effects of his dream , he lost his hold , and falling from the branch , down he came with all his weight right on the back of the lion .
Parsing [sent. 1521 len. 65]: The lion , so unexpectedly saluted , sprang up with a loud roar , tossing off the Bushman , and running away as fast as he could ; and the Bushman , recovering his legs and his senses , also took to his heels in a different direction ; and thus were the ` sleepers awakened , ' and the dream became true . ''
Parsing [sent. 1522 len. 19]: `` Besiegers retreating and fort evacuated both at the same time , '' cried the Major , laughing .
Parsing [sent. 1523 len. 18]: `` Well , I think you have had enough of the lion now , '' said Swinton .
Parsing [sent. 1524 len. 20]: `` No , we had quite enough of him last night , if you choose , '' replied Alexander .
Parsing [sent. 1525 len. 14]: `` But your lions are not quite so near as he was . ''
Parsing [sent. 1526 len. 3]: CHAPTER XV .
Parsing [sent. 1527 len. 52]: It was not until the evening that the Caffres and Hottentots returned with the cattle , which they had great difficulty in collecting ; two or three of the oxen were not brought back till late at night , so frightened had the animals been by the approach of the lion .
Parsing [sent. 1528 len. 53]: In the afternoon , as it was too late to think of proceeding , our travelers , with their guns on their shoulders , and accompanied by Omrah and Begum , who would always follow the Major if she was not tied up , strolled away from the camp to amuse themselves .
Parsing [sent. 1529 len. 68]: At first they walked to the hill from which they had such a splendid view of the valley covered with elephants , and , proceeding to where the male elephant had fallen , found that his flesh had , by the Caffres , the wolves , and the vultures , been completely taken off his bones , and it lay there a beautiful skeleton for a museum .
Parsing [sent. 1530 len. 33]: As , however , they had no room for such weighty articles in their wagons , they left it , after Swinton had made some observations upon the structure of the animal .
Parsing [sent. 1531 len. 16]: Begum would not go near the skeleton , but appeared to be frightened at it .
Parsing [sent. 1532 len. 48]: They then proceeded to the rock which had been their place of refuge when the herd of elephants had charged upon them ; and as they stood under it , they were suddenly saluted with a loud noise over their heads , sounding like quah , quah !
Parsing [sent. 1533 len. 26]: As soon as Begum heard it , she ran up to the Major with every sign of trepidation , holding fast to his skin trowsers .
Parsing [sent. 1534 len. 6]: `` What was that ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1535 len. 9]: said Alexander ; `` I see nothing . ''
Parsing [sent. 1536 len. 35]: `` I know what it is , '' said the Major ; `` it is a herd of baboons ; there they are ; do n't you see their heads over the rocks ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1537 len. 26]: `` Let them show themselves a little more , and we 'll have a shot at them , '' replied Alexander , cocking his gun .
Parsing [sent. 1538 len. 30]: `` Not for your life , '' cried Swinton ; `` you will be skinned and torn to pieces , if they are numerous , and you enrage them .
Parsing [sent. 1539 len. 12]: You have no idea what savage and powerful creatures they are .
Parsing [sent. 1540 len. 18]: Look at them now ; they are coming down gradually ; we had better be off . ''
Parsing [sent. 1541 len. 35]: `` I think so too , '' said the Major ; `` they are very angry ; they have seen Begum , and imagine that we have one of their herd in our possession .
Parsing [sent. 1542 len. 25]: Pray do n't fire , Wilmot , unless it is for your life ; we are too few to make them afraid of us .
Parsing [sent. 1543 len. 37]: Here they come ; there are a hundred of them at least ; let us walk away slowly -- it wo n't do to run , for that would make them chase us at once . ''
Parsing [sent. 1544 len. 60]: The baboons , some of which were of gigantic size , were now descending from the rock , grunting , grinning , springing from stone to stone , protruding their mouths , shaking their heads , drawing back the skin of their foreheads , and showing their formidable tusks , advancing nearer and nearer , and threatening an attack .
Parsing [sent. 1545 len. 16]: Some of the largest males advanced so close as to make a snatch at Omrah .
Parsing [sent. 1546 len. 17]: As for Begum , she kept behind the Major , hiding herself as much as possible .
Parsing [sent. 1547 len. 54]: At last one or two advanced so close , rising on their hind-legs , that the Major was obliged to ward them off with his gun , `` Point your guns at them , '' said Swinton , `` if they come too close ; but do not fire , I beg you .
Parsing [sent. 1548 len. 23]: If we only get from off this rocky ground to the plain below , we shall probably get rid of them . ''
Parsing [sent. 1549 len. 48]: The ground on which they were formed a portion of the rocky hill upon which they had taken shelter the day of the elephant-hunt ; and within twenty-five yards of them there was an abrupt descent of about four feet , which joined it to the plain .
Parsing [sent. 1550 len. 67]: They had gained half-way , parrying the animals off as well as they could , as they retreated backward , when some of the baboons came down from the other side of the rock , so as to attempt to cut off their retreat , their object evidently being to gain possession of Begum , whom they considered as belonging to them -- and a captive .
Parsing [sent. 1551 len. 34]: Their situation now became more critical ; for the whole herd were joining the foremost ; and the noise they made , and the anger they expressed , were much greater than before .
Parsing [sent. 1552 len. 38]: `` We must fire , I really believe , '' said the Major , when they heard a deep , hollow growl , followed up by a roar of some animal , apparently not very far off .
Parsing [sent. 1553 len. 67]: At this sound the baboons halted , and listened in silence ; again the growl was repeated , and followed up by the roar , and the baboons , at a shriek given by one on the rock , turned round and took to their heels , much to the delight of our travelers , who had felt the peculiar difficulty and danger of their situation .
Parsing [sent. 1554 len. 12]: `` What animal was that which has frightened them off ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1555 len. 4]: said the Major .
Parsing [sent. 1556 len. 28]: `` It was the growl of a leopard , '' replied Swinton ; `` we must keep a sharp look-out ; it ca n't be far off .
Parsing [sent. 1557 len. 10]: The leopard is the great enemy of the baboons .
Parsing [sent. 1558 len. 6]: But where is Omrah ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1559 len. 14]: They all looked round , but the boy was not to be seen .
Parsing [sent. 1560 len. 50]: At last he showed his head above the foot of the rocky hill , where there was a descent of four feet , as we have mentioned , then sprang up the rock , and began capering , and imitating the baboons as they came on to the attack .
Parsing [sent. 1561 len. 44]: As they were laughing at him , all at once he stopped , and putting his hands to his mouth he gave the growl and roar of a leopard , which they had heard , and then set off running away baboon fashion .
Parsing [sent. 1562 len. 21]: `` It was the Bushman , then , that frightened them off ; he is a clever little fellow . ''
Parsing [sent. 1563 len. 38]: `` And I am not sure that he has not saved our lives , '' replied Swinton ; `` but he has been brought up among them , one may say , and knows their habits well .
Parsing [sent. 1564 len. 38]: If he had not hid himself below the rocks before he imitated the leopard , it would have been of no use , for they would not have been frightened , hearing the growl proceeding from him .
Parsing [sent. 1565 len. 10]: I admire the boy 's presence of mind . ''
Parsing [sent. 1566 len. 19]: `` I thought at one time that the baboons had an idea that Omrah was one of them .
Parsing [sent. 1567 len. 9]: What a snatch they made at him ! ''
Parsing [sent. 1568 len. 45]: `` It would not have been the first time that these animals have carried off a boy , '' said Swinton ; `` I saw one at Latakoo , who had lived two years with the baboons , which had carried him off . ''
Parsing [sent. 1569 len. 8]: `` How did they treat him ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1570 len. 12]: `` Very well indeed ; but they kept him a prisoner .
Parsing [sent. 1571 len. 34]: When they found that he would not eat the coarse food which they did , they brought him other things ; and they invariably allowed him to drink first at the pools . ''
Parsing [sent. 1572 len. 10]: `` Well , that was homage to our superiority .
Parsing [sent. 1573 len. 17]: Confound their quahs , I shall not get them out of my head for a week .
Parsing [sent. 1574 len. 8]: What terrible large tusks they have ! ''
Parsing [sent. 1575 len. 9]: `` Yes , their incisors are very strong .
Parsing [sent. 1576 len. 36]: They often destroy the leopard when they meet it in numbers ; but if one happens to be away from the herd , he has , of course , no chance with such an animal .
Parsing [sent. 1577 len. 13]: Begum did not appear at all willing to renew her connection . ''
Parsing [sent. 1578 len. 54]: `` None of the monkey tribe , after they have lived with man , ever are ; indeed it is a question , if they had taken possession of her , whether they would not have torn her to pieces immediately , or have worried her to death some way or other . ''
Parsing [sent. 1579 len. 35]: `` Well , at all events , Swinton , you have been rewarded for your kindness to that poor little Bushman , and we have reaped the benefit of it , '' observed Alexander .
Parsing [sent. 1580 len. 21]: `` But here come some of the oxen ; I hope we shall be able to start early on Monday .
Parsing [sent. 1581 len. 14]: The native Caffres say that the wagons can not proceed much further . ''
Parsing [sent. 1582 len. 27]: `` No , not further than to the banks of the Umtata River : but you will then be not a great way from your destination .
Parsing [sent. 1583 len. 12]: Daaka is the chief 's name , is it not ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1584 len. 23]: `` Yes , that is his name ; and if he is as supposed to be , he is my first cousin .
Parsing [sent. 1585 len. 38]: How strange it sounds to me , as I look around me in this savage and wild country , that I should be within forty miles of a blood-relation , who is an inhabitant of it ! ''
Parsing [sent. 1586 len. 41]: `` Well , we shall soon know the truth ; but I must say , if it is only to end in a morning call , you have come a long way for the purpose , '' replied the Major .
Parsing [sent. 1587 len. 37]: `` I have come to ascertain a fact , which , from what I now know of the country and its inhabitants , will be the source of any thing but pleasure if it be established .
Parsing [sent. 1588 len. 25]: My only hope is that it may prove otherwise than we suppose ; and there is little chance of that , I fear . ''
Parsing [sent. 1589 len. 22]: `` At all events , come what may , '' observed Swinton , `` you will have done your duty . ''
Parsing [sent. 1590 len. 45]: On their return , they found all the men and cattle collected , and that night they increased the number of their fires , and tied the oxen to the wagons , that they might not be scattered by the return of the lion .
Parsing [sent. 1591 len. 20]: The latter did not , however , make his appearance , and the night was passed without any disturbance .
Parsing [sent. 1592 len. 71]: The following day being Sunday , the Hottentots were assembled , and desired not to start from the camp , as they would be expected to attend to prayers and Divine service ; and as no hunting expedition was proposed , the Caffre warriors , as well as the native Caffres , who came in with their baskets of milk and other articles for sale and barter , also remained .
Parsing [sent. 1593 len. 47]: Before dinner-time , the bell which had been brought with them from the Cape , to ring in case of any one having strayed from the camp , that he might be guided to return , was tolled by Bremen , and the Hottentots were assembled .
Parsing [sent. 1594 len. 11]: Prayers and a portion of the Bible were then read .
Parsing [sent. 1595 len. 51]: The Caffre warriors , who had been told that the white men were going to pray to their God , were very silent and attentive , although they could not understand what was said ; and the native Caffres , men , women and children , sat down and listened .
Parsing [sent. 1596 len. 29]: As soon as the service was over , the Caffre head man of the warriors asked the interpreter to inquire of our travelers why they struck the bell ?
Parsing [sent. 1597 len. 21]: was it to let God know that they were about to pray , and did he hear what they said ?
Parsing [sent. 1598 len. 24]: Swinton replied , that their God heard all that they said , and listened to the prayers of those who trusted in him .
Parsing [sent. 1599 len. 55]: A great many other questions were put by the Caffres , all of which were replied to with great caution by Mr. Swinton , as he was fearful that they might not otherwise be understood by the Caffres ; but they were , as it was proved by the questions which followed in consequence .
Parsing [sent. 1600 len. 91]: A great portion of the afternoon was passed away in explaining and replying to the interrogatories of these people , and our travelers felt convinced that by having kept the Sabbath in that savage land they had done some good by the example ; for , as Swinton truly observed -- `` The missionaries come into the land to spread the gospel of Christ ; they tell the natives that such is the religion and belief of the white men , and that such are the doctrines which are inculcated .
Parsing [sent. 1601 len. 73]: Now white men come here as traders , or are occasionally seen here as travelers ; and if the natives find , as they have found , that these white men , stated by the missionaries to hold the same belief , not only show no evidence of their belief , but are guilty of sins expressly forbidden by the religion preached , is not the work of the missionary nearly destroyed ?
Parsing [sent. 1602 len. 54]: `` I have often thought that the behavior of the Dutch boors toward the natives must have had such an effect ; indeed , I may say that the colony has been founded upon very opposite principles to those of ` doing unto others as you would they should do unto you . '
Parsing [sent. 1603 len. 46]: I believe that there never yet was an intercourse between Christians nominal and savages , in any portion of the globe , but that the savages have with great justice thrown in the Christians ' teeth , that they preached one thing but did another .
Parsing [sent. 1604 len. 8]: Unfortunately the taunt is but too true .
Parsing [sent. 1605 len. 17]: Even those who had left their country for religious persecution have erred in the same way .
Parsing [sent. 1606 len. 26]: The conduct of the Puritans who landed at Salem was as barbarous toward the Indians as that of Pizarro and his followers toward the Mexicans .
Parsing [sent. 1607 len. 12]: In either case the poor aborigines were hunted to death . ''
Parsing [sent. 1608 len. 43]: On Monday they started at daylight , and proceeded on the journey ; but they made little progress , on account of the difficulty of traveling with the wagons in a country consisting of alternate precipices and ravines , without any roads .
Parsing [sent. 1609 len. 50]: The second day proved to be one of greater difficulty ; they were obliged to cut down trees , fill up holes , remove large pieces of rock , and with every precaution the wagons were often out of order , and they were obliged to halt for repairs .
Parsing [sent. 1610 len. 44]: At night they were about ten miles from the Umtata River , and it was doubtful , from the accounts received from the natives of the country , if they would be able to go further with the wagons than to its bank .
Parsing [sent. 1611 len. 66]: But in the evening , news was brought that the Amaquibi , the nation of warriors which were governed by Quetoo , and which had come from the north , had been attacked by two of the native tribes , aided by some white men with guns ; that the white men had all been destroyed , and that the hostile army were marching south .
Parsing [sent. 1612 len. 20]: The native Caffres appeared to be in a panic , and this panic was soon communicated to the Hottentots .
Parsing [sent. 1613 len. 23]: At first , murmurings were heard as they sat round the fire , and at last they broke out into open mutiny .
Parsing [sent. 1614 len. 62]: Big Adam , with three others , came up to the fire where our travelers were sitting , and intimated that they must return immediately , as they would proceed no further ; that if it was decided to go on , the Hottentots would not , as they had no intention of being murdered by the savages who were advancing .
Parsing [sent. 1615 len. 52]: Swinton , who could speak the Dutch language , having consulted with Alexander and the Major , replied that it was very true that the army of Quetoo was to the northward ; but that the report of the defeat of the Caffres and of the army advancing was not confirmed .
Parsing [sent. 1616 len. 69]: It was only a rumor , and might all be false ; that even if true , it did not follow they were advancing in the direction in which they themselves were about to proceed ; that it would be sufficient time for them to retreat when they found out what were the real facts , which would be the case in a few days at the furthest .
Parsing [sent. 1617 len. 22]: But the Hottentots would not listen to any thing that he said ; they declared that they would proceed no further .
Parsing [sent. 1618 len. 34]: By this time all the other Hottentots had joined the first who came up to our travelers , and made the same demand , stating their determination not to proceed a mile further .
Parsing [sent. 1619 len. 23]: Only Bremen and Swanevelt opposed the rest , and declared that they would follow their masters wherever they chose to lead them .
Parsing [sent. 1620 len. 36]: Alexander now sent for the interpreter and the chief of the Caffre warriors , lent him by Hinza , and desired the interpreter to ask the Caffre whether he and his band would follow them .
Parsing [sent. 1621 len. 34]: The Caffre answered that they would ; Hinza had given them in charge , and they could not return and say that they had left them because there was an enemy at hand .
Parsing [sent. 1622 len. 25]: Hinza would kill them all if they did ; they must bring back the travelers safe , or lose their lives in their defense .
Parsing [sent. 1623 len. 48]: `` Well , then , '' said the Major , `` now we can do without these cowardly fellows , who are no use to us but to eat and drink ; so now let us discharge them at once , all but Bremen and Swanevelt . ''
Parsing [sent. 1624 len. 21]: `` I agree with you , Major , '' said Alexander ; `` what do you think , Swinton ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1625 len. 18]: `` Yes , let us discharge them , for then they will be in a precious dilemma .
Parsing [sent. 1626 len. 25]: We will discharge them without arms , and desire them to go home ; that they dare not do , so they will remain .
Parsing [sent. 1627 len. 38]: But let us first secure their muskets , which lie round their fire , before we dismiss them ; or they will not , perhaps , surrender them , and we may be in an awkward position .
Parsing [sent. 1628 len. 39]: I will slip away , and while I am away , do you keep them in talk until I return , which I shall not do until I have locked up all the guns in the store-wagon . ''
Parsing [sent. 1629 len. 10]: As Swinton rose , the Major addressed the Hottentots .
Parsing [sent. 1630 len. 41]: `` Now , my lads , '' said he , `` here are Bremen and Swanevelt who consent to follow us ; all the Caffre warriors agree to follow us ; and here are about twenty of you who refuse .
Parsing [sent. 1631 len. 44]: Now I can not think that you will leave us ; you know that we have treated you well , and have given you plenty of tobacco ; you know that you will be punished as soon as you return to the Cape .
Parsing [sent. 1632 len. 7]: Why then are you so foolish ?
Parsing [sent. 1633 len. 17]: Now look you : I am sure that upon reflection you will think better of it .
Parsing [sent. 1634 len. 28]: Let me understand clearly your reasons for not proceeding with us ; I wish to hear them again , and let each man speak for himself . ''
Parsing [sent. 1635 len. 39]: The Hottentots immediately began to state over again their reasons for not going on ; and thus the Major , who made each give his reason separately , gained their attention , and the time which was required .
Parsing [sent. 1636 len. 17]: Before they all had spoken , Swinton came back and took his seat by the fire .
Parsing [sent. 1637 len. 24]: `` All 's safe , '' said he ; `` Bremen and Swanevelt 's guns have been locked up with the others . ''
Parsing [sent. 1638 len. 9]: Our travelers had their own lying by them .
Parsing [sent. 1639 len. 35]: The Caffre warriors , who were standing behind the Hottentots , had all their assaguays in their hands ; but their shields , as usual , were hanging to the sides of the wagons .
Parsing [sent. 1640 len. 32]: The Major allowed the whole of the Hottentots to speak , and when they were done , he said , `` Now , Wilmot , turn the tables on them . ''
Parsing [sent. 1641 len. 85]: Alexander then got up with his gun in his hand , the Major and Swinton did the same , and then Alexander told the Hottentots that they were a cowardly set of fellows ; that with Bremen and Swanevelt , and the band of Caffre warriors , he could do without them ; that since they did not choose to proceed , they might now leave the camp immediately , as they should get neither food nor any thing else from them in future .
Parsing [sent. 1642 len. 44]: `` So now be off , the whole of you ; and if I find one to-morrow morning in sight of the camp , or if one of you dares to follow us , I will order the Caffres to run him through .
Parsing [sent. 1643 len. 12]: You are dismissed , and to-morrow we leave without you . ''
Parsing [sent. 1644 len. 57]: Alexander then called the chief of the Caffre warriors , and desired him , in the presence of the Hottentots , to give particular charge of the cattle , horses , and sheep , to his warriors during the night ; and if any one attempted to touch them , to run him through the body .
Parsing [sent. 1645 len. 30]: `` Do this immediately , '' said Alexander to the chief , who without delay spoke to his men , and they went off in obedience to his orders .
Parsing [sent. 1646 len. 51]: The Hottentots , who had heard all this , now retreated to their wagon , but were struck with consternation when they found that their guns had been removed ; for they trusted to their guns and ammunition to enable them to procure food and protect themselves on their return .
Parsing [sent. 1647 len. 49]: They consulted together in a low voice ; they looked round and perceived that our three travelers had quitted the fire , and were keeping guard with their guns upon the wagons , to prevent any attempt of breaking them open , on the part of the Hottentots .
Parsing [sent. 1648 len. 38]: Moreover , ten of the Caffres , with their spears , had since the breaking up of the conference , been put in charge of the wagons by the chief , at the request of the Major .
Parsing [sent. 1649 len. 8]: The Hottentots now perceived their forlorn position .
Parsing [sent. 1650 len. 25]: How could they , without arms and ammunition , and without provisions , return to the Cape , such a number of miles distant ?
Parsing [sent. 1651 len. 12]: How could they exist , if they remained where they were ?
Parsing [sent. 1652 len. 37]: When they insisted upon our travelers returning , they had quite overlooked the circumstance that these could protect themselves with the Caffre warriors , and that they were not in a condition to enforce their demand .
Parsing [sent. 1653 len. 29]: After a long conversation , they did what all Hottentots will do under any emergency , -- they lay down by the fire , and fell fast asleep .
Parsing [sent. 1654 len. 57]: Swinton , having ascertained that they were really asleep , proposed that they themselves should retire to the wagon , and leave the Caffres on guard , which they did ; as they well knew that a Hottentot once fast asleep is not easily roused up even to `` treason , stratagem , or spoil . ''
Parsing [sent. 1655 len. 89]: Shortly after break of day , Bremen came to them , stating that he found the wagons could proceed no further , as he had walked on , and discovered that a mile before them there was a ravine so deep that it would be difficult for the cattle to go down , and for the wagons impossible ; that at a distance of three miles below he could see the river , which was also so embedded in rocks , as to be impassable by the wagons .
Parsing [sent. 1656 len. 28]: The Major immediately went with Bremen , to satisfy himself of the truth of this , and returned , stating that further progress with wagons was impossible .
Parsing [sent. 1657 len. 16]: `` Well , then , we must now hold a council , '' said Swinton .
Parsing [sent. 1658 len. 47]: `` Of course , proceed you will , Wilmot , that is decided ; the only question is , as we must now proceed on horseback , what force you will take with you , and what shall be left in charge of the wagons ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1659 len. 14]: `` I think we can trust the Caffres , do not you ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1660 len. 45]: `` Yes , I do ; but I wish from my heart that the Hottentots had not rebelled ; for although in some respects cowardly fellows , yet with their muskets they are brave , and their muskets keep the natives in order . ''
Parsing [sent. 1661 len. 37]: `` To the Caffres , the contents of the wagons would prove a temptation ; but these are not temptations to the Hottentots , whose object is to get back safe , and receive their wages .
Parsing [sent. 1662 len. 10]: Thus we play them off against each other . ''
Parsing [sent. 1663 len. 45]: `` Here are all the Hottentots coming up to us , '' said the Major ; `` I hope it is to make submission ; it is very desirable that they should do so before they know that the wagons proceed no further . ''
Parsing [sent. 1664 len. 49]: The surmise of the Major was correct : the Hottentots had again canvassed the matter over , and , perceiving the helplessness of their position , had come in a body to beg forgiveness , and to offer to accompany our travelers wherever they pleased to take them .
Parsing [sent. 1665 len. 33]: It was a long while before Alexander would consent to receive them again , and not until they had made promise upon promise , that he seemed at last to be mollified .
Parsing [sent. 1666 len. 23]: Swinton then interceded for them , and at last Alexander consented , upon their future good behavior , to overlook their conduct .
Parsing [sent. 1667 len. 13]: This matter having been satisfactorily arranged , the former question was resumed .
Parsing [sent. 1668 len. 28]: `` One of you , I fear , must remain with the wagons , '' observed Alexander ; `` or both of you , if you please .
Parsing [sent. 1669 len. 22]: I have no right to ask you to go upon any wild-goose chase , and run into danger for nothing . ''
Parsing [sent. 1670 len. 35]: `` That one should remain with the wagons will be necessary , '' said Swinton ; `` and I think that the Major , if he does not object , is the proper person .
Parsing [sent. 1671 len. 33]: The party who are left must provide themselves with food by their guns ; and it will require more military tact than I possess to arrange that and to defend the wagons .
Parsing [sent. 1672 len. 26]: I will accompany you , Wilmot , as I can speak better Dutch , and the interpreter will not get on well without me . ''
Parsing [sent. 1673 len. 20]: `` Will you have the kindness to take charge of the wagons , Major , during our absence ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1674 len. 26]: `` I think , perhaps , it will be as well ; although I had rather have gone with you , '' replied the Major .
Parsing [sent. 1675 len. 28]: `` I propose that you take thirty of the Caffres , Bremen , and eight Hottentots with you ; leave me Swanevelt and the other Hottentots . ''
Parsing [sent. 1676 len. 20]: `` Yes , that will do very well ; we will leave the Caffre head man with you . ''
Parsing [sent. 1677 len. 23]: `` No ; he must go with the larger portion of his party ; he could not well be separated from them .
Parsing [sent. 1678 len. 37]: I will find a proper place for the wagons , and stockade myself regularly in ; that will be a good job for the Hottentots , and I dare say I shall do very well . ''
Parsing [sent. 1679 len. 46]: `` I shall not leave you Omrah , Major , '' said Swinton ; `` for , as we shall take four horses with us , I wish him to ride one , and he can attend upon us , as you have Mahomed . ''
Parsing [sent. 1680 len. 29]: `` You may have Begum to ride the other , '' replied the Major , `` if you please ; then you will each have a groom . ''
Parsing [sent. 1681 len. 69]: `` No , no , it would be a pity to part you and her ; however , there is no time to be lost , for if this great chief and warrior Quetoo is advancing , it may be as well to be ready for a retreat ; the sooner we are off , the sooner we shall be back ; so now to pack up . ''
Parsing [sent. 1682 len. 3]: CHAPTER XVI .
Parsing [sent. 1683 len. 64]: The first step taken by Alexander was to send for the Hottentots , and , after again reproving them for their former behavior , he asked who were ready to volunteer to proceed with him , as he had decided to leave the wagons with Major Henderson , and proceed on horseback the short distance of his journey which remained to be accomplished .
Parsing [sent. 1684 len. 43]: Several of the Hottentots immediately came forward ; the heads of the mutiny held back , and thus proved to Alexander that the men who had come forward were persuaded into it by the others , and regretted what they had done .
Parsing [sent. 1685 len. 15]: He therefore immediately accepted their services , and their muskets were returned to them .
Parsing [sent. 1686 len. 35]: Alexander then stated his intentions to the Caffre head man , who selected the thirty warriors that were required , and in the course of three hours every thing was ready for their departure .
Parsing [sent. 1687 len. 48]: It was arranged that in case of danger arising to either party , they should , if possible , fall back to the newly established Mission of Morley , on the sea-coast ; but otherwise , the wagons would remain where they were till Alexander 's return .
Parsing [sent. 1688 len. 61]: Having packed up all they required in small packages , to be carried by the Caffres , they bade farewell to the Major , and set off , having no baggage but what we have mentioned ; for Alexander would not be encumbered with a load of heavy articles which must prevent rapid progress , or rapid retreat if necessary .
Parsing [sent. 1689 len. 63]: In two hours they arrived by difficult passes at the banks of the Umtata River , which they crossed , and soon afterward falling in with a Caffre kraal , they were informed that Daaka , the chief whom they sought , did not reside more than twenty miles distant ; and they easily procured a guide to show them the way .
Parsing [sent. 1690 len. 28]: The reports of the advance of the Amaquibi army were here fully confirmed , and the natives were preparing to leave the kraal with all their cattle .
Parsing [sent. 1691 len. 30]: It appeared , however , that at present the army was stationary ; the warriors carousing and enjoying themselves after the victory which they had gained over the Caffres .
Parsing [sent. 1692 len. 56]: As these had been assisted by white men and their guns , the spirits of the Amaquibi were raised to an extraordinary degree , and they were intending to carry their arms to the southward , as soon as Quetoo , their chief , had somewhat recovered from his wounds received in the late action .
Parsing [sent. 1693 len. 26]: Indeed , it was the wounded state of their chief which was the principal cause of the army not having immediately proceeded to the southward .
Parsing [sent. 1694 len. 44]: Having obtained this information , the travelers resumed their journey along the banks of the Umtata , over a country of surprising beauty , the deep river being full of hippopotami , which were lying on the banks or snorting in the stream .
Parsing [sent. 1695 len. 35]: They could not wait to kill one during the daytime , but promised the men they would allow them to make the attempt in the evening , after their day 's march was over .
Parsing [sent. 1696 len. 50]: Toward sunset , they stopped on the banks of the river on a rising ground , and the Hottentots and some Caffres were then directed to go down to the river in chase of the hippopotami , as it was advisable to save their provisions as much as possible .
Parsing [sent. 1697 len. 17]: Before night they had succeeded , and the carcass of the animal was hauled on shore .
Parsing [sent. 1698 len. 24]: As soon as the party had taken as much as they required , the native Caffres carried off the remainder of the flesh .
Parsing [sent. 1699 len. 45]: As they were sitting down carousing by the fire which had been lighted , the Caffre head warrior came up to the interpreter , and told Alexander and Swinton not to say that they were Hinza 's warriors if asked where they came from .
Parsing [sent. 1700 len. 52]: On being asked why , he told them that Hinza had married a daughter of the chief of this country , and after a time had sent her back again to her father , and that this had created ill blood between the tribes , although no war had taken place .
Parsing [sent. 1701 len. 41]: Alexander and Swinton , who perceived that the advice was judicious , told him that they would not , and after partaking of the hippopotamus flesh they all lay down to repose under the far-spreading branches of a large tree .
Parsing [sent. 1702 len. 32]: The next morning they set off , and after an hour 's journey the guide told them that they were at the kraal of Daaka , the descendant of the Europeans .
Parsing [sent. 1703 len. 29]: The bellowing of the cattle and noise of the calves soon directed them to the spot , and they entered a kraal consisting of several very wretched huts .
Parsing [sent. 1704 len. 32]: On inquiring for Daaka , a woman pointed out a hut at a little distance , and , as they dismounted and walked up , he came out to meet them .
Parsing [sent. 1705 len. 32]: Swinton and Alexander shook hands with the chief , and said that they were very glad to see him , and that they had come far to pay him a visit .
Parsing [sent. 1706 len. 19]: The chief ordered a hut to be swept out for their accommodation , which they took possession of .
Parsing [sent. 1707 len. 24]: `` You have no idea , Swinton , '' said Alexander , `` how much I am excited already by this interview . ''
Parsing [sent. 1708 len. 50]: `` I can imagine it , my dear Wilmot , '' said Swinton ; `` it is but natural , for he is your kinsman by all report , and certainly , although a Caffre in his habits and manners , his countenance and features are strikingly European . ''
Parsing [sent. 1709 len. 20]: `` That I have observed myself , and it has fully convinced me of the truth of the statement .
Parsing [sent. 1710 len. 15]: I am most anxious to examine him -- we must call the interpreter . ''
Parsing [sent. 1711 len. 61]: The chief entered the hut soon afterward , and took his seat ; the interpreter was sent for , and the conversation was begun by Daaka , who like most of the Caffre chiefs , with the hope of obtaining presents , stated himself to be very poor , his cattle to be dying , and his children without milk .
Parsing [sent. 1712 len. 30]: Our travelers allowed him to go on for some time in this manner , and then sent for a present of beads and tobacco , which they gave him .
Parsing [sent. 1713 len. 22]: They then commenced their inquiries , and the first question they asked was , why he resided so near the sea .
Parsing [sent. 1714 len. 30]: `` Because the sea is my mother , '' replied he ; `` I came from the sea , and the sea feeds me when I am hungry . ''
Parsing [sent. 1715 len. 62]: `` In that reply he evidently refers to the wreck of the ship , '' observed Swinton ; `` and I presume , from the fish-bones , which we have seen about the kraal , that these Caffres feed on fish , which the other tribes do not , and therefore it is that he says his mother feeds him . ''
Parsing [sent. 1716 len. 7]: `` Was your mother white ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1717 len. 3]: inquired Alexander .
Parsing [sent. 1718 len. 36]: `` Yes , '' replied Daaka , `` her skin was white as yours ; her hair was just like yours , long and dark ; but before she died it was quite white . ''
Parsing [sent. 1719 len. 9]: `` What was your mother 's name ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1720 len. 8]: `` Kuma , '' replied the chief .
Parsing [sent. 1721 len. 9]: `` Had you any brothers and sisters ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1722 len. 14]: `` Yes , I had ; I have one sister alive now . ''
Parsing [sent. 1723 len. 7]: `` What is her name ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1724 len. 3]: inquired Swinton .
Parsing [sent. 1725 len. 8]: `` Bess , '' replied the chief .
Parsing [sent. 1726 len. 28]: `` This is very confirmatory , '' said Alexander ; `` my aunt 's name was Elizabeth ; she must have called her child after herself . ''
Parsing [sent. 1727 len. 8]: `` Whom did your mother marry ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1728 len. 38]: `` She first married my uncle , and had no children ; and then she married my father ; both were chiefs , and I am a chief ; she had five children by my father . ''
Parsing [sent. 1729 len. 7]: -LSB- Illustration : THE CAFFRE CHIEF .
Parsing [sent. 1730 len. 4]: P. 195 . -RSB-
Parsing [sent. 1731 len. 24]: A long conversation took place after this , the substance of which we may as well communicate to our reader in few words .
Parsing [sent. 1732 len. 59]: From the children of Kuma , supposed to be Elizabeth , the aunt of Alexander , were produced a numerous race of the European blood , who were celebrated in the Caffre land for their courage ; they were continually engaged in war , as their alliance was eagerly sought , and in consequence had nearly all perished .
Parsing [sent. 1733 len. 17]: Daaka himself was renowned for warlike exploits , but he was now a very old man .
Parsing [sent. 1734 len. 16]: In the evening the chief took his leave , and went to his own hut .
Parsing [sent. 1735 len. 42]: As soon as they were alone , Alexander said to Swinton , `` I have now so far fulfilled my promise to my worthy relation that I have seen this descendant of his child ; but what am I to do ?
Parsing [sent. 1736 len. 74]: An old man like him is not very likely to consent to go to England , and as for his sister Bess , he states that she is equally infirm ; the progeny of the rest of the family are scattered about , and he himself knows nothing about them ; to collect them would be impossible , and if collected , equally impossible to remove them , for they would not leave .
Parsing [sent. 1737 len. 93]: My old relative fancies , in his mind 's eye , his daughter weeping over her captivity , and longing to be restored to her country and her relations ; still retaining European feelings and sympathies , and miserable in her position ; her children brought up by her with the same ideas , and some day looking forward to their emancipation from this savage state of existence : I think if he were here , and saw old Daaka , he would soon divest himself of all these romantic ideas . ''
Parsing [sent. 1738 len. 41]: `` I think so too ; but there is one thing which has struck me very forcibly , Alexander , which is , if this Daaka is the son of your aunt how comes it that he is so old ?
Parsing [sent. 1739 len. 9]: When was the _ Grosvenor _ lost ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1740 len. 7]: `` In the year 1782 . ''
Parsing [sent. 1741 len. 8]: `` And we are now in 1829 .
Parsing [sent. 1742 len. 19]: Your aunt you stated to have been ten or twelve years old at the time of the wreck .
Parsing [sent. 1743 len. 29]: Allowing her to marry at the earliest age , Daaka could not well be more than forty-eight years old ; and surely he is more than that . ''
Parsing [sent. 1744 len. 44]: `` He looks much older , certainly ; but who can tell the age of a savage , who has been living a life of constant privation , and who has been so often wounded as his scars show that he has been ?
Parsing [sent. 1745 len. 12]: Wounds and hardship will soon make a man look old . ''
Parsing [sent. 1746 len. 21]: `` That is very true , but still he appears to me to be older than the dates warrant . ''
Parsing [sent. 1747 len. 16]: `` I think his stating that his sister was named Bess is full corroboration . ''
Parsing [sent. 1748 len. 18]: `` It is rather circumstantial evidence , Wilmot : now what do you propose to do ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1749 len. 53]: `` I hardly know ; but I wish to be in Daaka 's company some time longer , that I may gain more intelligence ; and I think of proposing to him that we should go down to visit the remains of the wreck of his mother , as he terms it .
Parsing [sent. 1750 len. 52]: I should like to see a spot so celebrated for misfortune , and behold the remains of the ill-fated vessel ; I should like to have to tell my good old uncle all I can , and he will wish that I should be able to give him every information . ''
Parsing [sent. 1751 len. 24]: `` Well , I think it is a good plan of yours , and we will propose it to him to-morrow morning . ''
Parsing [sent. 1752 len. 18]: `` And I should like to visit his sister Bess -- indeed , I must do so .
Parsing [sent. 1753 len. 11]: He says she is much younger than he is . ''
Parsing [sent. 1754 len. 40]: `` He did , and therefore I think his age does not correspond with our dates , as I observed before , '' replied Swinton ; `` but , as you say , you must see his sister . ''
Parsing [sent. 1755 len. 27]: Daaka had sent an old cow as a present to Alexander , which was a very seasonable supply , as the hippopotamus-flesh had all been eaten .
Parsing [sent. 1756 len. 20]: The next morning they proposed that he should accompany them to where the _ Grosvenor _ had been wrecked .
Parsing [sent. 1757 len. 36]: Daaka did not at first appear to know what they wished , and inquired , through the interpreter , whether they meant the ship that was wrecked on the sea-coast , pointing to the eastward .
Parsing [sent. 1758 len. 39]: On receiving an answer in the affirmative , he agreed to set off with them that afternoon , saying that it was about forty miles off , and that they could not get there until the next day .
Parsing [sent. 1759 len. 34]: About noon they set off on their journey , and as they made but slow progress over a rugged although most beautiful country , they stopped at night at a kraal about half-way .
Parsing [sent. 1760 len. 71]: Early the next morning they were led by Daaka and some Caffres who accompanied him to the sea-shore , and when they had arrived at the beach , it being then low water , Daaka pointed to a reef , upon which were to be seen the guns , ballast , and a portion of the keelson of a ship -- all that remained of the unfortunate _ Grosvenor _ .
Parsing [sent. 1761 len. 60]: As the sea washed over the reef ; now covering and now exposing these mementoes of misery and suffering , Alexander and Swinton remained for some time without speaking ; at last Alexander said -- `` Swinton , you have read the history of this unfortunate vessel , I know , for you asked me for it to read .
Parsing [sent. 1762 len. 36]: What a succession of scenes of horror do these remains , which from their solid weight only have defied the power of the winds and waves , conjure up at this moment in my mind .
Parsing [sent. 1763 len. 47]: I think I now behold the brave vessel dashed upon the reefs -- the scream of despair from all on board -- the heart-rending situation of the women and children -- their wonderful escape and landing on shore , only to be subjected to greater suffering .
Parsing [sent. 1764 len. 26]: See , Swinton , that must have been the rock which they all gained , and upon which they remained shivering through the night . ''
Parsing [sent. 1765 len. 17]: `` It is , I have no doubt , from its position , '' said Swinton .
Parsing [sent. 1766 len. 104]: `` Yes , it must have been ; I think I see them all -- men , women , and helpless children -- huddled together , half-clothed and suffering , quitting that rock by this only path from it , and setting off upon their mad and perilous journey ; the scattering of the parties -- their perils and hunger -- their conflicts with the natives -- their sufferings from heat and from thirst -- their sinking down one by one into the welcome arms of death , or torn to pieces by the wolves and hyenas as they lagged behind the others .
Parsing [sent. 1767 len. 12]: How much more fortunate those who never gained the shore . ''
Parsing [sent. 1768 len. 39]: `` Yes , indeed , '' replied Swinton ; `` except the eight who reached the Cape , and the five that Daaka asserts were saved , all the rest must have perished in that dreadful manner . ''
Parsing [sent. 1769 len. 37]: Alexander remained for some time in painful thought ; at last he turned to Daaka and said , as he pointed to the remains of the wreck , `` And this then is your mother ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1770 len. 35]: Daaka looked at him and shook his head , `` No , not my mother this , '' replied he ; `` my mother down there , '' pointing out in a northerly direction .
Parsing [sent. 1771 len. 8]: `` What does he mean , Swinton ?
Parsing [sent. 1772 len. 9]: he says this is not his mother . ''
Parsing [sent. 1773 len. 19]: `` I will speak to him , Wilmot ; you are too much agitated , '' replied Swinton .
Parsing [sent. 1774 len. 14]: `` Is not that the vessel which your mother was lost in ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1775 len. 7]: said Swinton , through the interpreter .
Parsing [sent. 1776 len. 45]: `` No , '' replied Daaka ; `` my mother came on shore in a vessel up the little river out there ; I was a boy when this large ship was wrecked ; and got some iron from her to make assaguays . ''
Parsing [sent. 1777 len. 4]: `` Merciful heaven !
Parsing [sent. 1778 len. 15]: what joy I feel ; I trust it is true what he says . ''
Parsing [sent. 1779 len. 33]: `` I have no doubt of it , Wilmot ; I told you he was too old a man , '' replied Swinton ; `` but let me question him further . ''
Parsing [sent. 1780 len. 47]: Our readers may imagine the impatience of Alexander while the questions of Swinton were being answered , and by which it appears that Daaka 's mother was lost at the mouth of the Lauwanbaz , a small river some miles to the eastward of the Zemsooboo .
Parsing [sent. 1781 len. 31]: An old Caffre , who had come down with Daaka , now gave a particular account of the wreck of the _ Grosvenor _ , corroborating all Daaka 's assertions .
Parsing [sent. 1782 len. 17]: `` Were there none of the _ Grosvenor 's _ people left in the country ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1783 len. 3]: inquired Swinton .
Parsing [sent. 1784 len. 18]: `` None , '' replied the old man ; `` they all went to the southward . ''
Parsing [sent. 1785 len. 10]: `` Did you hear what became of them ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1786 len. 31]: `` Some lay down and died , some fought the natives and were killed ; the wolves ate the rest ; not one left alive ; they all perished . ''
Parsing [sent. 1787 len. 15]: `` Were none of the women and children saved and kept as slaves ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1788 len. 20]: `` No , not one ; they had no meat , no milk , and they all died . ''
Parsing [sent. 1789 len. 57]: After some other inquiries , the old man , who at first did not reply willingly , stated that he had , with other Caffres , followed the last party ; had seen them all dead , and had taken off their clothes , and that as they died were buried by those who still survived .
Parsing [sent. 1790 len. 22]: `` A better fate , cruel as it was , than living as they must have lived , '' said Swinton .
Parsing [sent. 1791 len. 40]: `` Yes , truly , '' replied Alexander ; `` you do n't know , Swinton , what a load has been removed from my mind , and how light-hearted I feel , notwithstanding this recital of their sufferings .
Parsing [sent. 1792 len. 4]: My poor uncle !
Parsing [sent. 1793 len. 34]: God grant that he may live till my return with this distinct intelligence , with the assurance that he has no grandchildren living the life of a heathen , and knowing no God .
Parsing [sent. 1794 len. 19]: What a relief will it prove to him ; how soothing will it be to his last days !
Parsing [sent. 1795 len. 19]: How grateful am I to God , that I have had so happy an issue to my mission !
Parsing [sent. 1796 len. 46]: Now , Swinton , we will return as soon as you please ; as soon as we arrive at Daaka 's kraal , I will take down in writing the statement of these people , and then we will hasten back to the Major . ''
Parsing [sent. 1797 len. 50]: `` And I dare say , '' said Swinton , as he remounted his horse , `` that you will make old Daaka a more handsome present , for proving himself no relation to you , than if he had satisfactorily established himself as your own first cousin . ''
