Parsing [sent. 1799 len. 42]: I am so light hearted , Swinton , and so grateful to God that I almost wish to dismount in my anxiety to return my thanks ; but I do so in my heart of hearts , at all event . ''
Parsing [sent. 1800 len. 30]: On the following day they arrived at Daaka 's kraal , and then Alexander took down very carefully in writing the statements made by Daaka and the other Caffres .
Parsing [sent. 1801 len. 66]: They all agreed on the one point , which was , that the European descendants now living in the country were wrecked in another vessel many years before the loss of the _ Grosvenor _ , and that not one of the _ Grosvenor 's _ people -- men , women or children -- had survived , except the few who arrived at the Cape .
Parsing [sent. 1802 len. 29]: Having obtained these satisfactory documents , they made a handsome present to Daaka and the other Caffres , and immediately set out upon their return to the wagons .
Parsing [sent. 1803 len. 52]: As they journeyed back to the westward , they found the Caffres quitting their huts , and driving away the cattle , that they might not fall into the power of the army of Quetoo , which it was said was now in motion , and scattering the tribes before them .
Parsing [sent. 1804 len. 36]: As our travelers were not at all anxious to have any communication with these savage invaders , in two days they crossed the Umtata , and toward the evening were within sight of the wagons .
Parsing [sent. 1805 len. 13]: A shout from the Hottentots and Caffres gave notice of their approach .
Parsing [sent. 1806 len. 25]: The shout was returned , and in a few minutes they were shaking hands with the Major , who was delighted to see them .
Parsing [sent. 1807 len. 38]: `` I did not expect you back so soon , '' replied the Major ; `` and as I perceive that you are unaccompanied , I presume that your Caffre relations would not quit their kraals . ''
Parsing [sent. 1808 len. 32]: `` You shall know all about it , Major , very soon ; it will be enough at present to let you know that we have nothing but good news . ''
Parsing [sent. 1809 len. 49]: `` That I rejoice to hear ; but it was well you came back as you did , for I have been making every preparation , and had you not returned in a few days , I should have retreated ; the invaders are close at hand . ''
Parsing [sent. 1810 len. 51]: `` We know it , and , if they are told that there are wagons here well loaded , they will come on quickly , with the hopes of plunder , so we must delay no longer , '' replied Alexander ; `` to-morrow we will yoke and set off .
Parsing [sent. 1811 len. 23]: We can determine upon our route as we are traveling , but the first point is to retreat from this quarter . ''
Parsing [sent. 1812 len. 50]: `` Exactly ; the oxen are in prime order and can make a long day 's march , and we know our country for some days , at all events ; but enter my fortress , dismount , and let us go into the tent which I have pitched .
Parsing [sent. 1813 len. 22]: You shall then tell me your adventures , while Mahomed fries a delicate piece of elephant 's flesh for you . ''
Parsing [sent. 1814 len. 8]: `` Have you killed an elephant ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1815 len. 76]: `` Yes , but not without much difficulty and some danger , I assure you ; I wanted your help sadly , for these Hottentots are too much alarmed to take good aim , and I had only my own rifle to trust to ; but I have done very well considering , and I shall prove to our commander-in-chief that I have supplied the garrison without putting him to any expense during his absence .
Parsing [sent. 1816 len. 30]: We have been feeding upon green monkeys for three days , and very good eating they are , if you do not happen upon a very old one . ''
Parsing [sent. 1817 len. 25]: When they entered the inclosure made by the Major , they were surprised at the state of defense in which he had put it .
Parsing [sent. 1818 len. 44]: His hedge of thorns upon rocks piled up was impregnable , and the wagons were in the center , drawn up in a square ; the entrance would only admit one person at a time , and was protected by bars at night .
Parsing [sent. 1819 len. 22]: `` Why , Major , you might have held out against the whole force of the Amaquibi in this position . ''
Parsing [sent. 1820 len. 58]: `` Yes , provided I had provisions and water , '' replied the Major ; `` but I fear they would soon have starved me out ; however , it was as well to be prepared against any sudden night-attack , and therefore I fortified my camp : now come in , and welcome back again . ''
Parsing [sent. 1821 len. 42]: The news which they had to impart to the Major was soon given , and he was highly delighted at the intelligence : -- `` And now , '' said he , `` what do you mean to do , Wilmot ?
Parsing [sent. 1822 len. 14]: -- go back again , of course , but by what route ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1823 len. 41]: `` Why , Major , you and Swinton have been so kind in coming with me thus far , and I have been so successful in my expedition , that I shall now leave you to decide as you please .
Parsing [sent. 1824 len. 48]: I have effected all that I wished , my business is over , and I am ready to meet you in any way you choose ; any thing you decide upon I shall agree to willingly and join in heartily , so now speak your wishes . ''
Parsing [sent. 1825 len. 15]: `` Well , I will speak mine very frankly , '' replied the Major .
Parsing [sent. 1826 len. 38]: `` We have had some sport in this country , it is true , but not so much as I could have wished ; for game is rather scarce , with the exception of elephants and sea-cows .
Parsing [sent. 1827 len. 58]: Now I should like to cross the mountains , and get into the Bechuana and Bushman country , where game is as plentiful as I believe water is scarce ; we can return that way , if you please , almost as well as we can through the Caffre country -- what say you , Swinton ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1828 len. 9]: `` Well , I am of your opinion .
Parsing [sent. 1829 len. 79]: As Wilmot says , business is over and we have nothing to do but to amuse ourselves ; I am very anxious to pass through this country , as I shall add greatly to my collections , I have no doubt ; but it must not be expected that we shall fare as well as we have done in this ; it will be the dry season , and we may be in want of water occasionally . ''
Parsing [sent. 1830 len. 58]: `` I am equally desirous of going through that country , where I hope to shoot a giraffe , -- that is my great ambition , '' replied Wilmot ; `` therefore we may consider that we are all agreed , and the affair is settled ; but the question is , how shall we proceed back ?

Parsing [sent. 1834 len. 48]: `` I wonder if the quarrel between Hinza and Voosani has been made up , '' said Alexander ; `` for we must pass through the Tambookie tribe if we cross the mountains , and if there is war between them we may meet with difficulty . ''

Parsing [sent. 1836 len. 30]: All that can be settled now is , that to-morrow we start on our return , and that we will cross the mountains , if we possibly can . ''
Parsing [sent. 1837 len. 11]: `` Yes , that is decided , '' replied Alexander .
Parsing [sent. 1838 len. 57]: `` Well , then , as soon as you have finished your elephant-steak , Wilmot , we will get out a bottle of wine , drink the first half of it to congratulate you upon the success of your mission , and the other half shall be poured out in bumpers to a happy return . ''
Parsing [sent. 1839 len. 3]: CHAPTER XVII .
Parsing [sent. 1840 len. 3]: THE RETURN .
Parsing [sent. 1841 len. 43]: The delight of the Hottentots at the announcement of the return of the expedition was not to be concealed ; and now that they knew that they were retreating from the danger , as they were further removed they became proportionately brave .
Parsing [sent. 1842 len. 69]: We must not include all the Hottentots in this observation , as Bremen , Swanevelt , and one or two more , were really brave men ; but we do refer to the principal portion of them , with Big Adam at their head , who now flourished and vapored about , as if he could by himself kill and eat the whole army of the dreaded Quetoo .
Parsing [sent. 1843 len. 76]: As it was the intention of our travelers to pass over the Mambookei chain of mountains , into the Bushman and Koranna territory , they did not return the same route by which they came , but more to the westward through the territory of the Tambookie Caffres , not any one time entering upon the territory of the Amakosas , the tribe of Caffres governed by Hinza , who had lent them his warriors .
Parsing [sent. 1844 len. 30]: Voosani , the chief of the Tambookies , was very friendly , and had offered no opposition to their passage through a portion of his domains on their advance .
Parsing [sent. 1845 len. 41]: They now lost no time , but continued their journey as fast as they could , although during the day they saw a great quantity of game , and were almost every night saluted with the roaring of the lions .
Parsing [sent. 1846 len. 30]: In a week they found themselves on the banks of the White Kae River , and not far from the foot of the mountains which they intended to pass .
Parsing [sent. 1847 len. 49]: Here they halted , with the intention of remaining some few days , that they might unload and re-arrange the packing of their wagons , repair what was necessary , and provide themselves with more oxen and sheep for their journey in the sterile territory of the Bushmen .
Parsing [sent. 1848 len. 15]: During their route , the rumors relative to the army of Quetoo were incessant .
Parsing [sent. 1849 len. 30]: He had attacked and murdered Lieut. Farewell and his people , who were on a trading expedition in the interior , and taken possession of and plundered their wagons .
Parsing [sent. 1850 len. 57]: Flushed with success over white people armed with muskets , Quetoo had now resolved to turn his army to the southward , and attack the tribes of the Amaponda Caffres , governed by Fakoo , and the missionary station of Morley , lately established near the coast , between the St. John and the Umtata rivers .
Parsing [sent. 1851 len. 60]: To effect this , Quetoo commenced his ravages upon all the lesser tribes tributary to Fakoo , and having put them to indiscriminate slaughter , driven away their cattle , and burned their kraals , his army advanced to the missionary station , which the missionaries were compelled to desert , and fall back upon the St. John River .
Parsing [sent. 1852 len. 57]: One of the men belonging to the tribe near Morley came to the caravan where our travelers had halted , and , on being questioned as to the loss they had experienced , cried out , `` Ask not how many are killed , but how many are saved : our wives , where are they ?
Parsing [sent. 1853 len. 12]: and our children , do you see any of them ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1854 len. 17]: But Fakoo , the chief of the Amapondas , had roused himself and collected his army .
Parsing [sent. 1855 len. 9]: He resolved upon giving battle to the enemy .
Parsing [sent. 1856 len. 60]: He found the Amaquibi encamped in a forest , and he surrounded them with a superior army ; he then contrived , by attacking and retreating , to lead them into a position from which there was no escape but by the pass by which they had entered , and which he completely blocked up with his own forces .
Parsing [sent. 1857 len. 26]: The Amaquibi could not retreat , and a furious conflict took place , which ended in the destruction of the whole of Quetoo 's army .
Parsing [sent. 1858 len. 29]: Quetoo himself was not present , as he still remained confined with the wound he had received in the prior engagement , in which he had been victorious .
Parsing [sent. 1859 len. 44]: A portion of Fakoo 's army was sent against him , and he fled with the loss of all the cattle and treasures he had collected ; and thus was the invading force at last totally dispersed and not heard of any more .
Parsing [sent. 1860 len. 84]: This news was very satisfactory to our travelers , as they did not know whether they would have had time to make their arrangements , if Quetoo 's army had been victorious ; and it was still more pleasing to the Hottentots , who were now even braver than before , all lamenting that they had not remained on the banks of the Umtata River , where the combat took place , that they might have assisted at the destruction of the invaders .
Parsing [sent. 1861 len. 21]: It was toward the end of August before our travelers had made their preparations and were ready for a start .
Parsing [sent. 1862 len. 104]: They had decided to try the pass through the Mambookei chain of mountains , to the eastward of the one named Stormbergen , and as they expected to meet with some difficulties , it was decided that the Caffre warriors should not be dismissed till they had arrived at the Bushman territory ; they proposed then to turn to the N.W. , so as to fall in with that portion of the Orange River which was known by the name of the Vaal or Yellow River , crossing the Black or Cradock River , which is also another branch of the Orange River .
Parsing [sent. 1863 len. 37]: This arrangement was made , that they might get into the country more abounding with game , and better furnished with water than any other portion of the sterile deserts which they had to pass through .
Parsing [sent. 1864 len. 30]: Having , as usual , kept holy the Lord 's day , on the Monday morning they started in high spirits , and with their cattle in excellent order .
Parsing [sent. 1865 len. 31]: The passage through the ravine was very difficult ; they had to fill up holes , roll away stones , and very often put double teams to drag the wagons .
Parsing [sent. 1866 len. 26]: They made but ten miles on the first day , and found the night cold , after the heat to which they had been subjected .
Parsing [sent. 1867 len. 34]: The second day was also one of toil and danger , but on the third they found that they had commenced the descent , and the whole Bushman country was spread before them .
Parsing [sent. 1868 len. 29]: But the descent was even more perilous than the ascent , and it was not without great exertion that they saved their wagons from falling over the precipices .
Parsing [sent. 1869 len. 23]: On the fourth evening they had crossed the mountains , and were now at the foot of them on the western side .
Parsing [sent. 1870 len. 37]: It was with difficulty that they collected wood enough to make their fires for the night , and the continual roaring told them that they were now in the domain of the lion and his satellites .
Parsing [sent. 1871 len. 21]: At break of day they all rose , that they might view the country which they were about to traverse .
Parsing [sent. 1872 len. 44]: It was one wild desert of sand and stones , interspersed with small shrubs , and here and there a patch of bushes ; apparently one vast , dry , arid plain , with a haze over it , arising from the heat .
Parsing [sent. 1873 len. 54]: Our travelers , however , did not at first notice this change ; their eyes were fixed upon the groups of quaggas and various antelopes which were strewed over the whole face of the country ; and , as soon as they had taken their breakfast , they mounted their horses in pursuit .
Parsing [sent. 1874 len. 60]: It had been their intention to have dismissed the Caffres on that morning , but the chief of the band pointed out that it would be as well that they should kill some game , to provide them with food for their journey back ; and our travelers approved of the suggestion , as it would save their sheep .
Parsing [sent. 1875 len. 27]: Alexander and the Major set off with Bremen , Swanevelt , and Omrah on horseback , while the Caffres on foot kept well up with them .
Parsing [sent. 1876 len. 29]: The other Hottentots were ordered to remain with Swinton at the encampment , as they had to repair the damages done to the wagons in crossing the mountains .
Parsing [sent. 1877 len. 27]: Omrah had shown himself so useful , that he had been permitted to practice with a fowling-piece carrying ball , and had proved himself very expert .
Parsing [sent. 1878 len. 35]: He now was mounted on the Major 's spare horse ; that in case the Major 's was knocked up , he might change it , for Omrah 's weight was a mere nothing .
Parsing [sent. 1879 len. 39]: The plan of the chase was , that the Caffres should spread in a half-circle , and conceal themselves as much as possible , while those on horseback should turn the animals and drive them in their direction .
Parsing [sent. 1880 len. 59]: As they advanced on the plain , they discovered what the haze had prevented their seeing at early dawn , that the plain was covered with a variety of beautiful flowers , of the amaryllis and other tribes , and with the hills of ants and ant-eaters ' holes , which latter were very dangerous to the horses .
Parsing [sent. 1881 len. 25]: The sun was now up in the heavens , and blazed fiercely ; the heat was intense , although still early in the day .
Parsing [sent. 1882 len. 60]: When they turned their heads toward the mountains which they had passed , they were struck with astonishment at the grandeur of the scene : rocks and cliffs in wild chaos , barren ridges and towering peaks , worn by time into castellated fortresses and other strange shapes , calling to their fancy the ruins of a former world .
Parsing [sent. 1883 len. 68]: With the exception of a pool of water , near to which the caravan had halted , not a vestige of that element was to be seen in any direction ; all was one plain , ending only in the horizon , without a tree , the line only broken by the groups of animals and the long necks of the packs of ostriches in the distance .
Parsing [sent. 1884 len. 43]: If , however , the vegetable kingdom was deficient , the animal was proportionably abundant , and Alexander and the Major were soon at their speed after a troop of quaggas and zebras , which they succeeded in turning toward the Caffres .
Parsing [sent. 1885 len. 53]: As soon as the animals had entered the radius of the half-circle , and were within distance , they checked their horses and opened their fire upon them ; at the same time the Caffres showed themselves , and the animals were for a time confounded by finding themselves so nearly surrounded .
Parsing [sent. 1886 len. 89]: During their hesitation , and while they attempted to break through here and there , and then turned again , several were brought to the ground by the guns of the mounted party , till at last , as if they had summoned up their resolution , the whole herd , led by a splendid male , burst away in a direction close to the horsemen , and made their escape from the circle in a cloud of dust , scattering the stones behind them as they fled .
Parsing [sent. 1887 len. 34]: The Caffres ran up to the animals which lay wounded , and put them out of their misery by inserting the point of their assaguays into the spine , which caused immediate death .
Parsing [sent. 1888 len. 46]: Seven animals were killed , three zebras and four quaggas ; and as Swinton had requested that they might not be cut up till he had ascertained if he required their skins , Omrah was sent back to bring him to where they were lying .
Parsing [sent. 1889 len. 32]: Swinton soon came , and Alexander said to him , `` Now , Swinton , let us know if you want any of the skins of these animals to preserve . ''
Parsing [sent. 1890 len. 27]: `` No , '' replied Swinton , `` I have them already ; I just thought it possible that you might have killed a zebra . ''
Parsing [sent. 1891 len. 7]: `` Well , have we not ?
Parsing [sent. 1892 len. 7]: there are three of them . ''
Parsing [sent. 1893 len. 35]: `` No , my good fellow , they are not of the real zebra species ; they belong to a class described by Burchell , the traveler , which is termed the striped quagga .
Parsing [sent. 1894 len. 27]: The quagga and striped quagga , as you may see , have the ears of a horse , while the zebra has those of the ass .
Parsing [sent. 1895 len. 40]: The true zebra hardly ever descends upon the plains , but lives altogether upon the mountainous regions ; occasionally it may be found , it is true , and that is the reason why I came to see . ''
Parsing [sent. 1896 len. 10]: `` Are they good eating , these animals ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1897 len. 36]: `` The quagga is very indifferent food , but the striped quagga is very passable ; so if you intend to save any for our dinner , pray let it be some of the latter .
Parsing [sent. 1898 len. 7]: Have you done hunting to-day ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1899 len. 50]: `` Yes , '' replied the Major , `` if Wilmot is of my opinion , I think we had better not work our horses any more just now ; the plain is so full of large holes , -- ant-eaters ' holes , Bremen says they are . ''
Parsing [sent. 1900 len. 21]: `` Yes , they are ant-eaters ' holes , and very dangerous ; I have seen them several feet deep .
Parsing [sent. 1901 len. 31]: If we do not start to-day , I will ask the Hottentots to try and procure one for me to-night , as I wish to have a stuffed specimen . ''
Parsing [sent. 1902 len. 34]: `` We do not intend to start till to-morrow morning , '' replied Alexander ; `` we must dismiss the Caffres to-night , that they may be also ready to go home to-morrow .
Parsing [sent. 1903 len. 8]: They will now have provisions enough . ''
Parsing [sent. 1904 len. 18]: Our travelers now rode back to the caravan , leaving the Caffres to bring home the flesh .
Parsing [sent. 1905 len. 43]: As soon as they had dined , the chief of the warriors was desired to come with all his men , and Alexander then made every man a handsome present , consisting of tobacco , snuff , cloth , knives and beads .
Parsing [sent. 1906 len. 85]: To the chief of the band he gave three times as much as the others , and then , having delivered to him a very liberal collection of articles for their king Hinza , Alexander told the chief to acquaint the king that he had been very much pleased with the conduct of the men , and thanked his majesty for the loan of them , and requested that his majesty would accept of the packet of articles which he had selected for him .
Parsing [sent. 1907 len. 45]: He then thanked the men for their good conduct , told them to take all the flesh that they wished for the journey , and stated that they were at liberty to depart that evening or the next morning , as they thought proper .
Parsing [sent. 1908 len. 29]: The Caffres were perfectly satisfied with Alexander 's liberality , and the chief of the warriors , making a short speech in reply , retired with his men .
Parsing [sent. 1909 len. 19]: `` Well , I 'm very sorry that these fine fellows are leaving , '' said the Major .
Parsing [sent. 1910 len. 35]: `` And so am I ; but I could not well detain them , and they said that they could not go further with us without the king 's permission , '' replied Alexander .
Parsing [sent. 1911 len. 52]: `` Of course not , '' replied the Major ; `` but that does not lessen my regret at their departure ; they have been both steady and brave , as well as active and willing , and I do not expect that our Hottentots will serve us so well . ''
Parsing [sent. 1912 len. 26]: `` You are right not to expect it , Major , '' replied Swinton ; `` if you did , you would be miserably disappointed .
Parsing [sent. 1913 len. 14]: If they knew now where we were going , they would desert us .
Parsing [sent. 1914 len. 57]: The only hold that we have upon the greater number of them is their fear ; they go forward because they are afraid to go back ; but if they could get hold of our horses , with their guns and ammunition , they would leave us as soon as we advanced in the desert . ''
Parsing [sent. 1915 len. 32]: `` Very true , I fear ; but we have a few stanch fellows among them , and two at least whom we can depend upon -- Bremen and Swanevelt . ''
Parsing [sent. 1916 len. 15]: `` How far is it from here to the Black River , Swinton ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1917 len. 40]: `` About forty miles ; not so much perhaps to the river 's bed , but at least that , if not more , before we shall fall in with any water at this season of the year . ''
Parsing [sent. 1918 len. 15]: `` We must not fail to fill our water-kegs before we leave this . ''
Parsing [sent. 1919 len. 15]: `` No , for we shall have no water to-night , that is certain .
Parsing [sent. 1920 len. 71]: We can not travel more than twenty miles over such a country as this ; for turning here and there to avoid the holes and ant-hills , the twenty miles will be at least thirty , '' said Swinton ; `` but now I must go and tell the Hottentots to find me what I want : a pound of tobacco will procure it , I have no doubt . ''
Parsing [sent. 1921 len. 48]: `` But I have mine , '' observed the Major , after Swinton was gone ; `` we are too near the pool , and we shall be surrounded with lions to-night ; the Hottentots may pretend that they will go , but they will not . ''
Parsing [sent. 1922 len. 83]: `` One can not well blame them ; I 'm sure a pound of tobacco would not persuade me to put my head into a lion 's mouth ; but I agree with you , we are too near the pool , and as we must collect the cattle to secure them during the night , I think we had better fill our water-kegs , and then yoke and take up a position for the night about half a mile further off .
Parsing [sent. 1923 len. 13]: But here comes Swinton , who can give us his advice . ''
Parsing [sent. 1924 len. 36]: As Swinton agreed with them , they yoked the oxen , and drove forward about a mile from the pool ; they then secured them to the wagons and lighted large fires round the caravan .
Parsing [sent. 1925 len. 41]: The Major was correct as regarded the Hottentots ' procuring an ant-eater for Swinton ; they would not leave the fires , and the continual approach of the lions during the night proved that they were wise in so doing .
Parsing [sent. 1926 len. 44]: There was no occasion for the lions to roar ; the moaning of Begum , and her clinging to the Major , the trembling of the dogs , and the uneasiness of the cattle , invariably gave notice of lions being at hand .
Parsing [sent. 1927 len. 23]: Shots were fired off during the night , to keep them at a distance , but otherwise the night passed away undisturbed .
Parsing [sent. 1928 len. 25]: They started the following morning about daybreak , and , at the same time , the Caffres took their departure to their own country .
Parsing [sent. 1929 len. 35]: The ground over which the caravan traveled was stony and sandy at intervals , and they had not proceeded far before they again discovered a great variety of game dispersed over the level plain .
Parsing [sent. 1930 len. 63]: They did not , however , attempt to pursue them , as they were anxious to go on as far as possible , so as to give the oxen an opportunity of picking up what little food they could during the middle of the day , at which time the Major and Alexander proposed that they should go in pursuit of game .
Parsing [sent. 1931 len. 29]: But before they had traveled three hours , they were surprised at a cloud of dust , which obscured the horizon , in the direction they were proceeding .
Parsing [sent. 1932 len. 7]: `` What can that be ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1933 len. 3]: said Alexander .
Parsing [sent. 1934 len. 13]: `` I think it is springbok , '' said Bremen the Hottentot .
Parsing [sent. 1935 len. 3]: `` Springbok !
Parsing [sent. 1936 len. 12]: why , there must be thousands and thousands of them . ''
Parsing [sent. 1937 len. 40]: `` I believe that Bremen is right , '' said Swinton ; `` it must be one of the migratory herds of springboks ; I have never seen them , but I have often been told of them . ''
Parsing [sent. 1938 len. 38]: The body of antelopes now advanced toward them , keeping on a straight path ; and to state their numbers would have been impossible : there might have been fifty or a hundred thousand , or more .
Parsing [sent. 1939 len. 21]: As far as the eye could see in any direction , it was one moving mass covering the whole plain .
Parsing [sent. 1940 len. 61]: As they approached the caravan , those nearest huddled on one side and occasionally bounded away with the remarkable springs made by this animal , and from which it has its name , alighting not upon the earth , but , for want of room , upon the backs of its companions , and then dropping in between the ranks .
Parsing [sent. 1941 len. 55]: A hazy vapor arose from these countless herds as they moved on , and more than once the Hottentots , who were standing on the wagons , which had been stopped as the herd came up to them , pointed out a lion which was journeying with the crowds to feast at his leisure .
Parsing [sent. 1942 len. 24]: The animals appeared very tame , and several were killed close to the wheels of the wagons , for the evening 's supper .
Parsing [sent. 1943 len. 23]: Notwithstanding that the herd moved at a rapid pace , it was more than two hours before the whole had passed by .
Parsing [sent. 1944 len. 23]: `` Well , '' observed Alexander , `` I can now say that I have seen no want of game in Africa .
Parsing [sent. 1945 len. 7]: Where will they go to ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1946 len. 34]: `` They will go directly on to the southward , '' replied Swinton ; `` the migration of these animals is one of the most remarkable proofs of the fecundity of animal life .
Parsing [sent. 1947 len. 36]: Like the ants , they devour every thing before them ; and if we journey in the direction they have come from , we shall find no food for the cattle until after the rains .
Parsing [sent. 1948 len. 14]: After the rains fall , these animals will return to their former pastures .
Parsing [sent. 1949 len. 17]: It is the want of food which has brought them so far to the southward . ''
Parsing [sent. 1950 len. 30]: `` Their track is evidently from the north and eastward , '' said the Major ; `` had we not better change our course more to the northward ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1951 len. 23]: `` No , I should think not ; they have probably traveled on this side of the Nu Gariep or Black River .
Parsing [sent. 1952 len. 41]: We shall have neither water nor food for the cattle to-night , and therefore I think we had better go on as we are going , so as to make sure of water for them to-morrow , at all events .
Parsing [sent. 1953 len. 35]: It 's useless now stopping to feed the cattle , we had better continue right on till the evening ; we shall sooner arrive at the river , and so gain by it . ''
Parsing [sent. 1954 len. 15]: It was but half an hour before dark that they unyoked the tired oxen .
Parsing [sent. 1955 len. 33]: Water or grass there was none ; and , what was another misfortune , they could not find sufficient wood of any kind to keep up the necessary fires during the night .
Parsing [sent. 1956 len. 28]: All they could collect before dark was but enough for one fire , and they considered it better , therefore , that only one should be lighted .
Parsing [sent. 1957 len. 34]: The wagons were drawn up so as to form a square , inside of which were tied the horses ; the sheep were driven underneath , and the oxen were tied up outside .
Parsing [sent. 1958 len. 28]: They feasted well themselves upon the delicate meat of the springboks , but the poor animals had neither food nor water after their hard day 's journey .
Parsing [sent. 1959 len. 67]: As soon as they had supped they retired to their wagons , and the Hottentots remained by the side of the fire , which was but frugally supplied , that it might last till morning ; but that there were lions prowling in the vicinity was evident from the restlessness of the oxen , who tried to break the leathern thongs with which they were fastened .
Parsing [sent. 1960 len. 26]: The moon had just risen , and showed an imperfect light , when they perceived the bodies of some animals between them and the horizon .
Parsing [sent. 1961 len. 32]: They appeared very large , as they always do in an imperfect light , and the Hottentots soon made out that they were five or six lions not forty yards distant .
Parsing [sent. 1962 len. 35]: The truth of this supposition was confirmed by an angry roar from one of them , which induced most of the Hottentots to seize their guns , and some to creep under the wagons .
Parsing [sent. 1963 len. 19]: The oxen now struggled furiously to escape , for the , roar of the lions had spread consternation .
Parsing [sent. 1964 len. 18]: Our travelers heard it in their wagons , and were out with their guns in a minute .
Parsing [sent. 1965 len. 59]: At last one of the oxen broke loose , and , as it was running behind its companions , as if seeking a more secure shelter , being not more than three or four yards from them , another roar was followed by a spring of one of the lions , which bore the animal to the earth .
Parsing [sent. 1966 len. 32]: The Major and Wilmot were advancing before the fire to the attack , when the animal for a moment let go his prey , and was about to spring upon them .
Parsing [sent. 1967 len. 22]: Bremen called out for them to retreat , which they did , as the animal advanced step by step toward them .
Parsing [sent. 1968 len. 66]: Satisfied with their retiring , the lion then went to his prey , and dragged it to a distance of about fifty yards , where it commenced its meal ; and they distinctly heard , although they could not plainly distinguish , the tearing of the animal 's flesh and the breaking of its bones by the lion , while its bellowings were most pitiful .
Parsing [sent. 1969 len. 41]: They all now fired in the direction where they heard the noise ; the lion replied to the volley by a tremendous roar , and rushed up within twenty yards of the wagons , so as to be distinctly visible .
Parsing [sent. 1970 len. 37]: Bremen begged our travelers not to molest the animal , as it was evidently very hungry and very angry , and would certainly make a spring upon them , which must be attended with disastrous effects .
Parsing [sent. 1971 len. 33]: The other lions were also now moving round and round the camp ; they therefore reloaded their guns , and remained still , looking at the lion tearing and devouring his prey .
Parsing [sent. 1972 len. 39]: `` We must be quiet here , '' said Bremen to Alexander ; `` there are many lions round us , and our fire is not sufficient to scare them away , and they may attack us . ''
Parsing [sent. 1973 len. 18]: `` Would it not be better to fire our guns , -- that would frighten them ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1974 len. 33]: `` Yes , sir , it would frighten the other lions , perhaps , but it would enrage this one so near to us , and he would certainly make a charge .
Parsing [sent. 1975 len. 33]: We had better throw a little gunpowder upon some ashes now and then , as we have but a small fire : the flash will drive them away for the time . ''
Parsing [sent. 1976 len. 63]: In the mean time the lion was making his meal upon the poor ox , and when any other of the hungry lions approached him , he would rush at them , and pursue them for some paces with a horrible growl , which made not only the poor oxen , but the men also , to shudder as they heard it .
Parsing [sent. 1977 len. 38]: In this manner was the night passed away , every one with his gun in his hand , expecting an immediate attack ; but the morning at last dawned , to the great relief of them all .
Parsing [sent. 1978 len. 71]: The lions had disappeared , and they walked out to where the old lion had made his meal , and found that he had devoured nearly the whole of the ox ; and such was the enormous strength of his jaws , that the rib-bones were all demolished , and the bones of the legs , which are known as the marrow-bones , were broken as if by a hammer .
Parsing [sent. 1979 len. 28]: `` I really , '' observed the Major , `` have more respect for a lion , the more I become intimate with his feline majesty . ''
Parsing [sent. 1980 len. 24]: `` Well , but he is off , '' observed Swinton , `` and I think we had better be off too . ''
Parsing [sent. 1981 len. 3]: CHAPTER XVIII .
Parsing [sent. 1982 len. 18]: The oxen were yoked , and the caravan proceeded at slow pace to gain the wished-for river .
Parsing [sent. 1983 len. 74]: As our travelers walked their horses -- for the poor animals had been without food or water for twenty-four hours , and all idea of chasing the various herds of animals which were to be seen in their path was abandoned for the present -- Swinton remarked , `` We are not far from the track of the Mantatees , when they made their irruption upon the Caffres about eighteen months back . ''
Parsing [sent. 1984 len. 16]: `` I was intending to ask you for some information on that point , Swinton .
Parsing [sent. 1985 len. 17]: There has been more than one irruption into the country from the natives to the northward .
Parsing [sent. 1986 len. 37]: Mr. Fairburn gave me a very fair idea of the history of the Cape colony , but we were both too much engaged after our arrival in Cape Town for me to obtain further information . ''
Parsing [sent. 1987 len. 33]: `` I will , you may be assured , tell you all I know , '' replied Swinton ; `` but you must not expect to find in me a Mr. Fairburn .
Parsing [sent. 1988 len. 54]: I may as well remark , that Africa appears to be a country not able to afford support to a dense population , like Europe ; and the chief cause of this is the great want of water , occasionally rendered more trying by droughts of four or five years ' continuance . ''
Parsing [sent. 1989 len. 54]: `` I grant that such is the case at present , '' observed the Major ; `` but you well know that it is not that there is not a sufficient quantity of rain , which falls generally once a year , but because the water which falls is carried off so quickly .
Parsing [sent. 1990 len. 32]: Rivers become torrents , and in a few weeks pour all their water into the sea , leaving , I may say , none for the remainder of the year . ''
Parsing [sent. 1991 len. 9]: `` That is true , '' replied Swinton .
Parsing [sent. 1992 len. 25]: `` And so it will be until the population is not only dense , but , I may add , sufficiently enlightened and industrious .
Parsing [sent. 1993 len. 39]: Then , I presume , they will take the same measures for securing a supply of water throughout the year which have been so long adopted in India , and were formerly in South America by the Mexicans .
Parsing [sent. 1994 len. 21]: I mean that of digging large tanks , from which the water can not escape , except by evaporation . ''
Parsing [sent. 1995 len. 12]: `` I believe that it will be the only remedy . ''
Parsing [sent. 1996 len. 39]: `` Not only the remedy , but more than a remedy ; for tanks once established , vegetation will flourish , and the vegetation will not only husband the water in the country , but attract more . ''
Parsing [sent. 1997 len. 58]: `` All that is very true , '' replied Swinton , `` and I trust the time will come , when not only this land may be well watered with the dew of heaven , but that the rivers of grace may flow through it in every direction , and the tree of Christ may flourish . ''
Parsing [sent. 1998 len. 7]: `` Amen , '' replied Alexander .
Parsing [sent. 1999 len. 88]: `` But to resume the thread of my discourse , '' continued Swinton ; `` I was about to say , that the increase of population , and I may add the increase of riches , -- for in these nomadic tribes cattle are the only riches , -- is the great cause of these descents from the north ; for the continued droughts which I have mentioned of four or five years compel them to seek for pasture elsewhere , after their own is burned up .
Parsing [sent. 2000 len. 31]: At all events , it appears that the Caffre nations have been continually sustaining the pressure from without , both from the northward and the southward , for many years .

Parsing [sent. 2006 len. 12]: They are the mixed race between the Hottentots and the whites .
Parsing [sent. 2007 len. 49]: By the Dutch colonial law , these people could not hold possession of any land in the colony ; and this act of injustice and folly has deprived us of a very valuable race of men , who might have added much to the prosperity of the colony .
Parsing [sent. 2008 len. 36]: Brave and intelligent , industrious to a great degree , they , finding themselves despised on account of the Hottentot blood in their veins , have migrated from the colony and settled beyond the boundaries .
Parsing [sent. 2009 len. 54]: Being tolerably well provided with fire-arms , those who are peaceably inclined can protect themselves , while those who are otherwise commit great depredations upon the poor savages , following the example shown them by the colonists , and sweeping off their cattle and their property , in defiance of law and justice .

Parsing [sent. 2108 len. 14]: This last is smaller , but much fiercer , than the others . ''

Parsing [sent. 2110 len. 5]: Are they dangerous ? ''
Parsing [sent. 2111 len. 20]: `` The leopard shuns any conflict with man , but when driven to desperation it becomes a formidable antagonist .
Parsing [sent. 2112 len. 49]: I recollect very well two boors having attacked a leopard , and the animal , being hotly pressed by them and wounded , turned round and sprang upon the one nearest , pulling him to the ground , biting his shoulder , and tearing him with his claws .

''
