Parsing [sent. 2695 len. 18]: `` In the mean time I shall see if I can be of any use to Swanevelt .
Parsing [sent. 2696 len. 5]: Where 's Omrah ? ''
Parsing [sent. 2697 len. 17]: `` I saw him and Begum going out together just now , '' said the Major .
Parsing [sent. 2698 len. 10]: `` What for , I do not know . ''
Parsing [sent. 2699 len. 3]: `` Oh !
Parsing [sent. 2700 len. 36]: I told him to get some of the Bushman roots , '' said Alexander ; `` they are as good as potatoes when boiled ; and he has taken the monkey to find them . ''
Parsing [sent. 2701 len. 17]: The Major and Alexander remained on their beds till supper-time , when Mahomed woke them up .
Parsing [sent. 2702 len. 18]: They found themselves much refreshed by their sleep , and also found that their appetites had returned .
Parsing [sent. 2703 len. 47]: Buffalo-steaks and fried Bushman roots were declared to be a very good substitute for beefsteaks and fried potatoes ; and after they had made a hearty meal , Alexander inquired of Swinton what he had seen of buffalo-hunting when he had been at the Cape before .
Parsing [sent. 2704 len. 53]: `` I have only been once or twice engaged in a buffalo-hunt ; but I can tell you what I have heard , and what I have collected from my own knowledge , as to the nature of the animal , of which indeed to-day you have had a very good proof .
Parsing [sent. 2705 len. 75]: I told you this morning , that a single buffalo was more dangerous than a herd ; and the reason is this : -- At the breeding season , the fiercest bulls drive the others away from the herd , in the same manner as the elephants do ; and these solitary buffaloes are extremely dangerous , as they do not wait to be attacked , but will attack a man without any provocation .
Parsing [sent. 2706 len. 22]: They generally conceal themselves , and rush out upon you unawares , which makes it more difficult to escape from them .
Parsing [sent. 2707 len. 85]: They are so bold , that they do not fear the lion himself ; and I have been told by the Dutch boors , that when a buffalo has killed one of their comrades by goring and tossing him , it will not leave its victim for hours , but continue to trample on him with his hoofs , crushing the body with its knees as an elephant does , and with its rough tongue stripping off the skin as far as it can .
Parsing [sent. 2708 len. 28]: It does not do all this at one time , but it leaves the body , and returns again , as if to glut its vengeance . ''
Parsing [sent. 2709 len. 7]: `` What a malicious brute ! ''
Parsing [sent. 2710 len. 7]: `` Such is certainly its character .
Parsing [sent. 2711 len. 25]: I recollect a history of a buffalo-hunting adventure , told me by a Dutch farmer , who was himself an eye-witness to the scene .
Parsing [sent. 2712 len. 29]: He had gone out with a party to hunt a herd of buffaloes which were grazing on a piece of marshy ground , sprinkled with a few mimosa-trees .
Parsing [sent. 2713 len. 92]: As they could not get within shot of the herd , without crossing a portion of the marsh , which was not safe for horses , they agreed to leave their steeds in charge of two Hottentots , and to advance on foot ; thinking that , in case any of the buffaloes should charge them , it would be easy to escape by running back to the marsh , which would bear the weight of a man , but not of a horse , much less that of a buffalo .
Parsing [sent. 2714 len. 66]: `` They advanced accordingly over the marsh , and being concealed by some bushes , they had the good fortune to bring down , with the first volley , three of the fattest of the herd ; and also so severely wounded the great bull , which was the leader of the herd , that he dropped down on his knees , bellowing most furiously .
Parsing [sent. 2715 len. 46]: Thinking that the animal was mortally wounded , the foremost of the huntsmen walked out in front of the bushes from which they had fired , and began to reload his musket as he advanced , in order to give the animal a finishing shot .
Parsing [sent. 2716 len. 22]: But no sooner did the enraged animal see the man advancing , than he sprang up and charged headlong at him .
Parsing [sent. 2717 len. 53]: The man threw down his gun , and ran toward the marsh ; but the beast was so close upon him , that he despaired of escaping by that direction , and turning suddenly round a clump of copsewood , began to climb an old mimosa tree which stood close to it .
Parsing [sent. 2718 len. 12]: `` The buffalo was , however , too quick for him .
Parsing [sent. 2719 len. 79]: Bounding forward with a roar , which the farmer told me was one of the most hideous and appalling sounds that he ever heard , he caught the poor fellow with his terrible horns , just as he had nearly got out of reach , and tossed him in the air with such force , that after whirling round and round to a great height , the body fell into the fork of the branches of the tree .
Parsing [sent. 2720 len. 32]: The buffalo went round the tree roaring , and looking for the man , until , exhausted by wounds and loss of blood , it again fell down on its knees .
Parsing [sent. 2721 len. 27]: The other hunters then attacked and killed him ; but they found their comrade , who was still hanging in the tree , quite dead . ''
Parsing [sent. 2722 len. 57]: `` Well ; I have no doubt but that such would have been the fate of Swanevelt or of me , had the brute got hold of us , '' said the Major ; `` I never saw such a malignant , diabolical expression in any animal 's countenance as there was upon that buffalo 's .
Parsing [sent. 2723 len. 23]: A lion is , I should say , a gentleman and a man of honor compared to such an evil-disposed ruffian . ''
Parsing [sent. 2724 len. 26]: `` Well , Major , you have only to let them alone ; recollect , you were the aggressor , '' said Swinton , laughing .
Parsing [sent. 2725 len. 13]: `` Very true ; I never wish to see one again . ''
Parsing [sent. 2726 len. 37]: `` And I never wish to be in the way of a hippopotamus again , I can assure you , '' said Alexander , `` for a greater want of politeness I never met with . ''
Parsing [sent. 2727 len. 16]: During this conversation the Hottentots and Bushmen at the other fires had not been idle .
Parsing [sent. 2728 len. 65]: The Hottentots had fried and eaten , and fried and eaten , till they could hold no more ; and the Bushmen , who in the morning looked as thin and meager as if they had not had a meal for a month , were now so stuffed that they could hardly walk , and their lean stomachs were distended as round as balls .
Parsing [sent. 2729 len. 37]: The Bushman who had been tossed by the buffalo came up and asked for a little tobacco , at the same time smiling and patting his stomach , which was distended to a most extraordinary size .
Parsing [sent. 2730 len. 22]: `` Yes , let us give them some , '' said Alexander ; `` it will complete their day 's happiness .
Parsing [sent. 2731 len. 9]: Did you ever see a fellow so stuffed ?
Parsing [sent. 2732 len. 8]: I wonder he does not burst . ''
Parsing [sent. 2733 len. 6]: `` It is their custom .
Parsing [sent. 2734 len. 21]: They starve for days , and then gorge in this way when an opportunity offers , which is but seldom .
Parsing [sent. 2735 len. 58]: Their calendar , such as it is , is mainly from recollections of feasting ; and I will answer for it , that if one Bushman were on some future day to ask another when such a thing took place , he would reply , just before or just after the white men killed the buffaloes . ''
Parsing [sent. 2736 len. 9]: `` How do they live in general ? ''
Parsing [sent. 2737 len. 28]: `` They live upon roots at certain seasons of the year ; upon locusts when a flight takes place ; upon lizards , beetles -- any thing .
Parsing [sent. 2738 len. 10]: Occasionally they procure game , but not very often .
Parsing [sent. 2739 len. 32]: They are obliged to lie in wait for it , and wound it with their poisoned arrows , and then they follow its track and look for it the next day .
Parsing [sent. 2740 len. 23]: Subtle as the poison is they only cut out the part near the wound , and eat the rest of the animal .
Parsing [sent. 2741 len. 13]: They dig pit-holes for the hippopotamus and rhinoceros and occasionally take them .
Parsing [sent. 2742 len. 26]: They poison the pools for the game also ; but their living is very precarious , and they often suffer the extremities of hunger . ''
Parsing [sent. 2743 len. 21]: `` Is that the cause , do you imagine , of their being so diminutive a race , Swinton ? ''
Parsing [sent. 2744 len. 6]: `` No doubt of it .
Parsing [sent. 2745 len. 24]: Continual privation and hardships from generation to generation have , I have no doubt , dwindled them down to what you see . ''
Parsing [sent. 2746 len. 11]: `` How is it that these Bushmen are so familiar ?
Parsing [sent. 2747 len. 10]: I thought that they were savage and irreclaimable . ''
Parsing [sent. 2748 len. 32]: `` They are what are termed tame Bushmen ; that is , they have lived near the farmers , and have , by degrees , become less afraid of the Europeans .
Parsing [sent. 2749 len. 30]: Treated kindly , they have done good in return to the farmers by watching their sheep , and performing other little services , and have been rewarded with tobacco .
Parsing [sent. 2750 len. 10]: This has given them confidence to a certain degree .
Parsing [sent. 2751 len. 63]: But we must expect to meet with others that are equally wild , and who will be very mischievous ; attempting to drive off our cattle , and watching in ambush all round our caravan , ready for any pilfering that they can successfully accomplish ; and then we shall discover that we are in their haunts without even seeing them . ''
Parsing [sent. 2752 len. 5]: `` How so ? ''
Parsing [sent. 2753 len. 16]: `` Because it will only be by their thefts that we shall find it out .
Parsing [sent. 2754 len. 28]: But it is time for bed , and as to-morrow is Sunday you will have a day of rest , which I think you both require . ''
Parsing [sent. 2755 len. 16]: `` I do , '' replied Alexander , `` so good-night to you both . ''
Parsing [sent. 2756 len. 3]: CHAPTER XXII .
Parsing [sent. 2757 len. 11]: As arranged , they did not travel on the Sunday .
Parsing [sent. 2758 len. 56]: Early in the morning the oxen and horses and sheep were turned out to pasture ; all except the horse which had been ridden by Alexander on the preceding day , and which was found to be suffering so much that they took away a large quantity of blood from him before he was relieved .
Parsing [sent. 2759 len. 23]: The Bushmen still remained with them , and were likely to do so as long as there was any prospect of food .
Parsing [sent. 2760 len. 47]: The four buffaloes which had been killed , as well as the horse which had been gored to death , were found picked clean to the bones on the following day , by the hyenas and other animals which were heard prowling during the whole night .
Parsing [sent. 2761 len. 51]: But as large quantities of the buffalo-flesh had been cut off , and hung upon the trees near the caravan , there was more than sufficient for a second feast for the Bushmen and Hottentots , and there was nothing but frying and roasting during the whole of the day .
Parsing [sent. 2762 len. 61]: The sun was intensely hot , and Alexander and the Major both felt so fatigued from the exertions of the day before , that after breakfast they retired to their wagons , and Swinton did not attempt to disturb them , as they were in a sound sleep till the evening , when they were much refreshed and very hungry .
Parsing [sent. 2763 len. 42]: Swinton said he had thought it better that they should not be awakened , as the heat was so overpowering , and they could perform Divine service in the evening , if they thought proper , when it would be cooler .
Parsing [sent. 2764 len. 49]: This was agreed to , and , after an early supper , they summoned all the Hottentots , who , although gorged , were still unwilling to leave their fires ; as they said the Bushmen would devour all the flesh that was left , in their absence .
Parsing [sent. 2765 len. 12]: This remonstrance was not listened to , and they all assembled .
Parsing [sent. 2766 len. 28]: The prayers were read and the service gone through by the light of a large fire , for it was very dark before the service was finished .
Parsing [sent. 2767 len. 74]: The Bushmen , as the Hottentots prophesied , had taken advantage of their absence , to help themselves very liberally ; and as Swinton read the prayers , the eyes of the Hottentots were continually turning round to their own fires , where the Bushmen were throwing on large pieces of buffalo-flesh , and , before they were even heated through , were chewing them and tearing them to pieces with their teeth .
Parsing [sent. 2768 len. 24]: Never perhaps was there a congregation whose attention was so divided , and who were more anxious for the conclusion of the service .
Parsing [sent. 2769 len. 24]: This uneasiness shown by the Hottentots appeared at last to be communicated to the oxen , which were tied up round the wagons .
Parsing [sent. 2770 len. 63]: The fire required replenishing , but none of the Hottentots moved to perform the office ; perhaps they thought that if Swinton could no longer see , the service must conclude : but Swinton knew it by heart , and continued reading the Commandments , which was the last portion which he read , and Alexander and the Major repeated the responses .
Parsing [sent. 2771 len. 35]: The Major , whose face was toward the cattle , had observed their uneasiness , and guessed the cause , but did not like to interrupt the service , as it was just over .
Parsing [sent. 2772 len. 52]: Begum began clinging to him in the way she always did when she was afraid ; Swinton had just finished , and the Major was saying , `` Swinton , depend upon it , '' when a roar like thunder was heard , and a dark mass passed over their heads .
Parsing [sent. 2773 len. 53]: The bellowing and struggling of the oxen was almost instantaneously succeeded by a lion , with an ox borne on his shoulder , passing right through the whole congregation , sweeping away the remnants of the fire and the Hottentots right and left , and vanishing in a moment from their sight .
Parsing [sent. 2774 len. 11]: As may be imagined , all was confusion and alarm .
Parsing [sent. 2775 len. 17]: Some screamed , some shouted and ran for their guns ; but it was too late .
Parsing [sent. 2776 len. 65]: On examination , it was found that the lion had seized the ox which had been tied up near to where they were sitting ; their fire being nearly extinguished , and the one which should have been kept alight next to it altogether neglected by the Hottentots , in their anxiety to keep up those on which they had been broiling their buffalo-steaks .
Parsing [sent. 2777 len. 36]: The leather thongs by which the ox had been tied up were snapped like threads , and many of the other oxen had , in their agony of fear , broken their fastenings and escaped .
Parsing [sent. 2778 len. 25]: As the lion bounded away through the assembled party , it appeared as if the ox was not a feather 's weight to him .
Parsing [sent. 2779 len. 44]: He had , however , stepped rather roughly upon two of the Hottentots , who lay groaning , as if they had been severely hurt ; but upon examination it was found that they had only been well scratched and covered with ashes .
Parsing [sent. 2780 len. 23]: The Bushmen , however , had left their meal , and with their bows and small poisoned arrows had gone in pursuit .
Parsing [sent. 2781 len. 21]: Bremen and one or two of the Hottentots proposed also to go , but our travelers would not permit them .
Parsing [sent. 2782 len. 81]: About an hour afterward the Bushmen returned , and Omrah had communication with them ; and through Bremen they learned that the Bushmen had come up with the lion about a mile distant , and had discharged many of their arrows at him , and , they were convinced , with effect , as a heavy growl or an angry roar was the announcement when he was hit ; but , although he was irritated , he continued his repast .
Parsing [sent. 2783 len. 15]: Omrah then said , `` Lion dead to-morrow , -- Bushmen find him . ''
Parsing [sent. 2784 len. 63]: `` Well , '' said Alexander , as they went to their wagons , which , in consequence of this event , and their having to make up large fires before they went to bed , they did not do till late , `` I believe this is the first time that Divine service was ever wound up by such intrusion . ''
Parsing [sent. 2785 len. 30]: `` Perhaps so , '' replied Swinton ; `` but I think it proves that we have more cause for prayer , surrounded as we are by such danger .
Parsing [sent. 2786 len. 22]: The lion might have taken one of us , and by this time we should have suffered a horrid death . ''
Parsing [sent. 2787 len. 32]: `` I never felt the full force of the many similes and comparisons in the Scriptures , where the lion is so often introduced , till now , '' observed Alexander .
Parsing [sent. 2788 len. 36]: `` It was indeed a most awful sermon after the prayers , '' said the Major : `` I trust never to hear such a one again : but is it not our own fault ?
Parsing [sent. 2789 len. 29]: This is the second time that one of our oxen has been carried off by a lion , from the circle of fires not being properly attended to .
Parsing [sent. 2790 len. 25]: It is the neglect of the Hottentots , certainly ; but if they are so neglectful , we should attend to them ourselves . ''
Parsing [sent. 2791 len. 51]: `` It will be as well to punish them for their neglect , '' said Swinton , `` by stopping their tobacco for the week ; for if they find that we attend to the fires ourselves , they will not keep one in , that you may depend upon .
Parsing [sent. 2792 len. 13]: However , we will discuss that point to-morrow , so good-night . ''
Parsing [sent. 2793 len. 78]: Omrah came to the Major the next morning , before the oxen were yoked , to say that the Bushmen had found the lion , and that he was not yet dead , but nearly so ; that the animal had dragged away that portion of the ox that he did not eat , about half a mile further ; that there he had lain down , and he was so sick that he could not move .
Parsing [sent. 2794 len. 24]: At this intelligence they mounted their horses , and , guided by the Bushmen , arrived at the bush where the lion lay .
Parsing [sent. 2795 len. 30]: The Bushmen entered at once , for they had previously reconnoitered , and were saluted with a low snarl , very different from the roar of the preceding night .
Parsing [sent. 2796 len. 23]: Our travelers followed , and found the noble creature in his last agonies , his strength paralyzed , and his eyes closed .
Parsing [sent. 2797 len. 65]: One or two of the small arrows of the Bushmen were still sticking in his hide , and did not appear to have entered more than half an inch ; but the poison was so subtle , that it had rapidly circulated through his whole frame ; and while they were looking down upon the noble beast , it dropped its jaws and expired .
Parsing [sent. 2798 len. 50]: As our travelers turned back to join the caravan , Alexander observed : `` Those Bushmen , diminutive as they are in size , and contemptible as their weapons appear , must be dangerous enemies , when the mere prick of one of their small arrows is certain death .
Parsing [sent. 2799 len. 8]: What is their poison composed of ? ''
Parsing [sent. 2800 len. 31]: `` Of the venom extracted from snakes , which is mixed up with the juice of the euphorbia , and boiled down till it becomes of the consistency of glue .
Parsing [sent. 2801 len. 18]: They then dip the heads of the arrows into it , and let it dry on . ''
Parsing [sent. 2802 len. 20]: `` Is then the venom of snakes so active after it has been taken away from the animal ? ''
Parsing [sent. 2803 len. 9]: `` Yes , for a considerable time after .
Parsing [sent. 2804 len. 30]: I remember a story , which is , I believe , well authenticated , of a man who had been bitten through his boot by a rattlesnake in America .
Parsing [sent. 2805 len. 34]: The man died , and shortly afterward his two sons died one after the other , with just the same symptoms as their father , although they had not been bitten by snakes .
Parsing [sent. 2806 len. 49]: It was afterward discovered that upon the father 's death the sons had one after the other taken possession of and put on his boots , and the boots being examined , the fang of the rattlesnake was discovered to have passed through the leather and remained there .
Parsing [sent. 2807 len. 26]: The fang had merely grazed the skin of the two sons when they put on the boots , and had thus caused their death . ''
Parsing [sent. 2808 len. 17]: `` Are the snakes here as deadly in their poison as the rattlesnake of America ? ''
Parsing [sent. 2809 len. 18]: `` Equally so , -- that is , two or three of them ; some are harmless .
Parsing [sent. 2810 len. 21]: The most formidable is the cobra capella -LRB- not the same as the Indian snake of the same name -RRB- .
Parsing [sent. 2811 len. 22]: It is very large , being usually five feet long ; but it has been found six and even seven feet .
Parsing [sent. 2812 len. 23]: This snake has been known to dart at a man on horseback , and with such force as to overshoot his aim .
Parsing [sent. 2813 len. 22]: His bite is certain death , I believe , as I never heard of a man recovering from the wound . ''
Parsing [sent. 2814 len. 11]: `` Well , that is as bad as can be .
Parsing [sent. 2815 len. 6]: What is the next ? ''
Parsing [sent. 2816 len. 11]: `` The next is what they call the puff adder .
Parsing [sent. 2817 len. 31]: It is a very heavy , sluggish animal , and very thick in proportion to its length , and when attacked in front , it can not make any spring .
Parsing [sent. 2818 len. 34]: It has , however , another power , which , if you are not prepared for it , is perhaps equally dangerous -- that of throwing itself backward in a most surprising manner .
Parsing [sent. 2819 len. 19]: This is , however , only when trod upon or provoked ; but its bite is very deadly .
Parsing [sent. 2820 len. 14]: Then two of the mountain adders are among the most dangerous snakes here .
Parsing [sent. 2821 len. 34]: The mountain adder is small , and , from its not being so easily seen and so easily avoided , is very dangerous , and its bite as fatal as the others . ''
Parsing [sent. 2822 len. 12]: `` I trust that is the end of your catalogue ? ''
Parsing [sent. 2823 len. 33]: `` Not exactly ; there is another , which I have specimens of , but whose faculties I have never seen put to the test , which is called the spirting snake .
Parsing [sent. 2824 len. 18]: It is about three feet long , and its bite , although poisonous , is not fatal .
Parsing [sent. 2825 len. 43]: But it has a faculty , from which its name is derived , of spirting its venom into the face of its assailant , and if the venom enters the eye , at which the animal darts it , immediate blindness ensues .
Parsing [sent. 2826 len. 20]: There are a great many other varieties , some of which we have obtained possession of during our journey .
Parsing [sent. 2827 len. 18]: Many of them are venomous , but not so fatal as the first three I have mentioned .
Parsing [sent. 2828 len. 28]: `` Indeed , it is a great blessing that the Almighty has not made the varieties of snakes aggressive or fierce , -- which they are not .
Parsing [sent. 2829 len. 33]: Provided , as they are , with such dreadful powers , if they were so , they would indeed be formidable ; but they only act in self-defense , or when provoked .
Parsing [sent. 2830 len. 68]: I may as well here observe , that the Hottentots , when they kill any of the dangerous snakes , invariably cut off the head and bury it ; and this they do , that no one may by chance tread upon it , as they assert that the poison of the fangs is as potent as ever , not only for weeks but months afterward . ''
Parsing [sent. 2831 len. 23]: `` That certainly is a corroboration of the story that you told us of the rattlesnake 's fang in the boot . ''
Parsing [sent. 2832 len. 30]: `` It is so ; but although there are so many venomous snakes in this country , it is remarkable how very few accidents or deaths occur from them .
Parsing [sent. 2833 len. 84]: I made an inquiry at the Moravian Mission , where these venomous snakes are very plentiful , how many people they had lost by their bites , and the missionaries told me , that out of 800 Hottentots belonging to the Mission , they had only lost two men by the bites of snakes during a space of seven years ; and in other places where I made the same inquiry , the casualties were much less in proportion to the numbers . ''
Parsing [sent. 2834 len. 13]: `` Is the boa constrictor found in this part of Africa ? ''
Parsing [sent. 2835 len. 21]: `` Not so far south as we now are , but it is a few degrees more to the northward .
Parsing [sent. 2836 len. 18]: I have never seen it , but I believe there is no doubt of its existence . ''
Parsing [sent. 2837 len. 17]: `` The South American Indians have a very subtle poison with which they kill their game .
Parsing [sent. 2838 len. 10]: Are you aware , Swinton , of its nature ?
Parsing [sent. 2839 len. 9]: Is it like the Bushmen 's poison ? ''
Parsing [sent. 2840 len. 23]: `` I know the poison well ; it was brought over by Mr. Waterton , whose amusing works you may have read .
Parsing [sent. 2841 len. 26]: It is called the wourali poison , and is said to be extracted from a sort of creeping vine , which grows in the country .
Parsing [sent. 2842 len. 30]: The natives , however , add the poison of snakes to the extract ; and the preparation is certainly very fatal , as I can bear witness to . ''
Parsing [sent. 2843 len. 9]: `` Have you ever seen it tried ? ''
Parsing [sent. 2844 len. 9]: `` Yes , I have tried it myself .
Parsing [sent. 2845 len. 41]: When I was in Italy I became acquainted with Mr. W. , and he gave two or three of us , who were living together , a small quantity , not much more than two grains of mustard-seed in size .
Parsing [sent. 2846 len. 27]: We purchased a young mule to make the experiment upon ; an incision was made in its shoulder , and the poison inserted under the skin .
Parsing [sent. 2847 len. 13]: I think in about six or seven minutes the animal was dead .
Parsing [sent. 2848 len. 30]: Mr. W. said that the effects would have been instantaneous , if the virtue of the poison had not somewhat deteriorated from its having been kept so long . ''
Parsing [sent. 2849 len. 14]: `` The wourali poison only acts upon the nerves , I believe ? ''
Parsing [sent. 2850 len. 4]: said the Major .
Parsing [sent. 2851 len. 48]: `` Only upon the nerves ; and although so fatal , if immediate means are resorted to , a person who is apparently dead from it may be brought to life again by the same process as is usual in the recovery of drowned or suffocated people .
Parsing [sent. 2852 len. 31]: A donkey upon which the poison had acted was restored in this manner , and for the remainder of his days permitted to run in Sir Joseph Banks 's park .
Parsing [sent. 2853 len. 18]: But the poison of snakes acts upon the blood , and therefore occasions death without remedy . ''
Parsing [sent. 2854 len. 17]: `` But there are remedies , I believe , for even the most fatal poisons ? ''
Parsing [sent. 2855 len. 29]: `` Yes , in His provident mercy God has been pleased to furnish remedies at hand , and where the snake exists the remedy is to be found .
Parsing [sent. 2856 len. 57]: The rattlesnake root is a cure , if taken and applied immediately ; and it is well known that the ichneumon when bitten by the cobra capella , in his attack upon it , will hasten to a particular herb and eat it immediately , to prevent the fatal effect of the animal 's bite . ''
Parsing [sent. 2857 len. 31]: `` I once saw a native of India , '' said the Major , `` who for a small sum would allow himself to be bitten by a cobra capella .
Parsing [sent. 2858 len. 25]: He was well provided with the same plant used by the ichneumon , which he swallowed plentifully , and also rubbed on the wound .
Parsing [sent. 2859 len. 21]: It is impossible to say , but , so far as I could judge , there was no deception . ''
Parsing [sent. 2860 len. 20]: `` I think it very possible ; if the plant will cure the ichneumon , why not a man ?
Parsing [sent. 2861 len. 20]: I have no doubt but that there are many plants which possess virtues of which we have no knowledge .
Parsing [sent. 2862 len. 58]: Some few , and perhaps some of the most valuable , we have discovered ; but our knowledge of the vegetable kingdom , as far as its medicinal properties are known , is very slight ; and perhaps many which were formerly known have , since the introduction of mineral antidotes , been lost sight of . ''
Parsing [sent. 2863 len. 32]: `` Why , yes ; long before chemistry had made any advances , we do hear in old romances of balsams of most sovereign virtues , '' said Alexander , laughing .
Parsing [sent. 2864 len. 72]: `` Which , I may observe , is almost a proof that they did in reality exist ; and the more so , because you will find that the knowledge of these sovereign remedies was chiefly in the hands of the Jews , the oldest nation upon the earth ; and from their constant communication with each other , most likely to have transmitted their knowledge from generation to generation . ''
Parsing [sent. 2865 len. 53]: `` We have also reason to believe that not only they had peculiar _ remedies _ in their times , but also -- if we are to credit what has been handed down to us -- that the art of _ poisoning _ was much better understood , '' said the Major .
Parsing [sent. 2866 len. 24]: `` At all events , they had not the knowledge of chemistry which now leads to its immediate detection , '' replied Swinton .
Parsing [sent. 2867 len. 18]: `` But , Alexander , there are three hippopotami lying asleep on the side of the river .
Parsing [sent. 2868 len. 10]: Have you a mind to try your skill ? ''
Parsing [sent. 2869 len. 18]: `` No , not particularly , '' replied Alexander ; `` I have had enough of hippopotami .
Parsing [sent. 2870 len. 14]: By the by , the river is much wider than it was . ''
Parsing [sent. 2871 len. 18]: `` Yes , by my calculation we ought to travel no more to the westward after to-day .
Parsing [sent. 2872 len. 12]: We must now cut across to the Yellow or Val River .

Parsing [sent. 3248 len. 48]: Once when I was traveling in Namaqua-land , I observed a spot which was imprinted with at least twenty spoors or marks of a lion 's paw ; and as I pointed them out a Namaqua chief told me that a lion had been practicing his leap .
Parsing [sent. 3249 len. 120]: On demanding an explanation , he said that if a lion sprang at an animal , and missed it by leaping short , he would always go back to where he sprang from , and practice the leap so as to be successful on another occasion ; and he then related to me the following anecdote , stating that he was an eye-witness to the incident : '' ' I was passing near the end of a craggy hill from which jutted out a smooth rock of from ten to twelve feet high , when I perceived a number of zebras galloping round it , which they were obliged to do , as the rock beyond was quite steep .

Parsing [sent. 3465 len. 41]: `` Well , I think a passing shot will be best ; for if we are to hunt , we must send a party on the opposite side of the river , and that will be a tedious affair . ''
Parsing [sent. 3466 len. 26]: `` I think myself it will be better to proceed , '' said Swinton ; `` so now then , to scatter the enemy . ''
Parsing [sent. 3467 len. 42]: They soon arrived at that part of the river where they had at a distance discovered the elephants bathing ; but as they approached , the high reeds prevented them from seeing the animals , although they could hear them plainly .
Parsing [sent. 3468 len. 58]: At last , as they proceeded a little further up the river , they discovered a female with its young one by its side ; the mother playing with its offspring , pouring water over it with its trunk , and now and then pressing it into the water , so as to compel it to swim .
Parsing [sent. 3469 len. 50]: They watched the motions of the animals for some time , and the Major first broke silence by saying , `` I really have not the heart to fire at the poor creature ; its maternal kindness , and the playing of the little one , are too interesting .
Parsing [sent. 3470 len. 25]: It would be cruel , now that we do not want meat , for an eland is to be killed every ten minutes . ''
Parsing [sent. 3471 len. 14]: `` I am glad to hear you say so , '' replied Swinton .
Parsing [sent. 3472 len. 15]: `` Let us fire over them , and set them all in motion . ''
Parsing [sent. 3473 len. 26]: `` Agreed , '' said the Major ; `` this is to start them , '' and he fired off his rifle in the air .
Parsing [sent. 3474 len. 83]: The noise that ensued was quite appalling ; the shrieks and cries of the elephants , and the treading down and rushing through the reeds , the splashing and floundering in the mud , for a few seconds , was followed by the bounding out of the whole herd on the opposite bank of the river , tossing their trunks , raising up their ears , roaring wildly , and starting through the bushes into the forest from which they had descended .
Parsing [sent. 3475 len. 68]: Two large males only were to be perceived among the whole herd , the rest were all females and their young ones , who scrambled away after the males , crowding together , but still occasionally looking behind after their young ones , till they had all disappeared in the forest , the cracking and crushing of the bushes in which were heard for many minutes afterward .
Parsing [sent. 3476 len. 11]: `` That was a splendid scene , '' said Alexander .
Parsing [sent. 3477 len. 19]: `` Yes , it was a living panorama , which one must come to Africa to behold . ''
Parsing [sent. 3478 len. 19]: `` I do not think that I shall ever become a true elephant-hunter , '' said the Major .
Parsing [sent. 3479 len. 24]: `` I feel a sort of repugnance to destroy so sagacious an animal , and a degree of remorse when one lies dead .
Parsing [sent. 3480 len. 30]: At the same time , if once accustomed to the fearful crashing and noise attending their movements , I do not consider them very dangerous animals to pursue . ''
Parsing [sent. 3481 len. 9]: `` Not if people are cool and collected .

Parsing [sent. 3484 len. 26]: He did so and won his bet , for the elephant can not see behind him , and is not very quick in turning round .
Parsing [sent. 3485 len. 34]: However , a short time afterward he made the same attempt , and being foolhardy from success , the animal was too quick for him , and he was crushed to death . ''
Parsing [sent. 3486 len. 29]: Bremen now came up to them , to say that there was a party of people to the eastward , and he thought that there was a wagon .
Parsing [sent. 3487 len. 45]: On examination with their telescopes , they found that such was the case ; and our travelers turned their horses ' heads in the direction , to ascertain who they might be , leaving the caravan to proceed by the banks of the river .
Parsing [sent. 3488 len. 26]: In about an hour , they came close to them , and Swinton immediately recognized them as Griquas , or mixed European and Hottentot races .
Parsing [sent. 3489 len. 43]: Of course , they met in the most friendly manner , and the Griquas said that they had come to hunt the elephant , eland , and other animals ; the former for their ivory , and the latter for their flesh .
Parsing [sent. 3490 len. 41]: Their wagon , which was a very old one , was loaded with flesh , cut in long strips , and hanging to dry ; and they had a great many hundred-weight of ivory , which they had already collected .
Parsing [sent. 3491 len. 31]: As soon as our travelers had explained to them their own motions , the Griquas said that they would bring their wagon down in the evening and encamp with them .
Parsing [sent. 3492 len. 8]: Our travelers then returned to the caravan .
Parsing [sent. 3493 len. 13]: As they promised , the Griquas joined them late in the afternoon .
Parsing [sent. 3494 len. 23]: They were a party of sixteen ; all stout fellows , and armed with the long guns used by the Dutch boors .
Parsing [sent. 3495 len. 32]: They said that they had been two months from Griqua-town , and were thinking of returning very soon , as their wagon was loaded to the extent that it would bear .
Parsing [sent. 3496 len. 92]: The Major stating that it was their intention to hunt the giraffe , the Griquas informed them that they would not find the animal to the southward of the Val River , and they would have to cross over into the territories of the king Moselekatsee , who ruled over the Bechuana country , to the northward of the river ; and that it would be very dangerous to attempt so to do without his permission ; indeed , that there would be danger in doing so , even with it .
Parsing [sent. 3497 len. 13]: `` Do you know any thing of this person , Swinton ? ''
Parsing [sent. 3498 len. 30]: `` Yes , I have heard of him , but I did not know that he had extended his conquests so low down as to the Val River . ''
Parsing [sent. 3499 len. 6]: `` Who is he ? ''

Parsing [sent. 3501 len. 15]: `` Yes , '' replied Alexander ; `` we have heard of him . ''
Parsing [sent. 3502 len. 36]: `` Well , Moselekatsee was a chief of two or three tribes , who , when hard pressed by his enemies , took refuge with Chaka , and became one of his principal warrior chiefs .
Parsing [sent. 3503 len. 47]: After a time he quarreled with Chaka , about the distribution of some cattle they had taken , and aware that he had no mercy to expect from the tyrant , he revolted from him with a large force , and withdrew to the Bechuana country .
Parsing [sent. 3504 len. 23]: There he conquered all the tribes , enrolled them in his own army , and gradually became as formidable as Chaka himself .
Parsing [sent. 3505 len. 43]: In the arrangements of his army , he followed the same plans as Chaka , and has now become a most powerful monarch , and , they do say , is almost as great a tyrant and despot as Chaka himself was .
Parsing [sent. 3506 len. 23]: I believe that the Griquas are right in saying there would be danger in passing through his dominions without his permission . ''

Parsing [sent. 3509 len. 20]: However , we had better put this question to the Griquas , who probably can answer it better . ''
Parsing [sent. 3510 len. 92]: The Griquas , on being questioned , replied , that the best plan would be to send a message to the Matabili capital , where Moselekatsee resided , requesting permission to hunt in the country , and begging the monarch to send some of his principal men to receive the presents which they had to offer ; -- that it would not take long to receive an answer , as it would only be necessary to deliver the message to the first officer belonging to Moselekatsee , at the advanced post .
Parsing [sent. 3511 len. 24]: That officer would immediately dispatch a native with the message , who would arrive much sooner than any one they could send themselves .
Parsing [sent. 3512 len. 17]: Bremen and three other Hottentots offered to take the message , if our travelers wished it .
Parsing [sent. 3513 len. 18]: This was agreed to , and that afternoon they mounted their horses , and crossed the river .
Parsing [sent. 3514 len. 25]: By the advice of the Griquas , the camp was shifted about a mile further up the river , on account of the lions .
Parsing [sent. 3515 len. 17]: The weather now threatened a change ; masses of clouds accumulated , but were again dispersed .
Parsing [sent. 3516 len. 39]: The next day the weather was again threatening ; thunder pealed in the distant mountains , and the forked lightning flew in every direction ; but the rain , if any , was expended on the neighboring hills .
Parsing [sent. 3517 len. 33]: A strong wind soon blew up so as to try the strength of the canvas awning of their wagons , and they found it difficult to keep their fires in at night .
Parsing [sent. 3518 len. 48]: They had encamped upon a wide plain covered with high grass , and abounding with elands and other varieties of antelopes : here they remained for five days , waiting the reply of the king of the Matabili , and went out every day to procure game .
Parsing [sent. 3519 len. 41]: On the Sabbath-day , after they had , as usual , performed Divine service , they observed a heavy smoke to windward , which , as the wind was fresh , soon bore down upon them and inconvenienced them much .
Parsing [sent. 3520 len. 41]: Swanevelt stated that the high grass had been fired by some means or another , and as it threatened to come down upon the encampment , the Hottentots and Griquas were very busy beating down the grass round about them .
Parsing [sent. 3521 len. 45]: When they had so done , they went to windward some hundred yards and set fire to the grass in several places ; the grass burned quickly , till it arrived at where it had been beaten down , and the fire was extinguished .
Parsing [sent. 3522 len. 37]: That this was a necessary precaution was fully proved , for as the night closed in , the whole country for miles was on fire , and the wind bore the flames down rapidly toward them .
Parsing [sent. 3523 len. 55]: The sky was covered with clouds , and the darkness of the night made the flames appear still more vivid ; the wind drove them along with a loud crackling noise , sweeping over the undulating ground , now rising and now disappearing in the hollows , the whole landscape lighted up for miles .
Parsing [sent. 3524 len. 57]: As our travelers watched the progress of the flames , and every now and then observed a terrified antelope spring from its lair , and appearing like a black figure in a phantasmagoria , suddenly the storm burst upon them and the rain poured down in torrents , accompanied with large hailstones and thunder and lightning .
Parsing [sent. 3525 len. 25]: The wind was instantly lulled , and after the first burst of the storm a deathlike silence succeeded to the crackling of the flames .
Parsing [sent. 3526 len. 33]: A deluge of rain descended , and in an instant every spark of the conflagration was extinguished , and the pitchy darkness of the night was unbroken by even a solitary star .
Parsing [sent. 3527 len. 52]: The next morning was bright and clear , and after breakfast , they perceived the Hottentots who had been sent on their message to Moselekatsee , on the opposite bank of the river , accompanied by three of the natives ; they soon crossed the river and came to the encampment .
Parsing [sent. 3528 len. 66]: The natives , who were Matabili , were tall , powerful men , well proportioned , and with regular features ; their hair was shorn , and surmounted with an oval ring attached to the scalp , and the lobe of their left ears was perforated with such a large hole , that it contained a small gourd , which was used as a snuff-box .
Parsing [sent. 3529 len. 23]: Their dress was a girdle of strips of catskins , and they each carried two javelins and a knobbed stick for throwing .
Parsing [sent. 3530 len. 25]: They were heartily welcomed by our travelers , who placed before them a large quantity of eland-steaks , and filled their boxes with snuff .
Parsing [sent. 3531 len. 70]: As soon as they had finished eating , and drawn up a large quantity of snuff into their nostrils , they explained through the Griquas , who could speak their language , that they had come from the greatest of all monarchs in the world , Moselekatsee , who wished to know who the strangers were , what they wanted of him , and what presents they had brought .
Parsing [sent. 3532 len. 147]: Swinton , who was spokesman , returned for answer that they were hunters , and not traders ; that they had come to see the wonders of the country belonging to so great a monarch , and that hearing that his majesty had animals in his country which were not to be found elsewhere , they wanted permission to kill some , to show upon their return to their own people what a wonderful country it was that belonged to so great a monarch ; -- that they had brought beads and copper wire , and knives , and boxes for making fire , and snuff and tobacco , all of which they wished to present to the great monarch ; a part as soon as they had received his permission to enter his territory , and another part when they were about to leave it .
Parsing [sent. 3533 len. 70]: A handsome present of the above articles was then produced , and the messengers of the king , having surveyed the articles with some astonishment , declared that their king would feel very glad when he saw all these things , and that he had desired them to tell our travelers that they might come into his dominions with safety , and kill all the animals that they pleased .
Parsing [sent. 3534 len. 37]: That his majesty had commanded one of them to remain with the party , and that as soon as he had received his presents , he would send a chief to be answerable for their safety .
Parsing [sent. 3535 len. 25]: The Matabili then packed up the articles presented , and two of them set off at full speed on their return to the king .
Parsing [sent. 3536 len. 26]: The third , who remained , assured our travelers that they might cross the river and enter the Matabili country as soon as they pleased .
Parsing [sent. 3537 len. 17]: A debate now ensued as to whether they should go with their whole force or not .
Parsing [sent. 3538 len. 68]: The Matabili had informed them that in three days ' journey they would fall in with the giraffe , which they were in search of , and as there would be some risk in crossing the river , and they had every reason to expect that it would soon rise , the question was whether it would be prudent to take over even one of the wagons .
Parsing [sent. 3539 len. 77]: The opinion of the Griquas was asked , and it was ultimately arranged that they should take over Alexander 's wagon only , with fifteen pair of oxen , and that some of the Griquas should accompany them , with Swanevelt , Omrah , and Mahomed ; -- that Bremen and the Hottentots should remain where they were , with the other three wagons and the rest of the Griquas , until our travelers should return .
Parsing [sent. 3540 len. 42]: This arrangement was not at all disagreeable to the Hottentots , who did not much like the idea of entering the Matabili country , and were very happy in their present quarters , as they were plentifully provided with good meat .
Parsing [sent. 3541 len. 28]: Alexander 's wagon was therefore arranged so as to carry the bedding and articles they might require , all other things being removed to the other wagons .
Parsing [sent. 3542 len. 49]: Their best oxen were selected , and eight of the fleetest of their horses , and on the following morning , having ascertained from the Matabili the best place to cross the river , our travelers set off , and in an hour were on the other side .
Parsing [sent. 3543 len. 28]: There was no change in the country during the first day 's journey ; the same variety and brilliancy of flowers were every where to be seen .
Parsing [sent. 3544 len. 41]: The eland and the other antelopes were plentiful , and they were soon joined by parties of the natives , who requested them to shoot the animals for them , which they did in quantities even sufficient to satisfy them .
Parsing [sent. 3545 len. 43]: Indeed if they found them troublesome , our travelers had only to bring down an eland , and the natives were immediately left behind , that they might devour the animal , which was done in an incredibly short space of time .
Parsing [sent. 3546 len. 53]: The Matabili who had conducted them proved to be a chief , and if he gave any order , it was instantly obeyed ; so that our travelers had no trouble with the natives except their begging and praying for snuff , which was incessant , both from the men and women .
Parsing [sent. 3547 len. 39]: Neither did they fear any treachery from the Matabili king , as they were well armed , and the Griquas were brave men , and the superiority of their weapons made them a match for a large force .
Parsing [sent. 3548 len. 39]: Every precaution , however , was taken when they halted at night , which they invariably did in the center of an open plain , to prevent any surprise ; and large fires were lighted round the wagon .
Parsing [sent. 3549 len. 37]: They traveled on in this way for two days more , when in the evening they arrived at a large plain sprinkled with mimosa-trees , and abutting on the foot of a low range of hills .
Parsing [sent. 3550 len. 52]: The Matabili told them that they would find the giraffes on these plains , and the Major , who was very anxious , kept his telescope to his eyes , looking round in every direction till nightfall , but did not succeed in descrying any of the objects of his search .
Parsing [sent. 3551 len. 25]: They retired that night with anxious expectation for the following morning , when they anticipated that they should fall in with these remarkable animals .
Parsing [sent. 3552 len. 40]: Their guns were examined and every precaution taken , and having lighted their fires and set the watch , they went to bed ; and , after commending themselves to the care of Providence , were soon fast asleep .
Parsing [sent. 3553 len. 3]: CHAPTER XXVI .
Parsing [sent. 3554 len. 24]: With the exception of three lions coming very near to the encampment and rousing up the Griquas , nothing occurred during the night .
Parsing [sent. 3555 len. 44]: In the morning they yoked the oxen and had all the horses saddled ready for the chase ; but they were disappointed for nearly the whole day ; as , although they saw a variety of game , no giraffe appeared in sight .
Parsing [sent. 3556 len. 39]: In the afternoon , as they passed by a clump of mimosas , they were charged by a rhinoceros , which nearly threw down Alexander 's best horse ; but a volley from the Griquas laid him prostrate .
Parsing [sent. 3557 len. 24]: It was a very large animal , but not of the black or ferocious sort , being what is termed the white rhinoceros .
Parsing [sent. 3558 len. 61]: Within the last two days they had also observed that the gnoo was not of the same sort as the one which they had seen so long , but a variety which Swinton told them was called the brindled gnoo ; it was , however , in every other respect the same animal , as to its motions and peculiarities .
Parsing [sent. 3559 len. 29]: Toward the evening the Matabili warrior who accompanied them pointed to a mimosa at a distance , and made signs to the Major that there was a giraffe .
Parsing [sent. 3560 len. 13]: `` I can not see him -- do you , Alexander ? ''
Parsing [sent. 3561 len. 23]: said the Major ; `` he points to that mimosa with the dead stump on the other side of it , there .
Parsing [sent. 3562 len. 27]: Yes , it is one , I see the stump , as I called it , move ; it must be the neck of the animal .
Parsing [sent. 3563 len. 31]: Let loose the dogs , Swanevelt , '' cried the Major , starting off at full speed , and followed by Alexander , and Omrah , with the spare horse .
Parsing [sent. 3564 len. 44]: In a minute or two the giraffe was seen to get clear of the mimosa , and then set off in an awkward , shambling kind of gallop ; but awkward as the gallop appeared , the animal soon left the Major behind .
Parsing [sent. 3565 len. 27]: It sailed along with incredible velocity , its long , swan-like neck keeping time with its legs , and its black tail curled above its back .
Parsing [sent. 3566 len. 28]: `` Push on , Alexander , '' cried the Major ; `` if ever there were seven-league boots , that animal has a pair of them on .
Parsing [sent. 3567 len. 28]: He goes like the wind ; but he can not keep it up long , depend upon it , and our horses are in capital condition . ''
Parsing [sent. 3568 len. 85]: Alexander and the Major were now neck and neck , close to each other , at full speed , when of a sudden the Major 's horse stumbled , and fell upon an ostrich , which was sitting on her nest ; Alexander 's horse also stumbled and followed after the Major ; and there they were , horses and riders , all rolling together among the ostrich-eggs ; while the ostrich gained her legs , and ran off as fast as the giraffe .
Parsing [sent. 3569 len. 57]: As soon as they had got on their legs again , and caught the bridles of their horses , they looked round , but could not distinguish the giraffe , which was out of sight among the mimosa-trees ; while Omrah was very busy picking up their rifles , and laughing in a very disrespectful manner .
Parsing [sent. 3570 len. 10]: The Major and Alexander soon joined in the laugh .
Parsing [sent. 3571 len. 13]: No bones were broken , and the horses had received no injury .
Parsing [sent. 3572 len. 15]: All they had to do was to return to the caravan looking very foolish .
Parsing [sent. 3573 len. 24]: `` Your first essay in giraffe-hunting has been very successful , '' said Swinton , laughing , as they came up to him .

Parsing [sent. 3575 len. 3]: said Alexander .
Parsing [sent. 3576 len. 18]: `` However , we have got some ostrich-eggs for supper , and that is better than nothing .
Parsing [sent. 3577 len. 20]: It will soon be dark , so we had better encamp for the night , had we not ? ''
Parsing [sent. 3578 len. 12]: `` I was about to propose it , '' said Swinton .
Parsing [sent. 3579 len. 11]: `` Did you ever hunt the giraffe , Swinton ? ''
Parsing [sent. 3580 len. 30]: inquired Alexander , as they were making their supper on roasted ostrich-eggs ; each of them holding one between his knees , and dipping out with a large spoon .
Parsing [sent. 3581 len. 21]: `` Never , '' replied Swinton ; `` I have often seen them in Namaqua-land , but never killed one .
Parsing [sent. 3582 len. 31]: I remember , however , a circumstance connected with the giraffe , which would have been incredible to me , if I had not seen the remains of the lion .
Parsing [sent. 3583 len. 49]: You are well aware how long and strong are the thorns of the mimosa -LRB- or kamel-tree , as the Dutch call it , from the giraffe browsing upon it -RRB- , and how the boughs of these trees lie like an umbrella , close upon one another .
Parsing [sent. 3584 len. 14]: A native chief informed me that he witnessed a lion attacking a giraffe .
Parsing [sent. 3585 len. 25]: The lion always springs at the head or neck , and seizes the animal by that part , riding him , as it were .
Parsing [sent. 3586 len. 43]: The giraffe sets off at full speed with its enemy , and is so powerful as often to get rid of him ; for I have seen giraffes killed which had the marks of the lion 's teeth and claws upon them .
Parsing [sent. 3587 len. 52]: In this instance the lion made a spring , but the giraffe at that very moment turning sharp round , the lion missed his aim , and by the blow it received was tossed in the air , so that he fell upon the boughs of the mimosa on his back .
Parsing [sent. 3588 len. 33]: The boughs were not only compact enough to bear his weight , but the thorns that pierced through his body were so strong as to hold the enormous animal where he lay .
Parsing [sent. 3589 len. 32]: He could not disengage himself ; and they pointed out to me the skeleton on the boughs of the tree , as a corroboration of the truth of the story . ''
Parsing [sent. 3590 len. 25]: `` It does really approach to the marvelous , '' observed the Major ; `` but , as you say , seeing is believing .

Parsing [sent. 3597 len. 33]: Depend upon it , as soon as the king hears of it , we shall be looked upon as spies , and he may send a party to cut us off . ''
Parsing [sent. 3598 len. 11]: `` Have you said any thing to the Griquas ? ''
Parsing [sent. 3599 len. 29]: `` Yes , and they laughed , and said that they should not care if we went right up to the principal town , where Moselekatsee resides . ''
Parsing [sent. 3600 len. 31]: `` Well , they are bold enough , and so far are good traveling companions ; but we certainly did not come here to fight , '' observed the Major .
Parsing [sent. 3601 len. 14]: `` But does the Matabili with us know that they are Griquas ? ''

Parsing [sent. 3608 len. 25]: They rose early the next morning , and , leaving the wagon where it was , again proceeded on horseback in search of giraffes .
Parsing [sent. 3609 len. 18]: They rode at a slow pace for four or five miles , before they could discover any .
Parsing [sent. 3610 len. 18]: At last a herd of them were seen standing together browsing on the leaves of the mimosa .
Parsing [sent. 3611 len. 22]: They made a long circuit to turn them , and drive them toward the camp , and in this they succeeded .

Parsing [sent. 3613 len. 26]: After a chase of three miles , they found that the animals ' speed was rapidly decreasing , and they were coming up with them .
Parsing [sent. 3614 len. 18]: When within a hundred yards , Alexander fired and wounded a female which was in the rear .
Parsing [sent. 3615 len. 28]: The Major pushed on with the dogs after a large male , and it stopped at bay under a mimosa , kicking most furiously at the dogs .
Parsing [sent. 3616 len. 16]: The Major leveled his rifle , and brought the animal down with his first shot .
Parsing [sent. 3617 len. 28]: It rose again , however , and for a hundred yards went away at a fast pace ; but it again fell , to rise no more .
Parsing [sent. 3618 len. 17]: The female which Alexander had wounded received another shot , and was then also prostrated . ''
Parsing [sent. 3619 len. 25]: `` I have killed a _ giraffe _ , '' said the Major , standing by the side of the one he had killed .
Parsing [sent. 3620 len. 62]: `` It has been a long way to travel , and there have been some dangers to encounter for the sake of performing this feat ; but we have all our follies , and are eager in pursuit of just as great trifles through life ; so that in this I am not perhaps more foolish than the rest of mankind .
Parsing [sent. 3621 len. 26]: I have obtained my wishes -- I have killed a giraffe ; and now I do n't care how soon we go back again . ''

Parsing [sent. 3624 len. 59]: As they were not more than two miles from the wagon , they rode back , and sent the Griquas to bring in the flesh of the animals ; Swinton not caring for the skins , as he had already procured some in Namaqua-land , and the weight of them would be so very great for the wagon .
