Parsing [sent. 3626 len. 12]: They , however , readily consented to return the next morning .
Parsing [sent. 3627 len. 15]: That night , a messenger arrived to the Matabili chief who was escorting them .
Parsing [sent. 3628 len. 24]: What was the communication of course our travelers could not tell ; but their suspicions were confirmed by the behavior of the man .
Parsing [sent. 3629 len. 74]: When he found that , on the following morning , they yoked the oxen and retraced their steps , he begged them not to go , but to advance into the interior of the country , where they would find plenty of game ; told them that the king would be very angry if they left so soon ; and if he did not see them , his heart would be very sad .
Parsing [sent. 3630 len. 19]: But our travelers had made up their mind , and traveled back during the whole of that day .
Parsing [sent. 3631 len. 42]: The Matabili dispatched the messenger who had come to him , and who again set off at all speed ; at night he urged our travelers not to go back , saying that the king would be very angry with him .
Parsing [sent. 3632 len. 36]: But as the Griquas were now equally convinced that treachery was intended , they paid no attention to the Matabili chief , and continued their route , shooting elands by the way for their sustenance .
Parsing [sent. 3633 len. 19]: Late in the evening of the third day they found themselves on the borders of the Val river .
Parsing [sent. 3634 len. 52]: It was still two hours before dark , and as the Matabili pressed them to encamp where they were , they were satisfied that they had better not , and therefore they forded the river , and rejoined the caravan , under charge of Bremen , just as night closed in .
Parsing [sent. 3635 len. 73]: The Griquas said , that from the Matabili wishing them to remain on the other side of the river , they were persuaded that a force would arrive during that night or the following morning , and that it would be necessary to be on the look-out ; although probably the enemy would not venture to attack them without further orders , now that they were no longer in Moselekatsee 's dominions .
Parsing [sent. 3636 len. 25]: Every preparation was therefore made : the Griquas and Hottentots were all supplied with ammunition , and mustered with their guns in their hands .
Parsing [sent. 3637 len. 20]: The wagons were arranged , the fires lighted , and four men were posted as sentinels round the encampment .
Parsing [sent. 3638 len. 26]: What added still more to their suspicions was , that , about an hour after dark , the Matabili chief was not to be found .
Parsing [sent. 3639 len. 21]: `` My opinion is , '' said the Major , `` that we ought to steal a march upon them .
Parsing [sent. 3640 len. 17]: Our oxen are in excellent condition , and may travel till to-morrow evening without feeling it .
Parsing [sent. 3641 len. 15]: Let us yoke and be off at once , now that it is dark .
Parsing [sent. 3642 len. 24]: The moon will rise about two o'clock in the morning , but before that the wagons will be twelve or fifteen miles off .
Parsing [sent. 3643 len. 39]: Alexander and I , with Bremen , will remain here with our horses and wait till the moon rises , to see if we can discover any thing : and we can easily join the wagons by daybreak .
Parsing [sent. 3644 len. 25]: We will keep the fires up , to allow them to suppose that we are still encamped , that they may not pursue . ''
Parsing [sent. 3645 len. 23]: `` And also to keep off the lions , '' observed Alexander , `` which are not enemies to be despised . ''
Parsing [sent. 3646 len. 19]: `` I think it is a very good plan ; but why not have more men with you ?
Parsing [sent. 3647 len. 13]: We have plenty of horses , and so have the Griquas . ''
Parsing [sent. 3648 len. 12]: `` Well then , let us talk to the Griquas . ''
Parsing [sent. 3649 len. 54]: The Griquas approved of the plan ; and , having their own horses , six of them agreed to remain with Alexander and the Major , and Swanevelt and two more of the Hottentots were also mounted to remain ; which made a force of twelve men , well mounted and well armed .
Parsing [sent. 3650 len. 45]: The remainder of the caravan yoked the oxen to the wagons , and , under the direction of Swinton , set off in a southerly direction , across the desert , instead of going by the banks of the Val River , as before .
Parsing [sent. 3651 len. 40]: This had been arranged previously to any expected attack from the Matabili , as it would considerably shorten the distance on returning , although they knew that they would find much difficulty in procuring water for a few days .
Parsing [sent. 3652 len. 44]: After the caravan had departed , it was found that Omrah had helped himself to a horse and a gun , and had remained in the camp ; but as he was always useful , his so doing was passed over without notice .
Parsing [sent. 3653 len. 24]: In half an hour the wagons were out of sight , and the noise of their wheels was no longer to be heard .
Parsing [sent. 3654 len. 46]: They fastened their horses in the center of the fires , and sat down by them till the moon rose , when they directed their eyes to the opposite bank of the river ; but for some time nothing was discovered to confirm their suspicions .
Parsing [sent. 3655 len. 34]: When the moon was about an hour high , they perceived a body of men coming , down toward the banks , and the moon shone upon their shields , which were white .
Parsing [sent. 3656 len. 22]: As soon as they arrived at the bank of the river , they all sat down , without making any noise .
Parsing [sent. 3657 len. 21]: Shortly afterward , another body with dark-colored shields , made their appearance , who came down and joined the first .
Parsing [sent. 3658 len. 40]: `` We were not wrong in our suspicions , at all events , '' said the Major ; `` I should say that there are not less than a thousand men in these two parties which have already appeared .
Parsing [sent. 3659 len. 7]: Now , what shall we do ?
Parsing [sent. 3660 len. 17]: Shall we remain here , or shall we be off , and join the wagons ? ''
Parsing [sent. 3661 len. 29]: `` I really can hardly decide which would be the best , '' replied Alexander ; `` let us have a consultation with Bremen and the Griquas . ''
Parsing [sent. 3662 len. 33]: `` If we were to go away now , '' said Bremen , `` the fires would soon be out , and they might suspect something , and come over to reconnoiter .
Parsing [sent. 3663 len. 44]: When they found that we were gone , they would perhaps follow us , and overtake the wagons ; but if we remain here , and keep the fires up till daybreak , the wagons will have gained so much more distance . ''
Parsing [sent. 3664 len. 25]: The Griquas were of the same opinion ; and it was decided that they would remain there till daybreak , and then set off .
Parsing [sent. 3665 len. 26]: `` But , '' said Alexander , `` shall we leave this before they can see us , or allow them to see us ? ''
Parsing [sent. 3666 len. 43]: The Griquas said , that it would be better that the enemy should see them , as then they would know that the fires had been kept up to deceive them , and that the wagons were probably a long way off .
Parsing [sent. 3667 len. 21]: This having been agreed upon , a careful watch was kept upon the enemy during the remainder of the night .
Parsing [sent. 3668 len. 43]: Although the moon had discovered the approach of the Matabili to the party , the spot where the camp had been pitched was in the shade , so that from the opposite side of the river only the fires could be distinguished .
Parsing [sent. 3669 len. 47]: A little before dawn , some one was heard approaching , and they were all prepared to fire , when they discovered that it was Omrah , who , unknown to them , had crawled down to the banks of the river to reconnoiter the enemy .
Parsing [sent. 3670 len. 39]: Omrah , who was out of breath with running , stated that some of the Matabili were crossing the river , and that six had landed on this side , before he came up to give the information .
Parsing [sent. 3671 len. 33]: He pointed to a clump of trees , about three hundred yards off , and said that they had gone up in that direction , and were probably there by that time .
Parsing [sent. 3672 len. 29]: `` Then we had better saddle and mount , '' said the Major , `` and ride away gently to the wood on this side of the camp .
Parsing [sent. 3673 len. 14]: We shall then be able to watch their motions without being seen . ''
Parsing [sent. 3674 len. 10]: This advice was good , and approved by all .
Parsing [sent. 3675 len. 29]: They led out their horses without noise , and as soon as they had done so , they went back , and threw more fuel on the fires .
Parsing [sent. 3676 len. 33]: They then retreated to the wood , which was about the same distance from the camp , on the other side , as the clump of trees where the Matabili were secreted .
Parsing [sent. 3677 len. 62]: They had hardly concealed themselves , before the Matabili in the clump , surprised at not seeing the awnings of the wagons , and suspecting that they had been deceived , came out from their ambuscade ; first crawling on all-fours , and as they arrived at the camp , and found only fires burning , rising up one after another .
Parsing [sent. 3678 len. 36]: After remaining about a minute in consultation , two of the party were sent back to the river to communicate this intelligence to the main body , while the others searched about in every direction .
Parsing [sent. 3679 len. 59]: Alexander , with the Major and their party , remained where they were , as it was their intention to cross through the wood , until they came to the open ground , about a quarter of a mile to the southward , and then show themselves to the enemy , before they went to join the wagons .
Parsing [sent. 3680 len. 24]: In a few minutes it was daylight , and they now perceived that the whole body of the Matabili were crossing the river .
Parsing [sent. 3681 len. 13]: `` They intend to pursue us , then , '' said Alexander .
Parsing [sent. 3682 len. 52]: Omrah now pointed to the side of the river , in the direction which the wagons had traveled when they came up by its banks , saying , `` When go away -- ride that way first -- same track wagon go that way back -- same way wagon come . ''
Parsing [sent. 3683 len. 61]: `` The boy is right , '' said the Major ; `` when we start from the wood , we will keep by the riverside , in the track by which the wagons came ; and when we are concealed from them by the hills or trees , we will then start off to the southward after the wagons . ''
Parsing [sent. 3684 len. 49]: `` I see , '' replied Alexander ; `` they will probably take the marks of the wagon-wheels coming here , for those of the wagons going away , and will follow them ; presuming , as we go that way , that our wagons have gone also .
Parsing [sent. 3685 len. 18]: But here they come up the banks ; it is time for us to be off . ''
Parsing [sent. 3686 len. 25]: `` Quite time , '' said the Major ; `` so now let us show ourselves , and then trust to our heels . ''
Parsing [sent. 3687 len. 13]: The Matabili force was now within four hundred yards of the camp .
Parsing [sent. 3688 len. 27]: It was broad daylight ; and , with their white and red shields and short spears in their hands , they presented a very formidable appearance .
Parsing [sent. 3689 len. 52]: There was no time to be lost , so the party rode out of the end of the wood nearest the river , and , as soon as they made their appearance , were received by a yell from the warriors , who dashed forward in the direction where they stood .
Parsing [sent. 3690 len. 25]: The Major had directed that no one should fire , as he and Alexander did not wish that any blood should be shed unnecessarily .
Parsing [sent. 3691 len. 34]: They therefore waved their hands , and turning their horses ' heads galloped off by the banks of the river , keeping in the tracks made by the wagons when they came up .
Parsing [sent. 3692 len. 24]: As soon as they galloped a quarter of a mile , they pulled up , and turned their horses ' heads to reconnoiter .
Parsing [sent. 3693 len. 35]: They perceived that the Matabili force was pursuing them at the utmost speed : but as they had no horsemen , that speed was of course insufficient to overtake the well-mounted party in advance .
Parsing [sent. 3694 len. 17]: As soon as they were near , our party again galloped off and left them behind .
Parsing [sent. 3695 len. 60]: Thus they continued for four or five miles , the Matabili force pursuing them , or rather following the tracks of the wagons , when they observed a belt of trees before them about a mile off ; this the Major considered as a good screen to enable them to alter their course without being perceived by the enemy .
Parsing [sent. 3696 len. 44]: They therefore galloped forward , and as soon as they were hidden by the trees , turned off in a direction by which they made certain to fall in with the track which the wagons had made on their departure during the night .
Parsing [sent. 3697 len. 38]: They had ridden about two miles , still concealed in the wood , when they had the satisfaction of perceiving the Matabili force still following at a rapid pace the tracks of the wagons on the riverside .
Parsing [sent. 3698 len. 32]: Having watched them for half an hour , as they now considered that all was safe , they again continued their course , so as to fall in with the wagons .
Parsing [sent. 3699 len. 33]: `` I think we are clear of them now , '' said the Major ; `` they have evidently fallen into the trap proposed by that clever little fellow , Omrah . ''
Parsing [sent. 3700 len. 27]: `` He is a very intelligent boy , '' observed Alexander , `` and , traveling in this country , worth his weight in gold . ''
Parsing [sent. 3701 len. 35]: `` I wish Swinton would make him over to me , '' said the Major ; `` but , Alexander , do you observe what a change there is already in the country ? ''
Parsing [sent. 3702 len. 26]: `` I do indeed , '' replied Alexander ; `` and all ahead of us it appears to be still more sterile and bare . ''
Parsing [sent. 3703 len. 17]: `` Yes , when you leave the rivers , you leave vegetation of all kinds almost .
Parsing [sent. 3704 len. 58]: There is no regular rainy season at all here , Swinton says ; we may expect occasional torrents of rain during three months , but they are now very uncertain ; the mountains attract the greater portion of the rain , and sometimes there will not be a shower on the plains for the whole year . ''
Parsing [sent. 3705 len. 17]: `` How far shall we have to travel before we fall in with water again ? ''
Parsing [sent. 3706 len. 3]: inquired Alexander .
Parsing [sent. 3707 len. 40]: `` Swinton says there may be water in a river about sixty miles from where we started last night ; if not , we shall have to proceed about thirty miles further , to the Gykoup or Vet River .
Parsing [sent. 3708 len. 39]: After that we shall have to depend for many days upon the water we may find in the holes , which , as the season is now coming on , may probably be filled by the rain . ''
Parsing [sent. 3709 len. 68]: Alexander and his party rode for seven or eight miles before they fell in with the tracks of the caravan ; they then pulled up their jaded horses , and proceeded at a more leisurely pace , so that it was not till late in the evening that they discovered the wagons at some distance , having passed the dry bed of Salt River ahead of them .
Parsing [sent. 3710 len. 27]: During the whole day their horses had had neither food nor water , and the animals were much exhausted when they came up with the wagons .
Parsing [sent. 3711 len. 23]: The oxen also were fatigued with so long a journey , having made nearly fifty miles since they started the evening before .
Parsing [sent. 3712 len. 36]: The country was now stony and sterile ; a little vegetation was to be found here and there , but not sufficient to meet the wants of the animals , and water there was none .
Parsing [sent. 3713 len. 23]: During the day but little game had been seen , -- few zebras and ostriches only ; all other varieties had disappeared .
Parsing [sent. 3714 len. 42]: There was of course no wood to light the fires round the encampment : a sufficiency for cooking their victuals had been thrown into the wagons , and two sheep were killed to supply a supper for so numerous a party .
Parsing [sent. 3715 len. 21]: But the absence of game also denoted the absence of lions , and they were not disturbed during the night .
Parsing [sent. 3716 len. 48]: In the morning the Griquas parted company with them , on the plea that their oxen and horses were in too poor a condition to pass over the desert , and that they must make a direct course for the Val River and return by its banks .
Parsing [sent. 3717 len. 38]: Our travelers gave them a good supply of ammunition , the only thing that they wished for , and the Griquas , yoking their oxen to the crazy old wagon , set off in a westerly direction .
Parsing [sent. 3718 len. 35]: The route of the caravan was now directed more to the south-west , and they passed over an uninterrupted plain strewed with small land-tortoises , and covered with a profusion of the gayest flowers .
Parsing [sent. 3719 len. 45]: About noon , after a sultry journey of nine hours , they fortunately arrived at a bog , in which they found a pool of most fetid water , which nothing but necessity could have compelled either them or the exhausted animals to drink .
Parsing [sent. 3720 len. 43]: Near this pool in the desert they found several wild animals , and they obtained three gnoos for a supply of provision ; the little wood that they had in the wagon for fuel was all used up in cooking their supper .
Parsing [sent. 3721 len. 26]: A heavy dew fell during the night , and in the morning , before the sun rose , they were enveloped in a thick fog .
Parsing [sent. 3722 len. 20]: As the fog dispersed , they perceived herds of quaggas in all directions , but at a great distance .
Parsing [sent. 3723 len. 30]: They again yoked the oxen and proceeded on their journey ; the country was now covered with herbage and flowers of every hue , and looked like a garden .
Parsing [sent. 3724 len. 26]: `` How strange that the ground should be covered with flowers where there is no rain or water to be found , '' observed Alexander .
Parsing [sent. 3725 len. 27]: `` It is the heavy dews of the night which support them , '' said Swinton , `` and perhaps the occasional rains which fall . ''
Parsing [sent. 3726 len. 56]: A line of trees to the southward told them that they were now approaching an unnamed river , and the tired oxen quickened their pace ; but on their arrival they found that the bed of the river was dry , and not even a drop of water was to be found in the pools .
Parsing [sent. 3727 len. 32]: The poor animals , which had been unyoked , snuffed and smelt at the wet , damp earth , and licked it with their tongues , but could obtain no relief .
Parsing [sent. 3728 len. 43]: The water which they had had in the casks for their own drinking was now , all gone ; and there were no hopes of obtaining any till they arrived at the Vet River , at least twenty-five to thirty miles distant .
Parsing [sent. 3729 len. 31]: Two of the oxen lay down to rise no more , the countenances of the Hottentots were dejected and sullen , and our travelers felt that their situation was alarming .
Parsing [sent. 3730 len. 36]: While they were still searching and digging for water , the sky became overcast , thunder and lightning were seen and heard in the distance , and the clouds came rolling in volumes toward them .
Parsing [sent. 3731 len. 40]: Hope was now in every face ; they already anticipated the copious showers which were to succeed ; their eyes ever fixed upon the coming storm ; even the cattle appeared to be conscious that relief was at hand .
Parsing [sent. 3732 len. 15]: All the day the clouds continued to gather , and the lightning to gleam .
Parsing [sent. 3733 len. 48]: Night closed in , but the rain had not yet fallen ; the wind rose up , and in less than an hour all the clouds had passed away , the stars shone out brightly , and they were left in a state of suffering and disappointment .
Parsing [sent. 3734 len. 3]: CHAPTER XXVII .
Parsing [sent. 3735 len. 103]: As our travelers were sitting together , each occupied with his own melancholy thoughts , after the dispersion of the clouds and the anticipated relief , the Major said -- `` It is useless our remaining here ; we must all perish if we do not proceed , and it would be better for us to yoke and travel by night ; the animals will bear the journey better , and the people will not be so inclined to brood over their misfortunes when on the march as when thus huddled together here , and communicating their lamentations to dishearten each other .
Parsing [sent. 3736 len. 19]: It is now nine o'clock ; let us yoke and push on as far as we can . ''
Parsing [sent. 3737 len. 21]: `` I agree with you , Major , '' said Alexander ; `` what do you say , Swinton ? ''
Parsing [sent. 3738 len. 22]: `` I am convinced that it will be the best plan , so let us rouse up the people at once .
Parsing [sent. 3739 len. 22]: There is the roar of a lion at some distance , and we have no fires to scare them off . ''
Parsing [sent. 3740 len. 17]: `` The creaking of the wagon-wheels will be better than nothing , '' replied the Major .
Parsing [sent. 3741 len. 39]: The Hottentots were roused , and the orders given to yoke : the poor fellows were all sound asleep ; for a Hottentot , when he hungers or thirsts , seeks refuge from all his miseries in sleep .
Parsing [sent. 3742 len. 55]: The oxen were yoked , and they proceeded ; but hardly had they gone a mile , when the roar of three or four lions , close upon them , caused such alarm to the horses and the oxen which were not yoked that they started off in full gallop in a northerly direction .
Parsing [sent. 3743 len. 41]: Alexander , the Major , and Omrah , who were the best mounted , immediately set off in pursuit of them , desiring Swinton to proceed with the caravan , and they would drive on the cattle and join him .
Parsing [sent. 3744 len. 32]: They galloped off as well as the horses could gallop , and perceived the stray horses and oxen still at full speed , as if they were chased by the lions .
Parsing [sent. 3745 len. 54]: They followed in the direction , but it was now so dark that they were guided only by the clatter of their hoofs and their shoes in the distance ; and after a chase of four or five miles they had lost all vestiges of them , and pulled up their panting steeds .
Parsing [sent. 3746 len. 23]: `` We may as well go back again , '' said Alexander ; `` the animals must have made a circuit . ''
Parsing [sent. 3747 len. 51]: `` I suppose so , '' said the Major ; `` but my horse trembles so , that I had better dismount for a little while , that he may recover himself ; indeed , so had you too and Omrah , for the animals are completely worn out . ''
Parsing [sent. 3748 len. 25]: `` The clouds are rising again , '' said Alexander ; `` I trust that we may not be disappointed a second time . ''
Parsing [sent. 3749 len. 26]: `` Yes , and there is lightning again in the horizon -- may the Almighty help us in our distress , '' exclaimed the Major .
Parsing [sent. 3750 len. 36]: The horses , exhausted from want of water , continued to pant so fearfully , that it was nearly half an hour before they ventured to mount , that they might return to the caravan .
Parsing [sent. 3751 len. 31]: In the meantime the heavens had become wholly obscured by the clouds , and there was every prospect of a heavy shower ; at last a few drops did fall .
Parsing [sent. 3752 len. 5]: `` Thank God ! ''
Parsing [sent. 3753 len. 21]: exclaimed Alexander , as he lifted his face up to the heavens , to feel the drops as they fell .
Parsing [sent. 3754 len. 7]: `` Now let us return . ''
Parsing [sent. 3755 len. 36]: They mounted their horses and set off , but the stars were no longer visible to guide them , and they proceeded on at a slow pace , uncertain whether they were right or wrong .
Parsing [sent. 3756 len. 20]: This they cared little about ; their thoughts were upon the coming rain , which they so anxiously awaited .
Parsing [sent. 3757 len. 40]: For more than three hours they were tantalized by the lightning flashing and the thunder pealing , every moment expecting the flood-gate of the heavens to be opened ; but , as before , they were doomed to disappointment .
Parsing [sent. 3758 len. 35]: Before the morning dawned the clouds had again retreated ; and when the sky was clear , they found by the stars that their horses ' heads were turned to the northward and eastward .
Parsing [sent. 3759 len. 39]: They altered their course in silence , for they were worn out and despondent ; they suffered dreadfully from thirst , and it was pitiable to see the tongues of the poor horses hanging out of their mouths .
Parsing [sent. 3760 len. 12]: Day dawned , and there were no signs of the caravan .
Parsing [sent. 3761 len. 41]: A thick vapor was rising from every quarter , and they hoped that when it cleared up they would be more fortunate ; but no , there was the same monotonous landscape , the same carpet of flowers without perfume .
Parsing [sent. 3762 len. 56]: The sun was now three hours high , and the heat was intense ; their tongues clove to the roofs of their mouths , while still they went on over flowery meads ; but neither forest nor pool , nor any trees which might denote the bed of the river , caught their earnest gaze .
Parsing [sent. 3763 len. 16]: `` This is dreadful , '' said Alexander , at last , speaking with difficulty .
Parsing [sent. 3764 len. 23]: `` We are lost , that is certain , '' said the Major ; `` but we must trust in God . ''
Parsing [sent. 3765 len. 20]: `` Yes , we may now say , Lord help us , or we perish , '' replied Alexander .
Parsing [sent. 3766 len. 43]: At this moment , little Omrah , who had been behind , rode up to them , and offered them one of the Hottentots ' pipes , which he had lighted saying , `` Smoke , -- not feel so bad . ''
Parsing [sent. 3767 len. 39]: Alexander took it , and after a few whiffs found that it had the effect or producing a little saliva , and he handed it to the Major , who did the same , and felt immediate relief .
Parsing [sent. 3768 len. 33]: They continued to walk their horses in a southerly direction ; but the heat was now so great , that it became almost insufferable , and at last the horses stood still .
Parsing [sent. 3769 len. 27]: They dismounted and drove their horses slowly before them over the glowing plain ; and now the mirage deluded and tantalized them in the strangest manner .
Parsing [sent. 3770 len. 42]: At one time Alexander pointed with delight -LRB- for he could not speak -RRB- to what he imagined to be the wagons ; they pushed on , and found that it was a solitary quagga , magnified thus by the mirage .
Parsing [sent. 3771 len. 66]: Sometimes they thought that they saw lakes of water in the distance , and hastened on to them ; and then they fancied they were close to rivers and islands , covered with luxuriant foliage , but still were doomed to disappointment ; as all was the result of the highly-rarefied air , and the refraction of the sun 's rays on the sultry plain .
Parsing [sent. 3772 len. 40]: What would they have given for a bush even to afford them any shelter from the noonday sun , for the crowns of their heads appeared as if covered with live coal , and their minds began to wander .
Parsing [sent. 3773 len. 27]: The poor horses moved at the slowest pace , and only when driven on by Omrah , who appeared to suffer much less than his masters .
Parsing [sent. 3774 len. 22]: Every now and then he handed to them the pipe , but at last even that had no longer any relief .
Parsing [sent. 3775 len. 9]: Speech had been for some hours totally lost .
Parsing [sent. 3776 len. 27]: Gradually the sun sunk down to the horizon , and as his scorching rays became less intense they to a certain degree recovered their wandering senses .
Parsing [sent. 3777 len. 56]: At night they sat down by the side of the horses , and , worn out with fatigue and exhaustion , fell into a troubled sleep ; a sleep which , if it relieved their worn-out frames , condemned them to the same tantalizing feelings as had been created by the mirage during the day .
Parsing [sent. 3778 len. 36]: They dreamed that they were in the bowers of paradise , hearing heavenly music ; passing from crystal stream to stream , slaking their thirst at each , and reclining on couches of verdant green .
Parsing [sent. 3779 len. 57]: Every thing that was delightful appeared to them in their dreams ; they were in the abodes of bliss , and thus did they remain for an hour or two , when they were wakened up by the roar of a lion , which reminded them that they were without food or water in the desert .
Parsing [sent. 3780 len. 32]: They awoke speechless with thirst , their eyes inflamed , and their whole bodies burning like a coal , and the awful roar of the lion still reverberated along the ground .
Parsing [sent. 3781 len. 26]: They started on their legs , and found Omrah close to them , holding the bridles of the horses , which were attempting to escape .
Parsing [sent. 3782 len. 48]: They were still confused , when they were fully restored to their waking senses by a second roar of the lion still nearer to them ; and by the imperfect light of the stars they could now distinguish the beast at about one hundred yards ' distance .
Parsing [sent. 3783 len. 28]: Omrah put the bridles of their two horses in their hands , and motioned them to go on in the direction opposite to where the lion was .
Parsing [sent. 3784 len. 24]: They did so without reflection , mechanically obeying the directions of the man-child , and not perceiving that Omrah did not follow them .
Parsing [sent. 3785 len. 39]: They had advanced about one hundred yards with the terrified animals , when another loud roar was followed up by the shriek of the other horse , announcing that he had become a victim to the savage animal .
Parsing [sent. 3786 len. 60]: They both started , and dropping the reins of their horses , hastened with their rifles to the help of Omrah , of whose absence they now for the first time were aware ; but they were met half-way by the boy , who contrived to say with difficulty , `` Lion want horse , not little Bushman . ''
Parsing [sent. 3787 len. 50]: They waited a few seconds , but the cries of the poor animal , and the crushing and cracking of its bones , were too painful to hear ; and they hastened on and rejoined the other horses , which appeared paralyzed with fear , and had remained stationary .
Parsing [sent. 3788 len. 36]: They again led their horses on for an hour , when they arrived at a small pile of rocks ; there they again lay down , for they were quite exhausted and careless of life .
Parsing [sent. 3789 len. 36]: Not even the roar of a lion would have aroused them now , or if it had roused them they would have waited for the animal to come and put an end to their misery .
Parsing [sent. 3790 len. 29]: But another and a softer noise attracted the quick ear of Omrah , and he pushed Alexander , and put his finger up to induce him to listen .
Parsing [sent. 3791 len. 18]: Having listened a little longer , Omrah made signs to Alexander and the Major to follow him .
Parsing [sent. 3792 len. 31]: The noise which Omrah had heard was the croaking of a frog , which denoted water at hand , and the sniffing of the horses confirmed him in his supposition .
Parsing [sent. 3793 len. 49]: Omrah led the way through the rocks , descending lower and lower ; and ever and anon listening to the noise of the animal , till he perceived the stars of heaven above reflected in a small pool , which he pointed out to Alexander and the Major .
Parsing [sent. 3794 len. 90]: Down they dropped to earth and drank , and as soon as their thirst was satisfied they rose , and pushed Omrah forward to make him drink also ; and as the boy who had saved their lives was drinking , they kneeled down and prayed -- not loud , for they had not yet recovered their speech ; but if ever grateful prayers were offered up to the Almighty throne , they were by our two travelers , as they kneeled by the side of this small pool .
Parsing [sent. 3795 len. 35]: They rose and hastened to their horses , and led them down to the water , when the poor animals filled themselves almost to bursting , walked away , and returned to drink more .
Parsing [sent. 3796 len. 60]: They also repeated their draught several times , and then lay down , and would have fallen asleep by the side of the pool had not Omrah , who could now speak freely , said , `` No , no ; lion come here for water ; up the rock again and sleep there -- I bring horses . ''
Parsing [sent. 3797 len. 26]: This good advice was followed , and when they had gained the summit of the rising ground they again lay down and slept till daylight .
Parsing [sent. 3798 len. 33]: When they awoke , they found themselves much refreshed , but they now felt -- what they had not done during their extreme suffering from thirst -- the craving pangs of hunger .
Parsing [sent. 3799 len. 19]: Omrah was fast asleep , and the horses picking among the herbage , about two hundred yards off .
Parsing [sent. 3800 len. 16]: `` We have much to thank God for , '' said Alexander to the Major .
Parsing [sent. 3801 len. 20]: `` We have indeed , and , next to divine aid , we have to thank that poor boy .
Parsing [sent. 3802 len. 24]: We have been as children in his hands , and we are indebted to him and his resources for our lives this night .
Parsing [sent. 3803 len. 30]: I could not speak yesterday , nor could you ; but his courage in remaining with the horse as an offering to the lion I shall not forget . ''
Parsing [sent. 3804 len. 55]: `` He is a child of the desert , '' replied Alexander ; `` he has been brought up among lions , and where there is scarcity of water , and he has most wonderfully guided us in our path ; but we are still in the desert , and have lost our companions .
Parsing [sent. 3805 len. 5]: What must we do ?
Parsing [sent. 3806 len. 24]: Shall we attempt to regain the caravan , or push off to the westward , to fall in with the river again ? ''
Parsing [sent. 3807 len. 47]: `` We will talk of this an hour hence , '' replied the Major ; `` let us now go down to the pool , and as soon as I have had a drink I will try if I can not kill something for a meal .
Parsing [sent. 3808 len. 13]: My hunger is now almost as great as was my thirst . ''
Parsing [sent. 3809 len. 38]: `` And mine too , so I will go with you ; but we must be careful how we approach the water , as we may fall in with some animal to make a meal of . ''
Parsing [sent. 3810 len. 31]: `` Or with a lion , ready to make a meal of us , '' replied the Major ; `` so in either instance we must approach it cautiously . ''
Parsing [sent. 3811 len. 19]: As they walked to the pool , they discovered the head of an antelope just above a rock .
Parsing [sent. 3812 len. 9]: The Major fired , and the animal fell .
Parsing [sent. 3813 len. 29]: The report of the rifle was answered by a roar ; three lions bounded away from the rock , and went at a quick canter over the plain .
Parsing [sent. 3814 len. 34]: `` Both our suppositions have proved correct , '' observed Alexander , as they walked up to where the antelope lay dead ; `` but how are we to cook the animal ? ''
Parsing [sent. 3815 len. 28]: `` Any dry stuff will serve for a fire , if we can only get enough , and a very little cooking will serve us just now .
Parsing [sent. 3816 len. 4]: Here comes Omrah .
Parsing [sent. 3817 len. 23]: Let us carry the game up to where we slept last night , as soon as we have had a drink . ''
Parsing [sent. 3818 len. 26]: They went to the pool , and were surprised to behold the filthy puddle which had appeared to them so like nectar the night before .
Parsing [sent. 3819 len. 17]: They were not sufficiently thirsty to overcome their disgust , and they turned away from it .
Parsing [sent. 3820 len. 51]: Omrah now began collecting dried grass , and herbs , and lichen from the rocks , and had soon a sufficiency to make a small fire ; they struck a light , and cutting off steaks from the antelope , were in a short time very busy at the repast .
Parsing [sent. 3821 len. 29]: When their hunger was appeased , they found that their thirst was renewed , and they went down to the pool , and shutting their eyes drank plentifully .
Parsing [sent. 3822 len. 52]: Omrah cooked as much of the meat as the small fire would permit , that they might not want for the next twenty-four hours ; and the horses being again led to the water to drink , they mounted , and proceeded to the southward , followed by Omrah on foot .
Parsing [sent. 3823 len. 12]: Another day was passed in searching for the caravan without success .
Parsing [sent. 3824 len. 7]: No water was to be found .
Parsing [sent. 3825 len. 33]: The heat was dreadful ; and at night they threw themselves down on the ground , careless of life ; and had it not been sinful they would have prayed for death .
Parsing [sent. 3826 len. 33]: The next morning they arose in a state of dreadful suffering ; they could not speak , but they made signs , and resolved once more to attempt to join the caravan .
Parsing [sent. 3827 len. 23]: They proceeded during the whole of the forenoon in the direction by which they hoped to discover the tracks of the wagons .
Parsing [sent. 3828 len. 16]: The heat was overpowering , and they felt all the agony of the day before .
Parsing [sent. 3829 len. 26]: At last the horses could proceed no further ; they both lay down , and our travelers had little hopes of their ever rising again .
Parsing [sent. 3830 len. 41]: The scorching of the sun 's rays was so dreadful , that they thrust their heads into some empty ant-hills to keep off the heat , and there they lay in as forlorn and hopeless a state as the horses .
Parsing [sent. 3831 len. 35]: Speak they could not ; their parched tongues rattled like boards against the roofs of their mouths ; their lips were swollen and bloated , and their eyes inflamed and starting from the sockets .
Parsing [sent. 3832 len. 62]: As Alexander afterward said to Swinton , he then recollected the thoughts which had risen in his mind on his departure from the English shore , and the surmise whether he might not leave his bones bleaching in the desert ; and Alexander now believed that such was to be the case , and he prayed mentally and prepared for death .
Parsing [sent. 3833 len. 39]: The Major was fully possessed of the same idea ; but as they lay at some yards ' distance , with their heads buried in the ant-hills , they could not communicate with each other even by signs .
Parsing [sent. 3834 len. 14]: At last they fell into a state of stupor and lost all recollection .
Parsing [sent. 3835 len. 51]: But an Almighty Providence watched over them , and during their state of insensibility the clouds again rose and covered the firmament , and this time they did not rise in mockery ; for , before the day was closed , torrents descended from them and deluged the whole plain .
Parsing [sent. 3836 len. 65]: Omrah , who had held up better than his masters , crawled out of the ant-hill into which he had crept ; and as soon as the rain descended , he contrived to pull the heads of the Major and Alexander , who still remained senseless , from out of the ant-hills , and to turn their blackened and swollen faces to the sky .
Parsing [sent. 3837 len. 27]: As their clothes became saturated with the rain and the water poured into their mouths , they gradually revived , and at last were completely restored .
Parsing [sent. 3838 len. 30]: The wind now rose and blew fresh , and before morning they were shivering with cold , and when they attempted to get up found their limbs were cramped .
Parsing [sent. 3839 len. 32]: Soon after daylight the rain ceased , and they were glad to bask in the then cheering rays of the sun , which had nearly destroyed them on the day before .
Parsing [sent. 3840 len. 30]: The horses had recovered their legs and were feeding close to them ; and the flesh of the antelope , which had been untasted , was now greedily devoured .
Parsing [sent. 3841 len. 49]: Most devoutly did they return thanks for their preservation , and the hopes which were now held out to them of ultimately regaining the colony ; for they had abandoned all hopes of reaching the caravan , as they considered the risk of crossing the desert too great .
Parsing [sent. 3842 len. 24]: They made up their minds to push for the Val River as fast as they could , and proceed back by its banks .
Parsing [sent. 3843 len. 52]: They had two horses , and Omrah could ride behind one of them , when he was tired ; they had guns and ammunition , and although they were fully aware of the dangers to which they would be exposed , they thought lightly of them after what they had suffered .
Parsing [sent. 3844 len. 25]: They now mounted their horses , and proceeded at a slow pace toward the westward , for the poor animals were still very weak .
Parsing [sent. 3845 len. 20]: At sunset they had traveled about ten miles , and looked out for a spot to pass the night .
Parsing [sent. 3846 len. 35]: Wood to light fires they had none , but they hoped , if their horses were not taken away by the lions , to reach a branch of the river by the following evening .
Parsing [sent. 3847 len. 37]: There was now no want of water , as they repeatedly passed by small pools , which , for a day or two at least , would not be evaporated by the heat of the sun .
Parsing [sent. 3848 len. 48]: But they knew that by that time , if no more rain fell , they would have again to undergo the former terrible privations , and therefore resolved upon continuing their course toward the river as their safest plan , now that they had lost the caravan .
Parsing [sent. 3849 len. 72]: As they were seated on a rising ground which they had chosen for their night 's rest , and occasionally firing off their rifles to drive away the lions which were heard prowling about ; all of a sudden Omrah cried out , and pointed to the northward ; our travelers turned and perceived a rocket ascending the firmament , and at last breaking out into a group of brilliant stars .
Parsing [sent. 3850 len. 25]: `` It is the caravan , '' exclaimed the Major ; `` Swinton has remembered that I put some rockets into my wagon . ''
Parsing [sent. 3851 len. 16]: `` We must have passed it , '' said Alexander , springing on his feet .
Parsing [sent. 3852 len. 10]: `` God be praised for all his mercies . ''
Parsing [sent. 3853 len. 9]: `` Amen , '' replied the Major devoutly .
Parsing [sent. 3854 len. 31]: Omrah ran after the horses , which were feeding close to them , for their instinctive fear of the lions made them keep as close as possible to their masters .
Parsing [sent. 3855 len. 26]: They were soon mounted , with Omrah behind the Major , and set off at all the speed that they could obtain from the animals .
Parsing [sent. 3856 len. 24]: After an interval another rocket was seen , and by its light they discovered that they were not a mile from the wagons .
Parsing [sent. 3857 len. 59]: The horses appeared to be sensible of this , and went off at a quicker pace ; and in a few minutes they had rushed in among the cattle , and Alexander and the Major were received into the arms of Swinton , and surrounded by the Hottentots , who were loud in their congratulations at their return .
Parsing [sent. 3858 len. 54]: As soon as Alexander and the Major had made known their perils and sufferings to Swinton , the latter informed them that about three hours after they had left the caravan in pursuit of the cattle , the animals had returned , that of course , he had fully expected them to follow .
Parsing [sent. 3859 len. 64]: Finding that they did not arrive , he had decided upon remaining where he was , at all events , for another day ; but that the cattle were by that time so exhausted , that it was with difficulty they were moved , and he could not proceed with them more than ten miles , when they lay down in their yokes .
Parsing [sent. 3860 len. 28]: Thirteen had died , and the others must have shared their fate , if it had not been for the providential rain , which had restored them .
Parsing [sent. 3861 len. 39]: Swinton stated that he had been in a great state of alarm for them , and that he had almost satisfied himself that they had perished , although he had used every means that he could think of .
Parsing [sent. 3862 len. 21]: When he fired the rockets off , he had scarcely a hope of thus bringing them back to the caravan .
Parsing [sent. 3863 len. 30]: `` However , '' observed Swinton , `` it shows that we should never despair , and never leave a chance untried , even in the most desperate circumstances .
Parsing [sent. 3864 len. 31]: You are back again , and I thank the Almighty for it with all my heart and all my soul and all my strength , most fervently and most sincerely .
Parsing [sent. 3865 len. 17]: I have been very , very miserable , I can assure you , my dear fellows .
Parsing [sent. 3866 len. 12]: The idea of returning to the Cape without you was dreadful .
Parsing [sent. 3867 len. 24]: Indeed , I never would have left the country until I had found you , or had some clew to your deaths . ''
Parsing [sent. 3868 len. 29]: `` Our preservation has indeed been miraculous , '' replied the Major ; `` I never thought to have raised my head out of the ant-hill again . ''
Parsing [sent. 3869 len. 24]: `` Nor I , '' replied Alexander ; `` and next to the Almighty , we certainly owe our lives to little Omrah .
Parsing [sent. 3870 len. 24]: There is nothing that I would not do for that boy , if you will only give him over to my care . ''
Parsing [sent. 3871 len. 11]: `` Or mine , Swinton , '' replied the Major .
Parsing [sent. 3872 len. 43]: `` Depend upon it , '' replied Swinton , `` I will do all for him that ought to be done ; I owe him a debt of gratitude for preserving my friends , and will not forget to repay it . ''
Parsing [sent. 3873 len. 19]: `` Well then , you must allow us to help him as well , '' replied the Major .
Parsing [sent. 3874 len. 12]: `` How far are we now from the Modder River ? ''
Parsing [sent. 3875 len. 46]: `` About forty miles , I should think , and we had better push on as fast as we , can ; for although the river will contain water , the pools in the desert between us and the river will soon be dried up .
Parsing [sent. 3876 len. 24]: The cattle , however , are still very weak , and , as I have stated , we have lost all our relays .
Parsing [sent. 3877 len. 29]: But you must long to have a good night 's rest , so go to your wagons , and we will watch and keep off the wild beasts .
Parsing [sent. 3878 len. 32]: We have been obliged to fire our guns all night long since your absence , and have burned one of the spare poles of the wagons to cook our victuals . ''
Parsing [sent. 3879 len. 5]: Every thing is comparative .
Parsing [sent. 3880 len. 54]: When our travelers first took up their night 's lodgings in the wagons they found their resting-places hard , after sleeping in comfortable beds at Cape Town ; but now , after having passed their nights in the wild desert , their mattresses in the wagons were a luxury that was fully appreciated .
Parsing [sent. 3881 len. 41]: Returning thanks to Heaven for their preservation , Alexander and the Major slept soundly till morning , notwithstanding that the latter was often half roused by the importunities of Begum , who appeared delighted at the return of her master .
Parsing [sent. 3882 len. 14]: At daylight the oxen were yoked , and they proceeded on their journey .
Parsing [sent. 3883 len. 24]: There was no want of game ; indeed they were so plentiful , that they shot them from the caravan as they passed .
Parsing [sent. 3884 len. 47]: At night they had made twenty-five miles , and before they had unyoked , a deluge of rain again fell , and they passed a very uncomfortable night , as it was very cold , and they could light no fires , from want of fuel .
Parsing [sent. 3885 len. 51]: Any thing , however , was better than the want of water ; and early in the morning they again yoked their oxen , and , after a hard day 's toil , were rejoiced to perceive at a distance the trees which lined the banks of the Modder River .
Parsing [sent. 3886 len. 37]: The sight was hailed with joy by the Hottentots , who shouted aloud ; for they considered their dangers and difficulties to be over , now that they were approaching to the boundaries of the colony .
Parsing [sent. 3887 len. 3]: CHAPTER XXVIII .
Parsing [sent. 3888 len. 31]: As the cattle required some repose , after the sufferings they had gone through , our travelers resolved to remain a few days on the banks of the Modder River .
Parsing [sent. 3889 len. 9]: The pasturage was fine and the game abundant .
Parsing [sent. 3890 len. 32]: Gnoos and springboks were to be seen in every direction , and quaggas , bonteboks , and several other varieties of antelopes , were in profusion over the now undulating country .
Parsing [sent. 3891 len. 29]: Neither were our travelers sorry to have some repose for themselves , although every mile that they drew nearer to the Cape made them more anxious to return .
Parsing [sent. 3892 len. 52]: As usual , the caravan was halted on a rising ground , at some distance from the river , to avoid the wild beasts , which during the day were concealed , and during the night prowled on its banks , to spring upon the animals which came down for water .
Parsing [sent. 3893 len. 25]: As there was now plenty of wood , the fires were again lighted at night , and the oxen driven in and tied up .
Parsing [sent. 3894 len. 25]: During the day , the animals reveled on the luxurious pasture , and in a week had become quite sleek and in good condition .
Parsing [sent. 3895 len. 23]: Every day our travelers went out to hunt for a supply of provisions , and never returned without more than was sufficient .
Parsing [sent. 3896 len. 17]: Swinton was anxious to possess one or two more specimens of the oryx , or gemsbok .
Parsing [sent. 3897 len. 44]: This antelope , we have before observed , from having very straight horns , which at a distance appear as one , has given rise to the fabulous animal the unicorn , which is now one of the supporters of the royal arms .
Parsing [sent. 3898 len. 26]: It is a very formidable animal ; being the one that our travelers found with its horns pierced through the lion which had attacked it .
Parsing [sent. 3899 len. 47]: The horses being now fresh and in good heart , Alexander and the Major went in pursuit of this animal very often , but without success , as the chase was continually interrupted by the herds of ostriches and other game which fell in their way .
Parsing [sent. 3900 len. 53]: One morning , having discovered with the telescope that three of these gemsbok were some miles distant on a rising ground , they set off , accompanied by a portion of the Hottentots on foot , who were desired to go round , so as to drive the animals toward the camp .
Parsing [sent. 3901 len. 69]: Bremen and Big Adam were of the party , and they had made a circuit of three or four miles , so as to get on the other side of the game , which now darted down from the high ground , and , descending on the plain , stopped for a while looking at their pursuers , while the horsemen advanced toward them in the opposite direction .
Parsing [sent. 3902 len. 61]: A shot from Alexander at last brought one of these splendid animals to the ground , while the others fled off to a distance , so as to give no hopes of again coming up with them ; and the party on foot , as well as the horsemen , now proceeded to the spot where the gemsbok lay dead .
Parsing [sent. 3903 len. 46]: As Swinton wanted the animal for a specimen , it was placed on the back of the horse which Omrah rode as usual , and one of the Hottentots went off with it to the camp , which was not more than three miles distant .
Parsing [sent. 3904 len. 83]: They were debating whether they should make an attempt to get near to the other gemsbok , which were still in sight at a distance , or try for some other game , when they perceived three lions not far from them on a rising ground ; and suddenly the horses , from which they had dismounted to give them time to recover their wind , broke loose from the Hottentots who held the bridles , and galloped away toward the camp .
Parsing [sent. 3905 len. 31]: The cause of the panic was now evident , for a very large male lion had detached himself from the other two , and was advancing slowly toward the party .
Parsing [sent. 3906 len. 82]: As soon as they perceived the approach of the lion , which they had not at first , they all seized their guns ; but being wholly unprepared for such a sudden attack , there was a great deal of confusion ; the Major crying out , `` Let no one fire till I tell him , '' only produced more alarm among the Hottentots , all of whom , except Bremen , appeared to be at their wits ' ends .
Parsing [sent. 3907 len. 47]: When within fifty yards , the lion made one or two bounds , and in a moment was among them all , before they could bring their guns to their shoulders ; the retreat was general in every direction , and not a shot was fired .
Parsing [sent. 3908 len. 51]: All , however , did not escape ; Big Adam had started back , and coming with all his force against Omrah , who was standing behind him , had fallen over the boy , and they were both flat on their backs , when the lion made his spring .
Parsing [sent. 3909 len. 74]: The lion was standing up , looking proudly at his flying enemies , when Big Adam , who was close to him , attempted to rise and gain his feet ; but perceiving this , the animal , with a blow of its fore-paw , laid him prostrate again , set its foot upon his breast , and in this attitude again looked proudly round him , as if confident of his superiority .
Parsing [sent. 3910 len. 65]: Omrah , who had sense enough to lie still , had yet his eyes sufficiently opened to see what was going on ; and as the lion appeared to be looking at the scattered party , in a direction away from him , Omrah made one or two turns over , so as to get further off , hoping that he might escape unperceived .
Parsing [sent. 3911 len. 22]: The lion , however , heard the rustling , and turning round growled at him , and Omrah remained still again .
Parsing [sent. 3912 len. 60]: As Big Adam 's feet were turned toward Omrah , the lion now took up his position , deliberately lying down at full length upon Big Adam 's body , with his hind-quarters upon the Hottentot 's face , so that he not only secured his prisoner , but watched Omrah , who lay about three yards from him .
Parsing [sent. 3913 len. 25]: In the mean time the anxiety of the other party may be imagined ; they considered that Big Adam and Omrah must be sacrificed .
Parsing [sent. 3914 len. 37]: It was proposed to fire with good aim , so as , if possible , to bring the animal 's attention and indignation upon themselves ; but Swinton cried out not to fire on any account .
Parsing [sent. 3915 len. 14]: `` The animal is not hungry or even angry , '' said Swinton .
Parsing [sent. 3916 len. 15]: `` If let alone , he will probably walk away without doing them injury .
Parsing [sent. 3917 len. 15]: At all events , our firing will be the signal for their destruction . ''
Parsing [sent. 3918 len. 54]: The advice of Swinton was considered good , especially as it was backed by that of Bremen , who also said that the lion was not hungry , and that , by the way in which he , moved his tail , he was evidently more inclined to play than any thing else .
Parsing [sent. 3919 len. 55]: But in the mean time the pressure of the lion , whose weight was enormous , was not only more than Big Adam could bear , but the hind-quarters of the animal being over his face prevented him from breathing ; and at last he was compelled to struggle to get his head clear .
Parsing [sent. 3920 len. 55]: The consequence of his struggling was a severe bite on the leg , inflicted on poor Adam ; not , however , in a furious manner ; for the lion merely caught at him as a cat would at a mouse , to prevent its escape , or because it was not quite dead .
Parsing [sent. 3921 len. 46]: However , Big Adam had so far disengaged his head that he could now breathe ; and as the party kept crying out to him to lie still , he continued so to do , although nearly suffocated with the enormous weight of the animal .
Parsing [sent. 3922 len. 58]: Omrah , who had remained still during all this time , perceiving that the lion was licking the blood which flowed from the wound in Big Adam 's leg , thought that he might as well try another roll over , and being on his back , he turned over on his face away from the lion .
Parsing [sent. 3923 len. 93]: Thereupon the lion rose from off Big Adam , walked up to Omrah , and , to the horror of our travelers , took up the boy by his waistcloth , and , carrying him like a small bundle in his mouth , went back to Big Adam , and laying Omrah close down to the Hottentot 's head , again took up his position on his body ; now , however , with his paws upon the Hottentot 's breast , so that he might keep Omrah in view before him .
Parsing [sent. 3924 len. 24]: Little Omrah had sense enough not to move during the time that the lion carried him , or after he was laid down .
Parsing [sent. 3925 len. 43]: The change in the position of the lion occasioned our travelers and the party to walk round , so as to be able to watch the countenance of the animal , as every thing depended upon the temper he might be in .
Parsing [sent. 3926 len. 29]: The Major and Alexander became very impatient , and were for advancing to the attack , but Swinton persuaded them not to do so until the last moment .
Parsing [sent. 3927 len. 62]: The lion now put its fore-paw upon the Hottentot 's mouth , and again stopped his breath ; this occasioned another struggle on the part of Big Adam , which was followed by the animal seizing him by the arm and biting him severely ; but in so doing the lion removed its paw , and the man could breathe again .
Parsing [sent. 3928 len. 43]: The taste of blood appeared pleasant to the lion , for it continued biting the arm , descending from the shoulder to the hand , and as the blood flowed from the wounds on its paws , the lion licked it off .
Parsing [sent. 3929 len. 60]: Again and again it licked its paw clean , and then , with its glaring eyes fixed intently upon the Hottentot 's face , it smelt him first on one side and then on the other , and appeared only to be waiting for a return of appetite to commence a deliberate meal upon the poor fellow 's body .
Parsing [sent. 3930 len. 44]: In the mean time our travelers were standing about seventy yards distant , waiting for the signal to attack , when Bremen observed to Swinton -- `` He wo n't wait much longer , sir ; the blood has given him an appetite .
Parsing [sent. 3931 len. 15]: We must now drive him away , or they will both be killed . ''
Parsing [sent. 3932 len. 32]: `` I think so too , '' replied Swinton ; `` let us first try if we can disturb him without making him angry ; that will be the best way .
Parsing [sent. 3933 len. 22]: We must go back out of springing distance , and then all shout together , and keep hallooing at him . ''
Parsing [sent. 3934 len. 30]: This advice was followed ; they retreated a hundred yards , and then all shouted at once , and after that the Hottentots hallooed and bawled to the lion .
Parsing [sent. 3935 len. 36]: This had the effect intended : the lion rose from the bodies and advanced toward the party , who stood still hallooing at him , but not attempting to irritate him by presenting their guns .
Parsing [sent. 3936 len. 15]: The lion looked steadfastly at them for some time , and then turned away .
Parsing [sent. 3937 len. 46]: After retreating a few steps , it turned back to face them ; the whole party continued on the same spot , neither advancing so as to irritate him , nor retreating so as to let the animal suppose that they were afraid of him .
Parsing [sent. 3938 len. 46]: When the lion had continued for a few minutes this course of retreating and advancing , he turned right round , and went away at a hand canter , and our travelers immediately hastened to the spot where Big Adam and Omrah were still lying .
Parsing [sent. 3939 len. 83]: Omrah , who was not at all hurt , instantly jumped on his legs , and , if he had been afraid , appeared to have quite recovered his courage , as he cut all manner of capers , and laughed immoderately ; but Big Adam was greatly exhausted and could not move , as much from the immense pressure of the lion 's enormous body , as from the blood that he had lost by the wounds which he had received .
Parsing [sent. 3940 len. 41]: On examination , the bite in his leg was found to be much the most serious , as the bone was injured ; the wounds on his arm were all flesh-wounds , and although very painful , were not dangerous .
Parsing [sent. 3941 len. 18]: He was at present unable to speak , and was carried by his comrades to the camp .
Parsing [sent. 3942 len. 17]: Our travelers followed the Hottentots , as they all had enough of hunting for that day .
Parsing [sent. 3943 len. 33]: As soon as they arrived , Big Adam 's wounds were dressed by Swinton , and the poor fellow was accommodated with a bed , made up for him in the baggage-wagon .
Parsing [sent. 3944 len. 23]: They remained two days more on the banks of the Modder River , and then they forded it and continued their journey .
Parsing [sent. 3945 len. 42]: On the second day they perceived some small human figures on the summit of a hill at some distance , which the Hottentots declared to be Bushmen , of which people there were numerous hordes in this part of the country .
Parsing [sent. 3946 len. 34]: An attempt was made to open a communication with them , but in vain , as when any of the party advanced on horseback toward them , the Bushmen made a precipitate retreat .
Parsing [sent. 3947 len. 31]: As they were now in the neighborhood of these plunderers , every care was taken of the cattle , which were tied up before dark to prevent their being stolen .
Parsing [sent. 3948 len. 31]: On the following day they very unexpectedly fell in with a party of nine of the Bushmen , who were very busy devouring a quagga , which they had killed .
Parsing [sent. 3949 len. 28]: They replied to questions put to them with much fear and trembling , and , having been presented with some tobacco , they made a precipitate retreat .
Parsing [sent. 3950 len. 19]: On that night the fires of the Bushmen were to be seen on several of the surrounding hills .
Parsing [sent. 3951 len. 61]: They continued their course on the following day , when they fell in with about twenty women of the race we have just mentioned , who approached the caravan without fear , requesting tobacco and food ; the former was given to them in small quantities , and a shot from the Major 's rifle soon procured them the latter .
